LITERARY NOTES.
Australia really begins to be literary.
Buzz-z-z! . . . the air is full of the
clamor of little bees hurrying whither the
new hive sits bright in morning sunshine.
Here comes one who has gathered honey
from orange-blossom and hibiscus in far
Eastern islands ; yonder booms another with
whose store lingers the heavy fragrance
hidden in tropical orchid and magnolia ; that
has pilfered sweets from the roses of a
quaint, old-fashioned Southern garden ; this
brings a breath of wattle from Wairee hill,
where great, grey plains roll to the craving
West. It is inexpressibly pleasant to stand
and listen to them all, and to welcome each
dusty new-comer struggling homewards with
his precious load tucked tight- Alas! the
metaphor falls : indeed, it was never a
Being erect upon two legs like Mr. Pickwick
- for queen-bees do not voyage forth for
h ,iy, and so much of our literary honey is
being won by busy queans far up the slopes
of Helicon.
« * * «
Louise Mack is the last of the little tribe
to make a little curtsey with a little book
her first. The Bulletin, indeed, has known
her before ; but, though The Bulletin is all
very well in its way, a book is different. The
writer for newspapers, even for magazines, is
still like the unpractised bather who paddles
half-timorously at “ the shallow end ” : the
writer of a book, even a little one, is like the
expert swimmer who poises body, holds
breath, and dives boldly into “the deep.”
Louise Mack has not gone very deep this
time—half a yard, perhaps ; but then it is
probable that the best diver—who may be
taken, for the sake of argument, as Sergeant
Parkinson, of the Sydney Amateur Swim-
ming Association—cannot say that he got to
the bottom of the baths the first time he
tried. And Louise Mack is much younger
than Sergeant Parkinson ; and not so tall, so
that she has further to go.
* * * *
The title of Louise Mack’s book (published
by T. Fisher Unwin) is “ The World is
Round; ” but it might as well have been
“The Universe is Flat ” for all the connec-
tion it has with the story. The book, in
fact, would be just as clever with any other
title. That it is clever is undeniable : it
shows some insight, and some force, and a
fine capacity for plain description and colored
satirical description, and a gimletty faculty
of observation which would pierce you through
and through though you lived in a Lyrtu—
which is exactly 27,597,901,440,846,512
miles away from Sydney, and not merely
27,597,901,440,846,504, as certain ignorant
astronomers would have you believe. If one
were Caliph for a space, it would be joyous
to bring all the Bores of Bagdad each by
each into the Presence, with an intimation
that their wise fame had reached the ears of
the Commander of the Faithful, and they
were required to state their well-considered
views about woman’s-suffrage, and to recite
their latest poems on Federation, and to
make a few remarks concerning that burning
question of the referendum. And on the
steps of the throne would be sitting a small,
quaint, frail figure with a soft, round child-
face and large blue baby-eyes, poring over the
6tory of Es-Sindibad of the Sea, and in in-
tervals of eloquence the Bore would come to
pat her head and enquire how she was
getting on in Sums. And at length the chief
chamberlain would give a signal, and the
little figure on the steps of the throne would
rise up and proceed, in the purest Persian,
to ... ! And the Bore, ah, where
would he be ? Ask of the winds that far
around with fragments strewed the rose-gar-
dens ! —as Mrs. Hemans beautifully puts it.
If one were but Caliph for a space !
* * * «
All the characters in the book are more or
less drawn from life—carved from the real
ham ; and the author generally cuts pretty
close to the knuckle. There is hardly a dull
line, or a weak line, or a line false in fact or
sentiment, anywhere to be found. All is
most real, and graphic, and convincing, and
interesting.
* * * *
But let Louise Mack speak for herself of
than pseudo-intellectual Sydney set, with its
Literary Societies, and its wearying-, unending
phases after the Theories of the Moment, its
ignorance, its Aides-de-camp, its Society Women,
its Lights of the Medical Profession, and above
all, the affability with which one member agrees
with another’s opinions.
For she thanks God “there are no literary
societies in the bush, no aides-de-camp to
rend us archaic papers on Horace ” (O, Mr.
G niton '.). And here is a piece of an evening
with some “rather clever” Sydney people:—
- Jean eain<- and carried the suckling away, and
Ids interlocutor sat alone, waiting for a vic-
ti>ii. Fate, in the shape of Jean . . . made
M"-grave known to her with ceremonious for-
mal ;ty.
“ Y r ou can talk books together,” wickedly.
“Mr. Musgrave loves reading,” to the girl;
“ Miss Pomedda is the secretary of the Woman’s
Literary Society,” to Musgrave. “Are you
fond of reading?” asked the secretary. Mus-
grave had rapidly decided how to talk to her.
To encourage her would be sinful.
“No,” he said. “I’m a Philistine.”
“Fancy ! Are you really? Don’t you read at
all?”
“ The newspapers and the serial stories in the
magazines and reviews of things. I find that
q lite enough, and the best way to improve your
n ind if you mix with literary people.”
“I always read the reviews of books,” she said
artably. “Then you know if a book’s good or
I ad.”
“Du you?” said Musgrave innocently. “I
didn’t know that before. Are you fond of read-
ing?”
“My two favorite pastimes are reading and
thin ing,” she said, with well-bred pensiveness.
“Good Lord,” said Musgrave under his
breath. . . .
They were clearing tables and ciiairs away from
one end of the room, where a lady Stood, assuming
a slightly theatrical attitude.
“•'he is going to recite,” said Musgrave’s
companion, watching interestedly.
The reciter stood for a minute with her hands
clasped loosely before, her, her head a little bent,
as if in acknowledgment o. tumultuous applause
from the sil. nt and embarrass, d au<;i, tic,.
Then she raised her head slowly, showed Ler
teeth, lifted one hand, and began.
“ The Patriot,” she announced.
A moment’s pause. Then
“It was roses, roses, all the way,” she called in
a loud voice, with a strong affected masculine
flavor about it.
And then an awkward thing happened to her.
She forgot what came next.
She stood there, pressing her under-lip hard
with her forefinger, and gazing agouisedly at Jean,
who was nearest to her, and vainly trying to beat
up the forgotten lines from her treacherous brain.
There was a dead pause. The people in the room,
who had just carefully turned their eyes away
from the painful spectacle of a drawing-room
reciter’s face, had now to look back again to find
the reason of this silence.
“This is too rich for anything,” said Musgrave
in a jubilant voice to his companion. She did not
hear him. Her lips were making violent gestures
to somebody across the room. Though no sounds
issued from them.
Nobody had the courage to speak the second
line of the broken poem. Some of them must have
known it, but nobody dared suggest the forgotten
cue. Everybody felt as if the voice that suggested
it would echo round the room for hours after.
At last, after what seemed an interminable
time of horror, the reciter’s hands fell clasped
again before her, and in a slightly different tone
from that of her last utterauce she began
straightway to declaim Calverley’s “Shelter.”
Sue had gone from the ken of ungentle men,
Yet scarce did I mourn for that;
For I knew she was safe in her own home then,
And the danger past, would appear again.
“For ’’ —impressively pausing a minute to allow
their smiles to accumulate She was a water-
rat.”
But everybody had been wrought to such a pitch
over “The Patriot” that they had not the spirit
even to smile now. They knew they ought to,
but they could not.
She sank into a chair, and smiled nervously at
the carpet. The murmured “Thank-you’s” fell
flatly on the air.
Musgrave turned.
“Did you ever in your life see anything so
ghastly? .... A woman who recites in
her friends’ drawing-rooms should certainly be
put to death.”
She turned her head, and looked at him with
eyes so horrified, astounded, and full of naive
wonder that such a man should dare to exist, that
he felt the color rising to his cheek.
“That lady is my sister,” she gasped. Then
she rose and crossed the room, and left him alone
in stricken silence.
O dove-cots of Corioli ! Let the Austral
Salon shiver to think what might have hap-
pened if Louise Mack’s lot had fallen in
Melbourne !
* * t 9
There is no plot to speak of in “ The
World is Round ” —two men and a woman ;
woman takes one ; poison takes t’other.
And the chapters strung on this thread are
disconnected enough to stand alone; the
climax gains nothing from them. One
sketch-chapter gives the impressions of a
bush-governess ;
As for the Bushies, they are adorable, the long,
shy, silent creatures, with their green and purple
fly-veils, their nervous, uneven walk, their kind,
pure, childish eyes, their atrocious “yairs,”
“sniike,” “ niice diay,” their tenderness to
women and dumb aii'imils, their primeval
patriotism, their sturdy democracy. And the
young Bushies, the boys scarcely out of their teens
with their beards, and their home-made suits,
puce neckties, husky voices, hair-oil, pumps (as
they call their evening shoes), their hidden hoard
of unconscious sentiment, their reverence for
Rolf Boldrewood. Their bitter contempt for that
profound and scurrilous egoist, Max O’Rell-the
same Max who fattened on their land with a ready
continental smile, and went away and gave the
world a string of frothy sarcasms about their tea-
drinking and meat-eating and pitiable drunken-
ness and antecedents, their deep and undying love
for Gordon’s “Sick Stockrider,” and “How we
beat the Favorite.” I regard them all tenderly,
and never grow tired of watching them.
It may be that their conversation is at first a
little dijfficile. Here is a specimen of one that took
place the night after I arrived.
Bushie ; Do you like the country ?
Me : Well, I have hardly been here long enough
to know.
Bushie : Aeour [I don’t know if I have spelt it
quite correctly.]
Me : I am sure I shall like it, though ; every-
thing is so different from Sydney, and I was
deadly tired of Sydney.
Bushie : Aeour.
Me (desperately): I suppose you have been to
Sydney ?
Bushie; Yairs. [Pause.]
Bushie : Do you liike- horses?
Me : Oil, I love them.
Bushie : Can you ride?
Me : Well, I can canter all the way.
Bushie : Ca-antering is very ni-ice.
Me : Trotting is so~er —heavy.
Bushie: Yairs.
Me : I think most people prefer cantering, don’t
you ?
Bushie : Yairs.
Me : Which do you like best ?
Bushie ; Trotting.
Me (surprised): Do you ? oh, well, I would
even trot if I could be on horseback as often as I
liked. You see, I very seldom have a chance of
riding in Sydney. [He shuffles his eyes from one
foot to the other]. I live at Paddington. [He
lets them raise themselves as high as his hands].
And it would be terribly expensive to keep a
horse there. You don’t see many good horses in
Sydney [brings thein-wp to my face, at once drops
them, and looks away under the far chair], except
rac-rs, out walking with their jockeys.
Bushie: Aeour.
He repeats the eye-drill again and again, but
listens attentively to every word I say. . . .
The odor of undiluted gum-trees was strong in
the Bushie’s conversation ; no forced essences
tampered with its native perfume, so it was en-
joyable and fresh, and bush-like and slow.
Tin- Bush girls are shy, clever, wonderfully nen
and domesticated, and coiisnm-d with ambitions
which they never speak of. One of flic things
that struck mu most about them is their intense
fervent desire to improve their minds. 1 hey w
practise painstakingly day alter day tor years ->>
years, with no'further music lessons than the
elementary ones from a hard-worked mother.
Patiently they wait their chance, winch comes
sometimes in the shape of a musical governess on
an adjoining station, who is perhaps induct >1 to
give them a lesson once a month. llow t ie.y
rev el in that! And their reading is just as pains-
tako r and conscientious. History is the specia.
study to which they devote themselves and
between ourselves, Jean dear, if you and 1 were
put through the mill of knowledge of this parti-
cular subject with them, we would come out soine_
where at the bitter end. Or I, at any rate. It
you want to know anything, never ask a graduate.
Francis Adams notes in bush girls the
same intense yearning for culture. And
hut space shortens.
* * * *
Charles Whibley, a man with a spirited
English style and a hue knowledge of last-
century English literature, has a note in
April New Review on Wheatley’s new edi-
tion of Pepys’s Diary, which, he says, will
utterly extinguish (for intelligent people)
Braybrooke’s well-bowdierised edition. Then,
after the fashion of Macaulay and the dex-
terous Capuchins, the commentator hangs
his sermon on the peg :
A lust of being and moving, of exercising his
senses to their utmost, governed Pepys’s existence.
Unnumbered and innumerable are his crowded
hou'-s of glorious life. The man who “is with
child to see any strange thing,” is neither cynic
nor Philistine. Nothing came amiss to him. He
was as pleased with Sir George Ent’s discourse
upon “Respiration” as he was with the peer-
less beauty of Lady Castlemaine. Only he must
always he doing, or hearing, or seeing some new
thing. To-day he is singing with Knipp, and
listening with a hungry ear to the praise of his
famous song, Beauty, Retire ; to-morrow he is dis-
cussing with Dr. Whistler whether masts should
he kept dry or damp. Now he goes to Wills's to
meet “ Dryden the poet (I knew at Cambridge) ’;
now he is chaffering for cloves with some poor
seaman in a “ blind alehouse.” And all the while
he is drinking in life at its abundant source. His
zest is almost too violent, and you wonde how he
could have sustained, thiough many years of suf-
fering, this ferocious energy of enjoyment; how he
remained firm in this dogged determination to
miss no minute of lSpoing time. But to his indus-
try no transition seemed abrupt : he turned from
his mistress to his accounts without weariness or
regret, and no sooner had he found an end of his
figures than he was ready to play again with all
the spi- it of a released schoolboy. His philosophy
was the most arrogant that ever a man about
town imagined. “Read every book,” he said in
effect, “ see every play, empty every wine-cup,
kiss every woman.” And when he died, in all
piety he might have Ov-ned that he never missed
an opportunity. Alexander conquered the world
but Pepys, with a keener, more selfish under-:
standing of life, conquered a world for every sense.
He could not take a boat without singing to the
“ skuller he could not meet a Dutch bellman
without, taking his clapper in his hand, without
noting that “it is just like the clapper that our
boys frighten the bit ds away from the corn with
in summer-time in England.”
• * • •
Nor was it part of Pepys’s creed to put off till to-
morrow what might be enjoyed to-day. His was
the Epicureanism of Horace. “ Carpe Diem'' he
shouted in his joyous voice. “I do indulge my-
self a little the more in pleasure,” said he by way
of excuse to himself, “knowing that this is the
proper age of my life to do it; and out of my
observation that most men that do thrive in the
world, do forget to take pleasure during the time
that they a- e getting their estate, but reserve
that till they have got one, and then it is too late
for them 10 enjoy it with any pleasure.” So
Pepys let not an hour pass unchallenged, and by a
youth of pleasure prepared an old age of happi-
ness. He loved the amenities of life : art, music,
a new coat, the songs of birds, the river, the open
air were his perpetual delight. But before all
things he loved a pretty woman. At the outset
he was hut a modest wooer. He once—it was on
his return from Debt—sat side by side with “a
pretty sober Dutch lass,” and “ I could not fasten
any discourse upon her,” he declares with his
bland confession of failure. During the same
journey to Holland he found “ a pretty Dutch
woman in bed alone,’ and, “though he had a
month’s mind, he had not the boldness to go to
her.” But in a years space his boldness was
invincible.
“Musique and women,” said the jolly old
chap, “ I cannot but give way to whatever
my business is.” And the ladies seem to
have liked him quite as well as he liked „he
ladies. Doubtless the good God arranges it
so.
* * * •
There is more than a touch of the Pepysian
spirit in “ Machete,” who sends The Bulle-
tin this not quite perfect ballade of
Press no ices of the English edition of
“The Man Iron. Snowy River” are beginning
to come to hand, Mid are uniformly favorable
so far The Glasgow Herald says that the
Banjo’s “ ballads, especially the racing ones,
are full of such go that the mere reading
makes the blood tingle.’ lie critic, how-
ever, doesn’t quite grasp the rationale, of
brumby-hunting. He says
The leading ballad is descriptive of a sort of
steeplechase, which includes mountain-steeps of
which we are ignorant in this country. All the
crack horses and riders are gathered at the station.
Amongst the rest is a stripling cn a small and
weedy beast. This is the man from Snowy
River.” But the youngster and his horse are so
unlikely a couple that it is with the Jfa-
culty he is allowed to join m the race. Nobody
expects that he will be really m the running.
But the result proves otherwise. Ihe weedy
beast and his rider, who ought to have broken
their necks at a dozen places in the course, actu-
ally beat the whole field.
This is well-meant, anyway. And London
Times thinks that Paterson “ at his best
compares not unfavorably with the author of
‘Barrack-Room Ballads,’” which is a good
big thing to say. Most of the reviewers lay
proper stress on the horsey character of the
verses —which is as it should be, no doubt.
Elsewhere Pegasus is a nondescript : in Aus-
tralia he is reatty a horse, and you’ll gener-
ally find him in the saddling-paddock.
* * * *
And in this fashion speaks the Edinburgh
Scotsman, which, since Russell’s glorious
day, has generally deserved its reputation of
being the best literary authority in Scot-
land :
The verse of Mr. Paterson is not so serious a
thing as Lindsay Gordon’s. It has not the same
impetuous sweep, the same glory of mere motion.
But it has a good “ go ” of its own, and it covers
a much wider range than the concentrated view
of the older poet. It has the saving grace of
humor ; and the work has the peculiarly satis-
fying quality of bearing on its uwn face a
warrant of its fidelity to the life it describes. . .
And albeit Mr. Paterson is not above vivifying
the Scot by representing him to be fond of his
whist y and by writing a villainously bad dialect
of Scotch that is worse than the worst whisky
ever fabricated for export to the colonies, his book
will have a hearty welcome here from all who
value poetry that is racy of the soil that produced
it. The more ambitious poets of Australia have
erred in trying to write as sublimely as the best of
our home poets. But a book like this (though as
absolute poetry it ranks in the same class with Mr.
Kipling’s ballads) is worth a dozer of the aspiring,
idealistic sort, since it has a deal of real laughter
and a dash of real tears in its composition.
But perhaps the warmest praise of “The
Banjo ” is implied by the Scottish gentle-
man who, in a paper called Scottish Nights
Entertainments, translates “The Man from
Snowy River ” into North-of-Tweed dialect
—and puts his name to it.
• * * *
J. C. Neild—who is not the worst poet in
Australia—begins to feel the inconveniences
of fame. Justin McCarthy relates how, in
1868, after the British success in the
Abyssinian war, Disraeli declared that Napier
had “hoisted the standard of St. George on
the mountains of Rasselas.” “All England,”
says McCarthy, “ smiled at the mountains of
Rasselas. The idea that Johnson had in his
mind the very Abyssinia of geography and of
history when he described his Happy Yalley,
was in itself trying to gravity. . . Ever
since that time, when the expedition to
Abyssinia is mentioned in any company, a
smile steals over some faces, and more than
one voice is heard to murmur an allusion to
the mountains of Rasselas.” If it may be
said without offence to the memory of
Disraeli, Neild is somewhat in Dizzy’s pre-
dicament. He no sooner appears in a mixed
company nowadays than tragic arms are
uplifted, and many tragic voices recite his
immortal line—
Eftsoons the Nocent Waterspout will rise !
The Bulletin hereby declares the banns of
marriage between the Nocent Waterspout
and the Mountains of Rasselas ; and if any
person can show just cause or impediment
why these two should not be united in the
bonds of holy matrimony, it calls upon him
or her to step forward, or hereafter forever
hold his or her peace.
* * * *
And, by the way, though the verbal
accuracy of the quoted formula is not guar-
anteed, the English language is badly in
need of a demonstrative and a personal
pronoun of common gender to abolish the
awkward alternative phrases “his or hers,”
‘‘he or she,” “him or her.” (The New
oman ought to invert the order—it would
be so much more consistent with her con-
text—-though it doesn’t appear she has
thought of doing so thus far.) If some cul-
tured authority would float “se,” “sis,”
and “ sim or better combinations—on the
English-speaking market, there should be
enough common-sense among writers and
readers to make success certain.

In the Green Park-and saw and heard a very familiar

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating whatsizeyou generally take,
what are your peculiarities —a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these

SWAGMAN : "Could yer give us a pipe of tobacco, mate?”

T-O O K !
BEFORE YOU
T. E A F?
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
(xeorge-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “ There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to
Buy Everything

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“WHEN WOMEN’S WRONGS ARE
RIGHTED.”
When women’s wrongs are righted
Will men’s hearts still be blighted ?
When counsel smart, without a heart,
And judges fair, with faded hair,
And wigs, and wizened faces,
And spinsters bold, and bald and old,
Try breach-of-promise cases?
Will Love’s sweet troth be plighted
With coyness—still invited ?
When maidens prim, but no more trim,
And matrons cold, of years untold,
Two chins and double faces ;
With awesome air and vacant stare,
Try complicated cases ?
JVow women’s wrongs are righted,
Their troths are never plighted ;
They seldom flirt or wear a skirt,
They want no “ spoons ” but pantaloons,
And men are coy and just their toy—
And women’s wrongs are righted,
Men’s hearts no more are blighted.
ZETA.
“AN UNSET SONG.”
When thou shalt hear the west wind, cold and
drear,
Know, all my love doth whisper to thine ear !
Sere leaves will fall and murmur, yet
I cannot e’er forget—ah, no ! I cannot e’er forget.
Thy heart forgets ; my words of yore
All unremembered be;
Yet evermore, for evermore,
I weep in vain for thee, my life doth weep for
thee.
My life to meet with thine no more,
To meet with thine no more !
When thou shalt hear the sobbing of the sea,
Know, all the anguish in my memory
The moaning tides will tell thee ! Yet
I cannot e’er forget—ah, no ! I cannot e’er forget.
Oh, while my eyes have bitter tears
Till they no more may see,
For evermore long darkened years
Those tears shall fall for thee, my life shall weep
for thee.
My life to meet with thine no more,
To meet with thine no more !
A. W. STEWART.
THE AUCTION.
Here she stands, a peerless beauty, child of freak
and fashion,
Reared to meet the Marriage Market, fraught
with pulsing passion;
Never form more softly graceful rose on fairy
pinions,
Never goddess stepped more stately, high in
Jove’s dominions.
Hyacinthine locks that ripple, lips with love
a-tremble,
Dimpling chin and cheek of rosebud, blushes that
dissemble;
Smiles that burst like light upon you, sweetly and
serenely,
Ivory teeth that gleam and glisten, head erect
and queenly.
Dress the maid in costume fitting; deck her out
with laces;
Let the dainty darling trip it with her dainty
paces ;
Let her cast her subtle glamour like a goddess
o’er us;
Let her sweep her swanlike glories in a train
before us.
“Heart”—you say—“a heart she’s wanting.”
Well, we’ll see about one—
Never mind ! The darling’s better —better far
without one ;
Lead her to the mart, ye minions—Marriage mars
or makes her:
Sale commences — Going, going—gone 1 Sir Midas
takes her l
N.Q.
P. LUFTIG.
QUIBBLES—IN QUATRAINS.
The world is wide ! Its vastness is a curse
To those who’d travel on an empty purse ;
But, flying from a dun, or angry wife,
You’ll find its narrowness will plague you worse !
The world is narrow ! So the tourist says,
Who boasts of “ round the earth in sixty days.”
How wide it is in sympathy and love
They cannot know who tread these hackneyed
ways.
The world is narrow ; but it never mars
His flight, whose soul can soar beyond the stars.
The world is wide ; but, in its free expanse,
The soulless boor feels bound by prison bars !
J. LIDDELL KELLY.
DISARMED.
A virgin prayed : “ God keep me good,
Watch and protect my mother’s child.”
Invisible the devil stood
Beside the kneeling form, and smiled
To see the warm flush tinge her face
And neck ; a sweet, unholy sight,
That danger-signal of disgrace
Glowing against her bed-robe white !
She wrung her hands with fervor wild —
“ God keep megood.” The tempter smiled.
The devil saw her eyes upraised
Shine saintly in the cold moonray,
And, as he cynically gazed,
He heard the virgin softly pray—
“ God bless dear Jack; I love him so,
My love is more to me than life;
Take care of him.” Then virtue’s foe
Winced, as one struck by sudden knife.
The suppliant humble bowed her head—
* < God guard dear Jack. ” The tempter fled.
Melb.
E.F.

SATURDAY, JUNE 27, 1896.
Gone Under.
A- few days ago Treasurer Ward, of Maori-
land, joined the lamentable procession of
lamentable politicians who have “ gone un-
der ” —for a time at all events, if not for ever.
This pecuniary kind of sinkage is a peculiarity
of Australasian politics, while for various
reasons it is practically unknown in Great
Britain. The British Cabinet Minister is,
in almost every case, a man of wealth, who
isn’t dependent for his livelihood on a busi-
ness that goes to wreck while he is attending
to public affairs. Men of wealth are vastly
more numerous in Britain than they are
here, and as Britain has oidy to find one
Ministry out of forty million people, while
Australasia supplies seven Ministries out of
four millions, there are enough of them to
go round, which is not the case in these pro-
vinces. It is even possible, in the little
country “ down under,” to form a reasonably
democratic Ministry of rich men ; where the
wealthy class is so numerous some of them
must needs become more or less democratic,
if only out of the habit of being in opposi-
tion to the other lot. The British Minister,
too, receives something like a “ living wage,”
which isn’t the case in several of the Aus-
tralias ; and he doesn’t require to file his
schedule when he goes out of office, for his
suffering country gives him a very handsome
pension if he chooses to apply for it. Under
all these diverse conditions, the fact that
British statesmen don’t make a melancholy
procession into the insolvency court, and
that Australasian statesmen do, is sufficiently
understandable.
♦ * * *
The downfall of Treasurer Ward is a trifle
more pathetic than the usual collapse. Ward
has been for nearly six years an honored
member of about the ablest and most gene-
rally-respected Ministry which has ever held
office in Maoriland. He was a capable Trea-
surer ; that is to say, he always had a surplus
in visible cash —not merely on paper ; and
his surplus wasn’t made up by wrongfully
annexing the sinking-funds, and surrep-
titiously running them into revenue, and
denying afterwards that he did it, except
when it seemed necessary to do these things
in order to produce a surplus at all. There-
fore lie may be regarded as a reasonably
honest Treasurer, and agood deal more capable
than the average. The spectacle of the man
who ran the financial affairs of the State with
tolerable success for so long a time barely
managing to pay 9d. in the £ on his own lia-
bilities, under very shady circumstances,
is quite in keeping with the ways of
political life. Whether Ward’s exploded
company was a swindle of a very bad kind,
or a very bad case of neglect and imbecility
and hopeless incompetence, or a mixture of
the two, and whether it was Ward’s swindle,
or how much of it was Ward’s own imbecility,
are things that remain to be shown. Either
way, it is another essay on the lamentable folly
of not “ laying to your book.” The ex-Trea-
surer was a comparatively wealthy business
man at Invercargill when he took to politics.
Politics took him to Wellington, three days’
journey from his office and his business, and
thereafter his affairs seem to have run them-
selves. Then he floated them into a com-
pany of which he was managing director,
and the company also seems to have run
itself. It lasted just three years ; it ruined
Ward and nearly everybody else connected
with it ; it partially ruined the Colonial
Bank of M.L. ; and now the ex-Treasurer is
ejected from his political billet and his salary,
practically penniless, snowed up beneath a
mountain of false balance-sheets and other
troubles ; and accused more or less openly
—though so far on no apparent evidence —of
having misused his political position in the
effort to keep himself afloat. Also, a judge
has denounced him from the bench of the
Supreme Court in no very measured terms.
So far as the public saw it, Ward’s downfall
filled something less than a fortnight ; that
was all the time he occupied in sliding from
nearly the highest position in the State to
practical nothingness.
* * * *
The matter which made the ex-Treasurer’s
ruin absolutely certain and definite was his
famous visit to Hingland. Almost every
Australian politician who makes a triumphal
progress to the land of Bull goes under very
shortly afterwards. There is a fatality about
J. Bull’s country for “colonial” politicians.
Parkes went there in all his hair and his
glory, and came back to go insolvent, and be
thrown out of office and bad-egged by an in-
furiated populace. Dibbs rioted through
England and was made a K.C.M.G., and
poked his walking-stick into the bed of a
deceased monarch to see what it was stuffed
with, and his doings filled the cable intelli-
gence for many weeks, and the big cigar
which Wales gave him became histori-
cal ; and then he came back with his
Windsor uniform to be thrown out of
office and out of Parliament. Griffith
went to England in ’B7, and next year was
chased down a bye-street by a howling mob ;
his reputation fled, his party burst up, and
he only escaped utter shipwreck by pocket-
ing his pride in a “ reconciliation ” with his
bitterest opponent. M ‘llwraith went to
England, and, poor man, has ever since
been trying unsuccessfully to be a
heaven-born financier on one kidney and
a little bit of liver. Berry went
to England, and that ended his career as
Berry, the democrat. James Munro went
there, and was called back to file his schedule
and be assaulted in the street by infuriated
depositors. James Brown Patterson, being
unable to go to England and grovel to the
Royal Family, struck the great idea of invit-
ing royalty to come to Australia and be
grovelled to here ; and the curse worked
that way just as well as the other,
for he died almost immediately after-
wards. And the worst things that hap-
pened to Ward’s dreadfully - misman-
aged company occurred while he was
away in London being presented to the
Queen, and addressing admiring public meet-
ings, and otherwise making the same kind
of glare which Dibbs made a few years
previously. When he came back, there
were flags and processions and a half-
holiday in his honor, and the Ward
Farmers’ Association declared a big divi-
dend, and Ward (so he alleges) signed the
fraudulent balance-sheet without knowing
what was in it, or being aware that his co.
had been throwing money away in all direc-
tions with a shovel in his absence. Nobody
seems to care much about his explanations,
however, and nobody appears to grieve much
about his downfall. If he had been removed
by death, like Ballance, the public might
have put a few wreaths on his grave and
spoken kindly of him, but there is none of
the sort of pathos that the public is addicted
to about the man who removed by 9d. in
the £, and people don’t put wreaths on the
Insolvency Court, except in very rare in-
stances.
* * * *
However the affairs of Ward’s shady com-
pany may pan out on further investigation,
the present chances are that Ward has gone
under for good. A great many men get one
chance in life ; very few get two such chances
as he had and as he threw away. Australia is
full just now of men with somewhat similar
histories; the w'recks who were left over
from the last boom, and who expect to rise
again on the next one, oblivious of the fact
that the new boom wants new men,
and that the old ones will be of no
more account in it than if they
never existed. In the ordinaly course of
things the future of the ex-Treasurer will
consist of a seedy little wooden office, and
inside a tired-looking man, who is his own
entire staff and office-boy, picking up little
crumbs in the precarious commission-busi-
ness, and a cheap-looking 4( ® ? £ W) Finan-
door, with the inscription, , As _
cial Agent.” Meanwhile the Farmers a
s „ci»tiS„ ““““t at next
Government will have a harder g j
election than was generally ex P e^ d ’ ’
though it is still almost impossible tha t
can be defeated considering its.
and the wonderful meptitude o 1
tion, its prospects look a shade w
they did. It has met with many
the last three or four years. Ballan<
first, and took the best man out of pranks.
Buckley went also, though lie wa g
weakest man in the team and his a -
hardly noticeable. The brilliant Reevesihas
gone to England, and is beginning to figure
there as W. Pember Reeves, and looks in
serious danger of wandering into th e devious
wavs of K C.M.G.-dom. Ward, who was
rerouted to be one of the_ distinctly strong
men of the Cabinet, has just dropped
And in place of these veterans of the demo-
cratic Ministry which Ballance formed in
1891 there are beginning to drift into the
Cabinet smaller and comparatively obscure
individuals, with names like Thompson am
Jones, who don’t appear to fit in well with
the original plan. If a Ministry survives all
other chances it generally dies m the end o
too much repair, and judging from present
appearances the Seddon Government will
die from that cause in the end.
Laying the Ghost.
It has evidently been decided by N.S. VY ales
officialdom to quietly release the remainder of
the Mount Rennie prisoners during Judge
Windeyer’s absence in England. By this
arrangement Mr. WlNDEYEßisgiven opportunity
of avoiding all appearance of assenting to the
act and thereby seeming to acknowledge the
hideous injustice of the original trial as he con-
ducted it Also, he is enabled to avoid
the other impression that he dissents from it,
and that it is done despite his opposition and by
way of a slur on his judgment. As far as pos-
sible Mr. Windeyer gets rid of the Mount
Rennie ghost without appearing either to re-
pent or to be uurepentant, or even to be aware
that there ever was any ghost. At the same
time the Govt, lets him down easy per medium
of an article in the sneaking and obsequious
S.M. Herald, which tries to set forth (1) that
the trial and the conviction and the death-
sentence were just (the Herald said at the
time in a leading article that the trial was most
wwjust, but that doesn’t matter); and (2) that
though the Mount Rennie prisoners deserved
hanging it is quite the right tiling to let them
out after nine years’ imprisonment—less than
many a man gets for embezzlement—less than
Judge WINDEYER himself has given civil ser-
vants for stealing public moneys. This is how
it sets about the matter :
Before the occurrence of the Mount Rennie outrage the
colony had been shocked by several crimes of a somewhat
similar nature, some of which had from different causes
escaped adequate punishment. Looking back upon the
matter from the standpoint of the intervening years, we
may admit that the prompt arrest and clear conviction of
the culprits in the Mount Rennie affair were received by a
large part of the public, and by those charged with the
administration of the law, as furnishing an opportunity
for stamping out by the exercise of signal severity a par-
ticularly atrocious form of crime by which the colony had
been alarmed and disgraced. The full penalty was
exacted in the case of the four chief offenders; the two
others received sentences only short of death. Whether
as a consequence or not of this exemplary sternness we
need not now enquire ; but as a fact, since the execution
of the Mount Rennie culprits, the criminal records of the
colony have been free from cases of this particular kind
of crime in concert, by which they had before been too
frequently disfigured. The lapse of years has relegated
the Mount Rennie crime to what in so young a community
we may regard. as a comparatively distant past. The
interval has served to almost efface even the memory of the
feelings of shame'and alarm which the outrage, following
as it did upon similar occurrences, produced in the
mind of the public. We would fain hope that the moral
' character of the community has so improved as to render
crimes of this particular character less to be apprehended
in future. If this should prove to be the case, only a
minority of judgments would question that a considerable
factor in the improvement was supplied by the way in which
the dread powers of the law were exercised in the punish-
ment of this crime. The question now is not as to the
propriety of these punishments. It is one of a different
nature, and is addressed to entirely different faculties so
to speak, of the mind and conscience of the community.
It is whether the State could not without danger now
show mercy to the culprits still serving their sentence in
prison.
* * * *
The above pleas,which are all so constructed
as to let the Judge down as softly as possible,
are mostly false, and those which are not dis-
tinctly false are more or less garbled. N.S
Wales hadn’t been “shocked” by several
“ crimes of a somewhat similar nature ” during
the year or two, or three, or five, before the
Mount Rennie affair. The Herald, of course
doesn’t bind itself down to any definite period •
it simply says that there had been certain crimes
before the Mount Rennie episode, and in
the previous 100 years, or in the previous ten cen-
turies, there certainly had been, but if it means
to imply that there was any rush of sexual
ottences just before that episode it tells a false-
hood, and if it doesn't mean that then it deliber-
ately creates a false impression. The province
wasn t “ alarmed and disgraced” just then bv
any especial outbreak, and there was nothing
mote than usual to “stamp out.” Moreover
nothing in particular was stamped out. Since
the execution of the Mount Rennie culprits ”
the records of the province haven’t been
free ftom sexual crime any more than thcv
were before. The year which followed that
statistics (1893 and ’94) show a fficrt* 8
as compared with 1890 thp « US lnc * edse
don't go any further tack.’ “ftt
Herald s plea, that the ghost of \tp .. r> le
Should be lifted off its mind andßennie
the callous officials and politicians °f
tenanced the ineffably.barbarous “Sal ”T'
cause that lesson has done its work and i b
longer required, falls through and 8 T
possible reason for the commutation of tb ° uly
tences is that they were inflictpd ? f the Sen '
blood-drunkenness, on the evidenced
walker, a forger, and an arsonist, upon the
verdict of a jury that was going mad from ex-
haustion and want of sleep, and by a Judge
who was wholly unfitted judicial office.
Also it may be mentioned as a kind of side-
issue that, when the principal witness for the
gallows (Stanley) proved to be a man of
bad character and a passer of forgeries,
the police didn’t execute the warrant
against him (though he was visible daily
in the streets) until a pressman one day
seized a somnolent constable, and showed him
the wanted man, and forced him to take him
into custody. The felony was thereupon un-
lawfully compounded by certain persons,
who paid, or allowed to be paid, the
amount of the forged cheque out of the sub-
scriptions to the Stanley testimonial. Y\ hen
the case was called upon, the (squaied) piose-
cutor didn’t appear. The police didn’t do their
duty and make him appear, though he was a
well - known publican and could have been
forced to appear under penalty of losing his
license The Justice Department didn’t
prosecute. No well-known citizens were
arrested for compounding a serious crime, and
the blood-drunk daily press kindly dropped the
whole scandal. If it hadn’t been dropped,
and if as little as possible hadn’t been
said about the after conviction of the
third Crown witness for arson, even “right-
thinking” people might have begun to seri-
ously doubt the justice of that infamous
trial _so there was a general smother-
ing all round. Yet, curiously enough, the
Herald, in its list of reasons why the Mount
Rennie prisoners should be released (in a
way that won’t hurt the feelings of Mr. Win-
deyer), doesn’t mention one of these inter-
esting circumstances. And, by way of
another side issue, when the Herald speaks
of the diminution of crime, how is the crime
known as compounding a felony the one
which the Herald absolutely refused to
say anything about when it was done to
bolster up the credit of an utterly unreliable
Crown witness getting on ? Is it being
“ stamped out ”at all ? And is there any per-
ceptible diminution in that other crime, which
has no definite name, but which consists in the
police and the Crown Law Department, and the
high officials and the respectable daily press, all
becoming suddenly oblivious while a well-known
criminal escapes —a criminal whose conviction
would be very awkward for the persons respon-
sible for choking four youths, largely on his
evidence, and the papers who whooped for the
said execution, and the Lieut.-Governor and
ex-Chief Justice who deified him at public
meetings, and the notable people who subscribed
to his testimonial ?
Potsherds.
Canon Potter has just preached a political
sermon in St. Paul’s Cathedral, Melbourne, in
which he blasted the divorce laws, secular edu-
cation, and the Protective system. Here is Mr.
Potter’s first principle of government: —
Every Government is bound (1) to recognise God as the
Supreme Ruler, and to govern according to His laws.
It is just such an alleged principle that animated
all the heresy-hunts of the past. Every per-
secuting Pope, every defender of the faith,
every religious fanatie running amok, has
claimed some peculiar divinity as bis accomplice
before the fact. The God in whose name rapine
and murder and torture have been effected in
the past may only have been the delusion of
madness, but pestilential religions have still to
be feared ; and when a man claims that he
knows the special secrets of the true God, he
may still be a charlatan, may still be a victim
of mania, may still be an unscrupulous bigot,
like those who have wrecked States for all
time. Governing “ according to the laws of
God ” may still mean governing according to
distorted readings of a misunderstood Bible,
which supplies, and has ever supplied, the
seeker according to his tastes. Out of the
Bible, the fanatic can justify war, peace,
persecution, mercy, murder, honesty, theft,
asceticism, good - living, socialism, jingoism,
land nationalisation, communism, usury, poly-
gamy, suicide, slavery, bi-metallism, democracy,
monarchy, and anarchy. Which of these are
the “laws of God”? Sick and tired of the
wrangles of priests, the interpretations of
special-pleaders, and the lies of unctuous hypo-
crites, the democracy of these.days has settled
for itself that religion must confine itself
to a mental domain, without intrusion into
“practical politics.” The Government, as
the servant of the State, is required to
keep sectarian squabbles in their own
sphere, and to cease the domination which
has made religious rule in all ages the synonym
for intolerance and oppression. When the
Democracy makes mistakes it will suffer from
those mistakes—this is found to be a far safer
course than suffering from the mistakes of
opinionated hierarchies.
* * * *
This leads up to Mr. Potter’s second prin-
ciple :
Every government is bound (2) to weigh fairly and
justly the interests of all the governed alike.
If the government is Democratic, it will be
representative—but it will not be infallible.
Infallibility must be left to Potter and the
Popes. A Democratic government will rule in
accordance with what it thinks are the wishes
of the Democracy. The Democracy will not
require to be done those things that are infalli-
bly right—but what things appear to be right
and in accordance with the greatest good of
the greatest number. Mr. Potter has a
grievance with the Victorian Democracy, and,
unfortunately for himself, gives details :
A government violates the former principle if it keep
laws on the statute-book which tend to turn marriage into
a mere temporary alliance, not for higher but for lower
Purposes, and so to make men and women like the cattle
of the field, and not like the sons and daughters of the
Most High.
lhis is a priestly curse of the Shikls Divorce
Act, which makes the marriage contract nulli-
uable for such reasons as that one of the parties
has become a habitual criminal. Lofty talk
about criminal husbands being “ sons of the
Most High ” will never convince the democracy
that a defiled and maimed marriage contract is
a thing of duty and a join for ever.


Mr. Potter goes on :
A Government violates the former principle if it first
makes education a State monopoly, and then proscribes or
disregards the highest part of education —the education
of the moral sense and conscience.
This means, firstly, if it means anything, that
the only educators permitted in Victoria are
State school teachers. If so, it is a priestly lie.
It means, secondly, that the education of the
moral sense and conscience are prohibited in
Victoria. This looks like another holy false-
hood. Victoria has a system of compulsory
education. Free schools were established be-
cause the compulsory clauses of the Education
Act would otherwise have been a hardship.
The free schools were made secular because de-
nominational schools were proved by years of
experience to be a failure, and because the
democracy refused to adopt any State religion.
Nevertheless, the clergy have large opportuni-
ties for visiting and teaching in the schools at
the present moment —and they unanimously
stay away.
More Potter :
A government violates the latter principle if it take
toll of its people not for the legitimate expenses of govern-
ing, but in order to bolster up some trades at the cost of
the others, to create artificial monopolies, and to hinder
the free flow of wealth from shore to shore.
This attack on Protection by a Free Raider
implies that the Government is the initiator, and
that it forces the Protective System on the
“ governed.” This is the democratic ideal (and
the facts) upside-down. The people of Victoria
adopted Protection by a vast and overwhelming
majority—and whether it is a just or unjust
principle, as long as it is the province’s adopted
principle, the Government must administer it
or go. To assert that a Government must not
legislate in the interests of less than all classes
is humbug. In a democracy the Government
must introduce bills, etc., which it believes are
in accordance with what the democracy believes
to be necessary. The democracy does not, as a
rule, yearn for the interests of parsons, land-
monopolists, and sweaters —such “classes,” as
such, have to take their chances.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Geake Scandal.
I On the 29th April last Victoria was startled by
the conviction and sentencing to two years’ im-
prisonment (!) of a very fraudulent solicitor
named Geake. As far as anyone could remem-
ber, there was no modern precedent for a
solicitor’s conviction in that land of privileged
rascality. Numerous members of the profession
had been reprimanded more or less for offences
which were nearly bad enough to get another
sort of person hanged without the option of a
fine, but as for putting a lawyer into gaol—the
public had learned by experience to doubt
whether it could possibly be done at any price.
Geake’s conviction at Bendigo Assizes was such
an unaccountable accident that people began to
look around at once for a reason why the sen-
tence couldn’t be carried out —a nolle prosequi
after the event, or a new patent quash, or what
not. Consequently, there was a universal ejacu-
lation of “ I thought so ! ” when the discovery
of Geake’s apparent discomfiture was followed
by the news that Geake had appealed. Lhe
learned Judge, previous topassingsentence, made
a note of some precious point or other for the
consideration of the Full Court. Naturally the
outside world jumped to the idea Geake’s
conviction would get upset on appeal, inasmuch
as the imprisonment of one fraudulent trustee
from among so many couldn’t be decent in
Equity. However, the conviction of Geake
was upheld by the Full Court. Barrister
O’Loghlen advanced some sweet arguments
and technical objections to the effect that
Geake ought not to have been tried on the
charge which laid him low, but the baronet s
eloquence failed. Then was there nothing left
for the bowled-out lawyer (on paper, as a horse
reporter would write) save durance vile and
hominy. Just at this juncture sceptical
watchers of the case recollected that Geake,
pending the appeal, had been released on
ridiculously light bail. His own recognisances
in .£2OO, and those of a friendly law-clerk for
a like amount, were held to be sufficient
security for Geake’s appearance when called
upon ! The criminal was called for at Melb.
Criminal Court last week, but he did not appear.
He was non est. Nobody, strange to say, had
taken measures to prevent Geake’s new address
from becoming a dark mystery. So the Court,
without comment, ordered estreatment of the
recognisances, and the public said ‘ I thought
so ! ” with even more emphasis than before.
A Terribly Sad Case.
The other day a Sydney bank-clerk pleaded
guilty to having stolen <£ll2o from the till.
Like Corkett, the woolly-headed young rogue
in “ Silver King,” this reckless individual bor-
rowed money from his governors without men-
tioning of it, to put it on ’orses. He spoke
quite candidly on the point when asked whether
he had anything to say why sentence should
not be passed. “ I lost money on the races
confessed the dock-penitent, “and thought 1
could recover it at racing, and failed. All the
money went that way and with that object.
His salary, it seemed, was <£l3o a year, a te . r
seven years’ service in the bank. He laid muc i
stress on the smallness of his salary, which, m
his opinion, fully excused the comparative
magnitude of his embezzlement. And the
learned Judge was seemingly somewhat in
sympathy with the prisoner’s views. His Honor
passed a sentence of two years’ imprisonmeii
under protest, as it were. Duty compelled him
to “ look at the morality of the thing, neverthe-
less he felt bound to admit that “ the case was a
terribly sad one.” Justice Rogers s leniency
reminds one again of Corkett, in the drama.
When Corkett enquires of liis old men ,
Coombe, concerning the probable legal penalty
for borrowing a governor’s money without men-
tioning of it, he is told, truthfully, that the
British Bench is severe upon unauthorised bor-
rowers who back ’orses with the money. ( ull
from a “nice, kind Judge, with his stumnuck
in good working order,” a Corkett stands an
excellent chance of getting seven years haid
labor. The two cases of Corkett and the
Sydney bank-clerk are not quite on all fours,
and certainly The Bulletin doesn’t advocate a
policy of seven years’ hard labor for every
small-salaried embezzler who goes gambling on
the turf. But an ordinary working man in
receipt of a sure and steady 50s. per week gets
no judicial word of compassion when he is con-
victed of theft, small or large. There are no
extenuating circumstances in his case. He is
considered to have been well provided for by
his wage, inasmuch as millions of his fellow-
men contrive to exist honestly upon less money,
working longer hours the while. Justice Rogers
would probably drop heavily on an ambitious
bricklayer who, actuated by a genteel desire to
live in better style, stole £ll2O for betting-
purposes ; therefore, in dropping so softly upon
this cashier he gave one more proof that the
Law, for all it may say to the contrary, is a
barefaced respector of persons. The Law, ac-
cording to Mr. Rogers, can find some justification
for a roguish bank-clerk where it finds none for
a bricklayer. The one man’s inexcusable crime
is the other man’s unfortunate misdemeanor,
the difference in their degrees of guilt being
solely attributable to the other man’s “superior
position in society.” Thus saitli the law, as far
as The Bulletin can understand it. If it
didn’t intend to express itself to that effect, if
it isn’t a respector of persons, and a drawer of
nice distinctions, perhaps his Honor will kindly
explain where the especially terrible sadness
aforesaid could possibly come in.
Low Down on tlie Deity.
Scotch and thrifty Baron Lamington, of whom
it is alleged (doubtless truthlessly) that not a
sparrow falls to the grounds of Govt. House
but he secures its feathers for a bolster, last
week opened the twelfth Queensland Parlia-
ment with the usual shoddy simulacrum of
pomp and ceremony. The assembled mediocri-
ties were given to understand that the All-
Seeing Eye of Queen Vic. had them well in
focus ; and the economical peer then patted the
new boys on the head, gave them 3d. each (but
no whisky) with strict injunctions not to spend
more than Id. at a time, and retired in clearing
the ground for action with one generous and
comprehensive wish: “May the Blessing of
Almighty God rest upon your labors !” From
the infinitely great the new Parliament re-
bounded at once to the infinitely little. The
cocksure Cowley— Govt, instrument, servant
and partisan—was re-elected Speaker as a
matter of course ; and it was noted as signifi-
cant that Premier Nelson put up Attorney-
General Byrnes to “congratulate” his nominee,
and himself followed his follower. At latest
the Ministerial policy—a continuation of “ drift,
dribble, and bolster the bank,” without a single
promise of vital progress—was still under dis-
cussion. Contemplate the grotesque medley on
which the youthful Governor supplicated the
Divine blessing ! Admire Premier Nelson, the
blas6 and cynical election-rigger, who, having
secured a puppet-majority for his party and a
title for his female relations, is about to retire,
well-pleased, from the sordid little farce of
local politics. Note the disappointed glare
with which Truthful Tozer eyes the swollen
and ambitious Byrnes, the Premier’s under-
study, pining to earn, like him, the plaudits of
Fat-Man, the hisses of the people. Mark
Glassey, divorced from his piano, but not from
his eternal organ; Groom, grown old and
toothless, yet still avid of the Cabinet-seat
which will will-o’-the-wisp him into his grave ;
shaven Hoolan, jaundiced Dalrymple, take-it-
easy Drake. Cast an eye over the crowd of
mean little men with mean little motives—
here and there a heart honest, a head able and
from them pass to the electorates they repre-
sent —North, South, and Centre. Cairns !
with its latest leper kept for three
months within ten yards of the public
footpath, tended by the local constabu-
lary, now taken in a boat to be marooned
on the other side of the inlet, with strict
Government injunctions to “burn the boat to
avoid infection.” Rosewood ! with its melan-
choly swamp jobbed under the Agricultural
Lands Purchase Act, which provides for the
re - purchase of estates without reference
to Parliament. Mackay ! lying in wait for
the loans on the sugar-lands of Govt, supporters,
the railways through the selections of Govt,
members —“If you want a railway, you must
return the men who can get you a railway.”
What a stew ! what a spectacle ! And over all,
enclosing, protecting, broods with outstretched
wings the serene and consolatory Paraclete :
upon all, with true Scottish humor, Baron
Lamington invokes the blessing of Almighty
God !
Sydney Daily Telegraph advances a reason
why the attempted prosecution of Tooth and
Co. on a charge of illegally obtaining rebate of
beer duty should have been smothered :
No doubt, where a man applies for a summons against
another on a charge of having committed some indictable
offence, of which he is himself the victim, and gives the
necessary guarantees of good faith, it is a usual and a
proper thing for one to issue. But it is quite a new
doctrine that where the charge is that somebody has
in hired somebody else, who emphatically denies having
been so injured, the person making it is entitled to insist
upon a Crown prosecution. If that was the case, whole-
sale blackmail might soon be expected to become the
order of the day.
This is glaringly false logic from beginning to
end. The beer-duty is part of the revenue. It
isn’t the property of the Customs Department,
but of the people at large, of whom the person
making the charge is one. The alleged fraud,
if it were proved to exist, would be a personal
wrong to the informer as well as to every other
member of the community. There was, there-
fore no allegation that a wrong had been com-
mitted against “somebody else.” The defenders
of Tooth and Co. are merely, by their mis-
placed enthusiasm, bringing down suspicion—
quite possibly unjust suspicion—on that firm;
when people so earnestly demand that a case
shall not be publicly investigated, the great
illogical public begins to wonder if they are
afraid of the results of an investigation.
Premier Reid’s advocacy of the firm is especi-
ally misplaced, seeing that he admits lie is
“ intimately acquainted with two or three
leading members of the co. and is, therefore
barracking for his personal friends. And the
conduct of the Government which won l e\en
allow a summons to issue against the firm which
by an unfortunate coincidence happens to
contain two or three of the Premier’s friends,
is, to say the least, widely different froin its
conduct in arresting—not issuing a summons
against, but arresting by warrant, and rushing
into the lock-up, and endeavoring to retain
there without bail - its political enemy,
Crick, on what, after all, proved to be an
unfounded charge. And when it alleges that it
won’t proceed against Tooth and Co. (with
whom, or some of whom, Premier Reid is
“ intimately acquainted ”) because the evidence
is insufficient, people with awkward memories
recall that it proceeded vigorously against
Crick, not only with insufficient evidence, but
with absolutely no evidence worth calling such
at all. Altogether the unhappy fact that
Premier Reid is “ intimately acquainted” with
leading members of Tooth and Co. makes the
matter look more lamentable than ever.
Cabbage Garden Conservatism is at great
pains to deny Lormer his well-earned credit
for blotting certain honorary magistrates out of
existence. Thus the Argus in a recent issue :
“ At the meeting of the Cabinet yesterday the
Solicitor-General brought under the notice of
his colleagues the suggestion made by the
Lormer Board that the services rendered by
detective M‘Manamny to the Board, and to
Mr. Maxted, barrister-at-law, who conducted
the inquiry, should be recognised by the Govern-
ment.” Informant Lormer, but for whose
original statement of cases calling for inquiry
the Board would never have come to pass, is an
item of no inportance, judging from this in-
spired par. Moreover, the suggestion to grease
detective M'Manamny seems to have emanated
from the Crown Law Office, or the Argus, for
the Lormer Enquiry Board disowns it indig-
nantly. Wherever the suggestion may have
come from it is clearly an attempt to belittle
Lormer’s services to the public. He it was
who shifted Rabiport, Baxter and Co. from
their defiled judgment seats.
The following is the programme with which
the Kingston Government starts the liew
session of S.A. Parliament: —
" Australian Federation on Democratic basis, includ-
ing a uniform Australian franchise consisting of
adult suffrage and with payment of members applied
to both Houses of the Federal Parliament; interpro-
vincial Freetrade ; the Referendum ; elective Ministries;
reduction of the Council franchise from £25 to £l5 for
occupiers and extension of the Council franchise to lease-
holders with £2OO improvements ; continuous Parlia-
ments, with two-member districts, but one member re-
tiring every two years; avoidance of fresh taxation and
increase of the income-tax exem]ition to £l5O ; loan
consolidation ; Civil Service reform and superannuation :
the Kopperamanna railway ; extension of the export de-
partment ; amendment of shipping laws; law reform ;
aid to early closing and drink-sale amendment
bills. Social reforms, particularly including («) the raising
of the age of consent; (6) simplification of the remedies
of deserted wives on the lines of English legislation ; (c)
Opium Bill ; opposition to the Japanese treaty, and ex-
clusion of colored labor.
The Continuous Parliament idea is an unpro-
mising fad, and the Age of Consent racket looks
like a foolish effort to capture the Dirty Saint
vote. Also, the proposal to reduce taxation is
the sort of drivel which every Australian trea-
surer hangs out to attract support, when the
thing really wanted is to reduce borrowing.
Otherwise, Kingston’s programme looks an
honest democratic policy, and unless something
unforeseen happens (something unforeseen often
does happen) the Government, which has
already the longest continuous record of office
in S.A. history, should last through this Par-
liament.

The long-winded case of Edwards v. Melb.
Board of Works has at last been settled. A
Board official in charge of the Board’s land at
Whittlesea allowed the grass to take fire in 1892,
and the conflagration spread to Edwards’ land,
where it did much damage to property, and in-
directly to plaintiffs wife who tried to extinguish
it. Edwards and wife got a verdict, but the
Board kept appealing, and got four separate
trials, and lost each time. The damage was
proved ; it was also proved that the fire started
on the Board’s land, and that the Board’s official
didn’t try to put it out; but the weak-kneed
Bench kept haggling over a question as to
whether the Board’s representative “approved ”
of the fire, which, as approval is an inward
affair, no one could possibly answer. The Full
Court has now rejected the Board’s appeal for
a fifth trial, so the Fitzgibbon institution,
having spent ,£5OO or so in costs to try and
avoid paying <£ll7 damages, seems to be finally
left. As for plaintiff, hiscrops were destroyed and
his wife was badly injured, and after four years
law-struggle he gets about enough damages to
pay one-half of the cost of getting them. Why
can’t there be some finality about the civil law ?
In the criminal law a man gets one hearing
before the court and, possibly, one hearing on
appeal upon points of law only. In a civil
case the rich litigant can easily get four trials,
with a possibility of a fifth one and a Privy
Council appeal at the end of all, and he can be
heard over and over again on matters of
evidence as well as matters of law. A hy not
give just as much legal rope in one case as
in the other ?
Dear Bulletin, If E. W. O’Sullivan,
M. really wants to know why certain
N. W. Protectionist leaders are “suspect” of
Democracy, let him hark his memory back to a
few weeks ago, when he moved a motion in a
public meeting that the Governor be petitioned
to dissolve Parliament. That will explain a
g6od deal of restless doubt as to his democratic
bona-Jides —for any Governor who would have
dared to dissolve Parliament without constitu-
tional advice from his advisers would have been
as big a fool as the man who, while asking h in
to do such an outrageous thing, still jogs along
wondering why the people suspect his demo-
cratic protestations. The Protectionists, in my
humble opinion, have the best of the economic
argument and the worst of the advocates.
The most devoted advocacy of programmes will
not counteract in the public mind a suspicion
of theatrical stupidity. In politics stupidity
is the one unpardonable offence, and O’Sulli-
van’s programme-mongering, his stagey inter-
pellations, and “ put-the-limeliglit-on-me ” atti-
tudes in politics have rendered him suspect.
Moreover*he must look about for better illus-
trations and arguments than his well-known
“ Napoleon was a Protectionist, Lincoln was
a Protectionist, I am a Protectionist: there-
fore* Protection is right.” It doesn’t follow at
all j because, apart from these points of agree-
ment, Napoleon was a tyrant, Lincoln was a
lawyer, and O’Sullivan is O’Sullivan, lhe
present N.S.W. Protectionist collective “ light
and leading ” is too shaky on the feet, too stiff
in the back, and too fat in the head to encour-
age hope of ultimate success. A political
Joshua is still badly wanted ; but he must not
expect to break down the walls by simply
blowing bis own trumpet. —Yours, O’Hooligan.
Writes a Tasmanian correspondent: —
In a recent issue you say, “ The Vic. Upper House is the
most solid, reliable, and brutally arrogant stumbling-
block in Australasia.” This is inaccurate. The Tas.
Council is about the most hopelessly Tory Chamber on
this planet. Nothing but Federation, a revolution, or an
earthquake can bring progressive legislation into force in
this island. The Council has consistently rejected every
Democratic measure introduced into the Tas. legislature
during the past 30 years, including Manhood and Woman-
hood Suffrage, Police Centralization, and about a dozen
Land-tax Bills ; and last session it threw out without dis-
cussion a Bill to provide for its own dissolution whenever
it twice rejected any measure sent up by the Assembly.
In Victoria, where three-fourths of the Assembly voters
also possess the Council franchise, there is a faint hope of
ultimate reform. In Tasmania, there is none. Only 20
per cent, of the adult males can vote for the
Tory Chamber, and it probably fairly represents
the' views of that 20 per cent. Premier Braddon,
who does not believe in representative government
at all, cheerfully promises to introduce a straight-
out electoral-reform measure next session in the con-
fident and fully-warranted expectation that the Council
will throw it under the table without dividing. It is
amusing to see the brisk alacrity with which the crusted
members of the Assembly pass reforming Acts. They
know the said measures might be relegated to a church
debating-society for all the benefit their introduction is
likely to bring to the province. Besides, instead of a
third of the fossils retiring every second year, a sixth re-
tire annually, so that, even in the utterly impossible con-
tingency of inducing the reactionary percentage of elec-
tors to put in sound Democrats for four years in succes-
sion, it would take from now until 1900 to get a Radical
majority. Your remarks re the personnel of the Victorian
Councillors apply verbatim to the Tasmanians. Only one
of the 18, Piesse, even professes to be a Democrat. He
only got in by accident and does not stand a million-to-
one chance of re-election.
Australian alleged justice is mostly a matter
of longitude and liver. At Brisbane, on May
11, Judge Cooper gave a man three years’ p.s.
for stealing a saddle ; at Perth, on May 4,
P, M. Cowan awarded only six months to
another man who stole a side-saddle —probably
the more valuable article. P.M. Smithers, of
Sydney, on May 26 gave a man three months’
hard for stealing <£2 10s. from a Jap. ;
and on same date same magistrate only
awarded another fellow one month hard
for stealing £2 2s. 6d. from a white. Next day
P.M. Giles, also of Sydney, stretched two
people for four months’ each—one for stealing
jj], and the other for stealing a watch value
M. And four days after P.M. Edwards, also
of Sydney, awarded £1 or 21 days for stealing
tools value £2 ; and same sentence for stealing
bags value Is. 3d. Exactly how long a thief
goes up tor, or whether he’s let off with a fine,
evidently depends a lot on the place where he
has the good or bad luck to get arrested.
The police practice of holding back witnesses,
so justly reprobated by N.S.W. Judge Murray,
has been the cause of a development on the
prisoner’s side which the Judge does not appear
to have noticed. The accused person, if guilty
of even a tithe of the criminality charged
against him, sees at a very early stage of the
proceedings that the police are “holding back,
and consequently, when his turn comes, he
“reserves his defence.” And if he be wholly
innocent and his lawyer “cute,” he still reserves
his defence, as he can best substantiate his
innocence when the complete charges are before
him. It thus happens that the lower courts
have become mere skirmishing grounds—places
where the contestants take, as it were, trial
falls out of one another—and not tribunals for
seriously considering the guilt or innocence of
persons arraigned.
A time-honored rule of English law sets out
that the courts will never enquire into the
“ adequacy of a consideration.” Which means
that, as long as there is a “consideration,” no
matter how flimsy in nature, a Contract entered
into must be carried out to the bitter end. If,
for instance, a man engages to Vvork for ss. per
year, that contract will be enforced as far as
the courts can enforce it. This doctrine has
been productive of enormous evils, and is
due to the disinclination of the judges
to unravel the difficult question of values
—in fact, to the laziness of Judges. But,
as the judges are drawn from the wealthier
classes, one exception is made to this rule,
namely, the case of a prodigal reversioner. If
this young spark sells his patrimonial rights
for a song, the contract is easily upset. The
legislature has always tacitly acquiesced in this
judicial shirking of work. Accordingly, it is
interesting to note that the \ ic. Stamps Act
has expressly referred to the necessity of certain
deeds being for “ bona fide adequate pecuniary
consideration ” in order to escape taxation.
This provision will probably lead to new de-
velopments in the rather absurd English doc-
trine of consideration.
On the Darling (N.S.W.), the art to dance
at an aboriginal corrobboree is essentially the art
to shiver. The champion of Buckaroon is open to
shiver any man, white or black, for a young gin
and ten plugs of tobacco.
The lamentable organ of the students at
Melb. University, Alma Mater, brings forward a
“Proposal in Politics,” which aims at procuring
a M.P. for the University after the English
model. There are 600 students, about three-
fourths of whom are under 21, and in order to
atone for this smallness in number, the brazen
voice of the University suggests that matricula-
tion should be taken as the basis of the voting-roll.
Considering that hardly one in half-a-dozen school-
boys or schoolgirls who matriculate goes on to the
University, this proposal takes the cake for calm
cheek. And as the University man and matricu-
lated man is scattered all over Victoria, and is
especially rife in the city, and at the same time no
mention is made of disfranchising him in his own
electorate, it is to be presumed that the hopeful
undergraduate considers himself worthy of an
extra vote by virtue of “ cul-chaw ”—of course
claiming a third in acknowledgment of hi?
“thrift.”

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOB COMING WEEK.
Lyceum “ A Trip to Chinatown.”
Her Majesty’s “ The Crimson Thread.”
Royal “ The Derby Winner.”
Tivoli Rickards’ Variety Co.
The farewell performance of the Brough-
Boucicault Co. on Saturday night, when Dot Bou-
cicault got a big benefit and a presentation, was a
great spectacle. The Cri. was crammed in every
part, even the orchestra being occupied by the ad-
miring public, in consequence of which the band
had to thrum its little bit of music on the stage
behind the curtain, and then fly in disorder with
its drum and its cymbal and sackbut and shawm
and trombone before the curtain went up. The
‘ ‘ Amazons ” was played for this affecting occasion,
probably because the Frenchman, De Grival, is
about the best of Boucicault’s many good char-
acters. Then, after the show, a big crowd ap-
peared on the stage, including Cecil Ward with
the black eye he got from Dorrington in Act 11.,
and Titheradge leaning wearily on a chair-back,
and Brough in the background ; and ex-Premier
Dibbs proceeded to wrestle with the presentation
in a truly painful spirit. Dibbs was probably
selected for the job because he is a general favorite
and is only a yard taller than Boucicault; and he
rose to the emergency with all the lightness and
appropriateness of a kedge-anchor in a drawing-
room. For ten solemn minutes he wandered round
himself, and fell, metaphorically speaking, over
his own feet, and kept coming out at the same hole
lie went in at, and lost himself in a great maze of
adjectives and prepositions and conjunctions, and
left half-finished statements sticking up all over
the place like the remains of old scaffoldings which
Luscombe hadn’t properly inspected. Finally, he
made an unusually bad break and waded ashore
with his peroration in his mouth, and handed
Boucicault the presentation album and the purse.
He hung on to the purse for some time after he
had torn himself away from the album, till people
began to think he wasn’t going to part with it,
but meant to put it to Boucicault’s credit in the
Savings Bank. Also, he paid a dreadfully awk-
ward compliment to Mrs. Brough—it was pro-
bably well-meant, but it would almost have justified
Brough in stoushing the orator with a brick—and
retired with both feet in his mouth as usual.
Dibbs seldom speaks without putting one foot in
his mouth, but this time he broke his own record
hopelessly.
* * * *
Boucicault made a perfect little oration in
reply. He never misplaced a word, or lost the
thread of his ideas, or fell down with an awful
crash in his vocabulary, or shuffled wildly with his
boots; and everything he said was in good taste
and to the point. Then Brough said a few words
with moderate success, and the function ter-
minated. The presentation album contained a
charming collection of water - color and other
sketches by leading Sydney artists, and the purse
is said to have been solid. So, with these assets in
his possession, Dot Boucicault starts England-
wards, and the good wishes of Australia in general
go with him.
** * *
Hoyt’s comedy co. appears at Sydney
Lyceum on Saturday in “ A Trip to Chinatown,”
and The Bulletin’s views on that American
frivolity will be duly recorded in next issue.
“Trilby” finishes its career to-morrow (Friday)
night. The latter days of “Trilby” have been-
somewhat disappointing, inasmuch as the heroine,
who didn’t show her feet to any great extent even
at the beginning, has lately been showing them
less than ever, and as the drama is distinctly a
feet drama this tendency to omit the lady’s ex-
tremities is reprehensible. However, it is possible
that even if Miss Crane had been a shoeless centi-
pede she wouldn’t have shown enough feet to
satisfy some of her admirers.
** * *
George Darrell promises “ The Crimson
Thread ” for Saturday night at Her Majesty’s.
This is one of Darrell’s many dramas which hasn’t
yet been seen in Sydney, and as Sydney might
wake up and cry for it in the night if it wasn’t
produced here, it is coming as above-mentioned.
** * *
Harry Rickards is re-appearing for ten
nights only at Sydney Tivoli, and ventriloquist
Win ton and the tuneful Ada Colley are also on
the bill. Also, the coroner’s jury has committed
on a charge of manslaughter the hulking indi-
vidual who fell out of the gallery while struggling
for a front seat, and killed a stall patron by drop-
ping on his spine. So far as The Bulletin knows,
dropping out of a gallery is about the only act
which doesn’t constitute a capital offence in N.S.
Wales, and, owing to this strange omission, the
person who fell will probably escape the gallows.
If it proves that there is no law to reach him
possibly Attorney-General Want will draft a bill
for the suppression of profane clumsiness and the
violent, hob-nailed variety of shoving and tramp-
ling in public places —these being the apparent
causes of the trouble in this instance.
** * *
The bright and cheery “ Derby Winner ”
will run for some little time longer at Sydney
Royal. Fitzgerald’s Circus is now taking its
damp, frost-bitten lion and its rheumatic tiger
around the suburbs prior to its departure for the
milder climate of Queensland. Prof. Kennedy is
running his mean, ugly show at Sydney Opera
House, and alleges in his advertisement that it is
“Endorsed by the Clergy,” which presents the
clergy in the worst light this paper has yet seen
them in. And otherwise things are cold and dank
and melancholy, and there is no joy left in the
N.S.W. capital—save the Income-tax.
