Mdlle. Trebelli began her concert season
in Sydney Town Hall on Saturday night, and
made even a bigger success than she did last year.
The co. includes baritone John Prowse ; Strauss,
the well-known ’cellist; Walter Kirby and Miss
Odell-Fawcett; and these four are all excellent in
their respective ways. The star’s voice was in
magnificent condition, and the big audience in-
cluded His Ex., the Gov. The daily papers, by
the way, remark that Gov. Hampden “ honored ”
the sweet singer with his presence -which is the
natural daily paper way of putting it.
* ifc- * *
The second of the Potter-Bellew produc-
tions at Melb. Princess’s is “ Charlotte Corday,” a
fragile tragedy, said to have been written more or
less by the two principals. The programme
describes it as “ founded on an incident in the life
of Charlotte Corday ” viz., the stabbing of
Marat. Properly speaking, it is founded on the
conception of the incident, built up to the
occurrence of the incident, and rounded off with
the incident’s immediate result. In the first act,
beautiful, cranky Charlotte merely thinks of kill-
ing Marat; in the second act, she quite makes up
her mind to kill Marat; in the third act, she
actually does the deed ; and, in the fourth, she
goes away to be guillotined for her pains. Char-
lotte and Marat run the show between them,
assisted by 21 subsidiary characters on the stage,
and much rumbling of voices crying “Marat!”
behind the scenes. The roar of the distant multi-
tude is without question the most natural acting
in the piece. It wafts an aroma of revolution
across the footlights.
* * * *
Mrs. Potter’s appearance is highly sugges-
tive of the Charlotte Corday—the saintly young
virgin with a gory mission—depicted in Carlyle’s
“ History of the French Revolution.” She looks
the noble character, and inasmuch as Charlotte
was probably eccentric in her modes of speech,
and stagey in gesture, Mrs. Potter may claim to
act the part in the right spirit. All the same, she
fails to make one believe that she is other than
Mrs. Potter, and even her strongest situations
carry Very little weight. The assassination of
Marat in his bath is by no means thrilling. The
tyrant, who seemingly sits huddled up in a
large arm - chair, with his feet in mustard-
ana-water, gets to angry words with his lady
visitor. Then she bends over the absurd
mummy and lunges her right arm at his
chest, after which she informs her audience
that France is Free, and the curtain falls. Nobody
shudders. Subsequently, when Mrs. Potter takes
leave of her friends in prison, and goes away to
execution, she doesn’t convey much sense of her
approaching doom. Indeed, the lady must nave
lost such nervous force as she possessed six years
ago, if her performance on Saturday was the best
she can do.
* * *
The Marat presented by Bellew is an aged,
creaky caricature of numanity, combining the
mannerisms, and much of the “ business,” of
Irving’s Louis XI. with some of the crawl-
someness of Harvey Duff, the Oirish informer.
The actor is evidently hiding his identity,
and his Marat can’t be accepted as the ugly mon-
ster of 50 years, directing Parisian fury in the
early days of the Reign of Terror. The maddest
mob would scarcely be swayed by this decrepit
wheezer, who shrieks and falls in greasy ruins at
the reflection of his own face in a mirror. The
performance is a clever study of stage tricks, but
not convincing. The second act passes in the
studio of Jacques David, where Charlotte Corday
is supposed to sit to the painter for the character
of Judith. David, who was only six years younger
than Marat, is played in light juvenile style by
Scot Inglis, and Charlotte’s lover (an unnecessary
incident) is played in heavy juvenile style by R.
Stewart. Cathcart, Edwin Kelly, Geo. Ireland,
and Hans Phillips are the most noticeable people
in the background, but none of them disturb the
slow current of a sad story.
* * * #
On Saturday the Princess’s bill will be
changed to “ Camille,” in which Mrs. Potter and
Bellew opened their previous campaign.

*# * *
“ The Pointsman,” a drama in several
pungent acts and one famous railway catastrophe,
is concluding the Westmacott season at Mel-
bourne Royal. The weak feature of “ The
Pointsman ” is its plot, which starts with the
murder of a young man named Fred, and then
appears to lose the run of its moral purpose.
After a while the hero gets upon the track of the
licensed victualler who slew poor Fred, and pur-
sues him with enthusiasm, but considering that
Fred’s corpse was never discovered, while the evi-
dence of Ins decease would certainly break down
under cross-examination, it seems a pity to trouble
any more about the matter.
* * * *
Nevertheless, “ The Pointsman ” grips an
audience’s attention; and holds it to the bitter end.
A reckless barmaid, who went astray before
matrimony, and feels disposed to repeat the indis-
cretion afterwards, is a type of Hebe character
and full of human interest. Likewise, her com-
monplace husband, who takes to diink, and a
longshore loafer who never took to anything else,
and the cold-blooded villain in evening dress, and
the energetic railway-porter married to the bar-
maid’s virtuous sister—all these people are real.
Fred may be said to live until he meets his doom
in the prologue, and there is even an air of
naturalness about the train that comes snorting
across the stage, although it travels without an
engine-driver or stoker.
* * * *
This play doesn’t fit the Royal company as
neatly as “ Silver King ” did. Miss Harrie Ire-
land and Plimmer are quite satisfactory, and
Miss Fitzmaurice Gill, as the barmaid, acts with a
lot of untrained force and inexperienced ability.
King Hedley and numerous other people seem
either too large or too small for their parts, the
worst offender of them all being the actor /who
shone as the drunken passenger in “Silver King.”
This time he is a serious medical inai A\ n the
character he amounts to a criunn
profession. _

The boom in Town Hall concerts has died
away for the moment, but on Monday ( • ) a
series of Winter Popular Concerts will be in-
augurated at that spacious building, on " pc
occasion Titheradge is to appear in a mysterious
something described as a New Role. Also, a
pianist of historic interest will appear at the
Bijou on June 30. Chevalier De Kontski, a hale
octogenarian who studied under Beethoven and
frequently sat at meat with Mendelssohn, Spohr,
and other old masters, is the artist in question.
Glen and Co. are responsible for the Chevalier s
visit, and the affable Hugo Fischer is bringing his
unique celebrity into due notice.

A small touring co. in low water hit upon a notable
scheme the other day. Arriving at a township, with no
money to go on or back, the genius of the manager rose
to the occasion. The last pound scraped from their
united pockets was devoted to advertising, and next day
the township hoardings were placarded
“ To-night! To-night ! The All-Star Company at the
Mechanics’ Institute.
The Way of the World ! Thrilling Drama
FOR MEN ONLY !"
That night the hall was crammed to the doors. The per-
formance was mild as milk ; but the co. cleared enough to
pay its hotel bill and come back by rail.

The Argus praises Miss Gill’s performance in “ Points-
man, ’ because it was not “melancholy” like Maud
Williamson’s (the original). The Lizzie who can be cheerful
really deserves praise. In the prologue she starts her
lively career with the bailiffs in the house, and is aban-
doned by the man she is living with ; in act I. she is
denounced as his mistress in the presence of her happy,
innocent sister, who implores her to say she is his wife—
which truth compels her to deny; in the next she becomes
an “ honest woman,” and is bashed on the head by the
porter who “gave her his name,” and with whom she is
starving in a garret; in the last act the villain who aban-
doned her turns up and tries to slit her throat. These
trifling circumstances naturally make any ordinary woman
bright. The garret-scene has been plaved in lace skirts
silk dress and jewels, less “depressing” than rags, but
you can t call it art — or nature. It has taken the Argus
seven years to pick holes in a performance it eulogised on
the two separate occasions M.W. played the part.

Smellbourne’s medical men are rolling up in force to
sample the feats of the Frantz Family, at the Opera
House. And the women seem equally interested. T’other
n j<rht, as a well-known surgeon was leaving the theatre
with a party of ladies attached, he asked them how about
the following Thursday—would they rather see “ As You
Like It,” or come again to the Rickards show. With one
dulcet voice the visions replied, “Come here again.”
Which suggests that educated muscle is mightier than
the poetic "drama these times. Even Bellew’s legs seemed
as naught to them compared with the spectacle of Mrs.
Frantz "twirling her loving husband round her little
finger.

Here and there, Mrs. Potter somewhat mars the graces
of Shakespeare’s diction and the music of his blank verse.
The word “wrestled” becomes “reselled" (accent heavy
on the “ ell ”), “ cough” becomes “ korf,” and a “saucy
lackey ” is Pottered into a “ sossy leckey.” Indeed, the
woman who shone in “ As You Like It,” for her pleasant,
matter-of-fact way of treating the bard, was Miss Lynda
Raymond as Celia.
One new spot in the 8.8. co.’s last performance of the
“ Magistrate ” was Hay man, as Stipendiary Posket’s man-
servant. It was a new reading of the little part; a fat,
unctuous reading ; the performance of an oily, confiden-
tial flunkey, who had suspected his master of all kinds of
midnight riot from the beginning. People expected to
see Harman’s flunkey offer to sell Posket a harem, cheap,
when that magistrate had fallen from grace ; or else prod
him in the ribs and give him a photo, of a burlesque
female with very little on. The difference between Hay-
man and his predecessor in the part raises the question :
When the respectable old man is going out surreptitiously
at a late hour, apparently after drink and Flossies, should
the flunkey preserve his wooden aspect to show his perfect
training, or should he grin respectfully, just as a hint that
he knows what the old man is about, and that his silence
will require to be paid for? Obviously, if he is too
wooden, it may' not dawn on the old man that he needs
payment.
The express-train effect in “ The Pointsman,” at Melb.
Royal, was rather a failure on the opening night, owing
to the mechanist having fitted the engine with a heavier
boiler, or something, than it had carried at rehearsal.
Since the necessary- balance of weight, fore and aft, was
re-established, the train has travelled like wildfire, and
this sensation is one of the gaudiest jewels in Westma-
cott’s crown.
Manager Williamson, it is rumored, lies awake o’ nights
haunted by thoughts of the new panto, story which he is
slowly distilling from “Gulliver’s Travels” and “The
Light of Asia.” No brain on earth could keep itself free
from excitement under the circumstances. Why not try
a counter-irritant in the form of a musical comedy
decocted from the psalms of David and the “ Mystery of a
Hansom Cab,” working four hours a day on both schemes
at once ? It is by such desperate expedients that Augustus
Harris keeps himself going.
The most flattering thing they can say of one member
of a certain theatrical co. now visiting Australia is that
his wife is the prettiest woman on the English stage—and
he deserves it.
Beliew, in his dressing-room at the theatre, is not at all
the languid, poetic dreamer that gushing girlhood would
expect him to be. He doesn’t recline in a luxurious arm-
chair whilst a valet combs his curly white hair (quite
white by this time) and changes his boots. In fact, Beliew
dispenses with the assistance of a valet, or dresser, and
rushes through his own toilet in about the quickest time
on record. He is even younger and more spry off the
stage than on, and twenty-five years hence will probably
be as light on his feet as Boucicault pire at the same age.
Australia has a strong contingent of artistic and literary
successes in Europe, but some of the South American
States are also well represented. Mascagni and Leon-
cavallo were both students in Buenos Ayres ; and Bem-
berg, a rising composer, and author of “ Elaine,” is a
native of Brazil.

The Wild-Cat Column.
The collapse of the Ward Farmers’ Association
In Maorilaud is one more interesting essav on balance-
sheets. The Association was the stock and station busi-
ness of Treasurer J. G. Ward floated into the usual
limited-liability co. Ward was managing director and
great panjandrum of the business. In its last balance-
sheet the Association showed a profit of £4236 for the
year, with £2280 remaining from the year before, and. it
declared a div. of 7 per cent., a bonus of 3 percent, to
shareholders on goods purchased, a rebate of 25 per cent,
on commission charged to shareholders on their wool-
sales, a rebate of 10 per cent, on other sales, and gave its
employes a bonus of 3 per cent, on salaries and otherwise
rioted around. The overdraft at the Colonial Bank was
set down at less than £l2OO. It is now'alleged on affidavit
that the overdraft was £26,000, but that a draft for
£25,000 was drawn on somebody or other as a matter of
form, and credited to the co. just before the balance-sheet
was made up, and that it was debited again afterwards,
so that the overdraft went up again to the original £26,000,
and was really £26,000 all the time. It is also averred that
a large amount owing to the bank on bills and drafts was
left out of the statement. Ward and the Association to-
gether owe the Colonial Bank £166,000, and the pro-
spective loss to the bank is about £lOO,OOO. The fallen
Treasurer himself owes £96,000 in various directions, and
sets down his assets at £4OOO, so he promises to pan
out for ninepence in the £. A considerable part
of the bank’s security consists of Ward’s unsup-
ported p.n. for £55,000, and another security ap-
pears to be his draft for 120,000; and part of the
Association’s assets consist of £38,513 owing by Ward
directly and indirectly. Its other assets consist mostly
of “book debts, £85,000,” of which nearly £40,000 are
said to be bad, and “ goodwill ” (of all things in the
world), “ £11,000.” The Association is expected to pan
out for 10s. or less in the £.
The Ward Farmers’ Association existed three
years altogether, and lost £lOO,OOO. Apparently to
the very last it kept putting down its bad debts
and its “goodwill” and Ward’s hopeless liabilities,
as good assets, and seemingly the Colonial Bank
also put down the Association’s dreadful overdraft
as a good asset to the very last. Ward, as boss
director, was following politics in Wellington, and the
Association far away in Invercargill practically ran itself.
No one seems to know exactly which was Ward and which
was the Association, or whether Ward knew how the
Association was going, or whether the Association knew
itself, or whether the amalgamation of the Colonial Bank
(which was staggering under the Association’s overdraft)
with the B.N.Z. was done by the Government to save
Ward in any way, or whether Ward was sacrificed to save
the bank, or whether the Colonial Bank directors urged
on the amalgamation because they were afraid to keep on
professing to regard Ward as a good asset any longer,
Anyhow, there is a fine, large, robust odour about the
balance-sheet business once more, and Treasurer Ward is
one more among the great army of politicians who have
gone under.
® * * * *
It is announced that the Commercial Bank of
Australia has practically arrived at an agreement with its
reconstructed depositors. The terms are understood to
include an extension of time for repayment of deposits,
and an extension of the dates for payment of calls, which
are to fall due henceforth every half-year instead of every
three months. The Commercial called up all its available
capital—£l,Boo,ooo, the biggest call in Australia—and its
shareholders are staggering so badly under the load that
some relief was much needed. In addition, the interest
on the reconstructed deposits is to be reduced to the rate
now current in Victoria, which probably means 3 per cent,
instead of the present 4J. On the £5,700,000 of deposits
this would mean a saving of £85,500 a year, and if the
falling-away in the profits doesn’t continue till this
saving is all swallowed up, and the board doesn’t
fool it away in paying dividends on preference
capital, but devotes it "rigidly to writing off its
bad debts, the Commercial should gradually get into a
better position. No one really knows what the Commer-
cial’s bad debts amount to —whether they are £500,000 or
£3,000,000 ; the revelation is put off, as in the cases of two
other Melbourne banks, until the Legislature passes a
new Companies Act authorising the writing-off of lost
capital ; therefore no one can even seriously profess to
know how long it will take to arrive at the better position
above mentioned—it may be three years, or again it may
be 50. But unless some hitch in the arrangements arises
at the eleventh hour the big institution should be
definitely on the up-grade. Another valuable concession
has been obtained from the old creditors, inasmuch as
new deposits are henceforth to have a prior claim on the
bank’s assets. This will enable the Commercial to get
new business which was formerly unattainable, and with-
out which it would be practically a dead concern going
through a process of unofficial liquidation.
The S.A. State Bank made a very small be-
ginning, the other day, by selling £20,000 worth of 3)4
per cent, bonds to provide money for lending out on
mortgage. The amount offered was vastly in excess of
the demand, and the price realised was something like
£lO3. The chief feature of the Land Mortgage depart-
ment of the S.A. State Bank up to date has been its
lamentable slowness in getting afloat and the miserable
smallness of its beginning ; £20,000 with which to start
the Credit Foncier business is a mere drop in the bucket.
Moreover, the management has done nearly two months
hard thinking before it made even this small commence-
ment. Still, it has begun well enough in one sense ; the
£5OO or £6OO premium realised on this small issue is a
considerable amount towards the working expenses of the
institution.
Some day The Bulletin hopes to see a State
Land Bank started on a still better basis than that of
S.A., good as the principle of it undoubtedly is. In
N.S.W., for example, the average paper currency afloat is
about £1,400,000 or £1,500,000. If the State became the
sole issuer of paper currency it could easily keep this
amount afloat with a basis of £200,000 or £300,000 of gold,
which represents a sufficient margin for fluctuations. It
would be necessary, of course, to devise means of making
the banks circulate the State paper, which they would
probably avoid doing if possible ; but this could be
effected by the simple and effective, if somewhat high-
handed, process of requiring them to keep a proportion
of their local reserves in Government currency. This
arrangement would mean that the State Land Bank
would have, say, £1,100,000 to go into the mortgage-
business with, and this amount would cost it only 2 per
cent.—the present bank-note tax, which would, of course,
be lost —and the cost of maintenance. If the State chose
to keep its note-issue in the same dilapidated condition as
some of the private issues, the item of maintenance could
be kept down very low. And against this it would have
the profit, whatever it may be, that accrues through loss
of notes by fire, water, and all manner of accidents. The
State could conduct the paper-money business on a far
smaller gold-margin than the private banks, simply because
the reserve would be concentrated, and it would be a matter
of keeping one gold reserve and one margin, instead of a
dozen. Also, it could maintain it on a cheaper rate, be-
cause it would involve one printing and engraving opera-
tion instead of 12, or thereabouts. A State Land Bank,
with only the proceeds of its paper-issue for capital,
Wild lend money, so far as its capital went, at 4 per cent.,
md make a good profit out of the business.. When the
ST.S.W. and Victorian State Banks get afloat, if they ever
Jo, it would be worth while to consider the advisability of
Tunning the mortgage business on this basis.
* * * *
The A.M.P. Society advertises that it will lend
farge sums on mortgage at 4 per cent. Where does the
reconswindled bank, with its load of 4i per cent, deposits,
coine in under such conditions as these ? Also, if the
private borrower can get money in Australia at 4 percent.,
at what rate could Government loans be floated? And, if
rates like this are going to prevail in Australia, what is to
become of a certain little insurance-society which offers
to keep the amount of any death-claim in hand, if di-
rected, for an apparently indefinite number of years, and
pay the insured’s widow 5 per cent, interest on it, without
charging any extra premium for this big additional risk ?
The unfortunate Land Mortgage Bank of Vic-
toria is appealing to its British debenture-holders
( t1)00, 000 worth of them) for a reduction of interest from
L 4 per cent., and the debenture-holders are
‘ trunking about it.” Also, it makes a call ,of £1 per
share equal to £250,000 in all -on its miserable Victorian
shareholders. If the reduction of interest is conceded
the Land Mortgage misery should pull through and
become a dividend-paying concern again—some day. If
not, its prospects seem hardly worth mentioning. The
present value of shares is something less than nothing, or
thereabouts.
* • * *
The Guardian Accident and Guarantee Insur-
ance Co. (Melb.). which recently went into liquidation,
makes a call of 14s. 6d. per share for winding-up purposes.
The list of calls in Victoria these times stretches out
almost to the crack of doom.
* * * *
N.S.W. Government alleges that it is consider-
ing a scheme for the fire insurance of all State buildings
in a State insurance fund. This should mean a consider-
able saving, inasmuch as the average fire insurance
premium is about twice as much as the risk actually justi-
fies, the balance being absorbed in working expenses.
* * * *
South Melbourne municipal council is having a
riot over a bill for £6 for coach-hire for the, council when
it went to the funeral of an alderman’s deceased wife—
the said alderman being the man who moved that the bill
should be paid. Therefore, the ratepayers pay that alder-
man’s expenses for attending his own wife’s funeral.
Another row is over an account for £2 12s. for advertising
certain cookery classes “ formed under the auspices of
Lady Brassey ” —the S. Melb. council not only grovels to
Lady Brassey but even to her cookery class. It was
decided to charge both bills to the miserable taxpayer,
and the municipal auditor will pass them or else get the
sack. The beginning, middle, and end of an auditor’s
business is to pass things without making any beastly
fuss about them.
» * • *
According to a Vic. Supreme Court decision,
“ gift-money ” from one live person to another live person
(not being a legacy) is not “ income ” within the meaning
of the Income-tax Act, and is not therefore liable to
income-tax. A chance for an odd case or two : A. and B.
have each £lO,OOO in personalty. A. makes B. a “gift”
of his £lO,OOO ; B. does ditto to A. with hist £lO,OOO. Do
A. and B. thus escape income-tax each on £10,000?
* » * *
The pecuniary loss to the owners of ships lying
strike-bound at Newcastle—reckoning wages, interest on
capital, insurance, and harbor-dues —is set down at £2OOO
per day.
* * * *
The Newcastle coal-strike has entered on a new
and threatening phase. The men had almost resolved to
go back to work, and the owners were asked to state their
terms. These were practically a reduction on the rates
previously in force, with a proviso that the unions would
no longer be recognised. On this last vital clause the
men have decided to hold out at all risks. They point
out that it is only by union that they have gained decent
conditions of labor, that the union is the only thing which
stands between them and absolute tyranny. Which is
true. The pits remain closed, and the owners are not
importing other labor, as in the last great strike, but
appear content to wait the issue. The outlook is unpro-
mising all round.
* * * *
N.S.W. Govt, and B.H. Water Supply Ltd.
threaten to come into conflict. Three months ago Govt.
Analyst reported the water “ unfit for human consump-
tion,” but no serious steps were taken to improve the
quality. The officer again makes the same report, where-
upon the Minister has notified that unless effective
measures are adopted the statutory fine of £5O per day
will be collected—if it can be. Meantime, the water con-
tinues good enough for mines’ supply; and the po. worries
about little else, apparently. Its troubles about the town,
which uses the tap-water when it can’t get lead-polluted
rain-water to drink. Any town which hereafter permits a
money-grubbing foreign co. to get a monopoly of its
water-supply almost deserves to be poisoned by degrees.
* * * *
Little Jimmy Mirams still hangs on to the
assertion that Melb. Premier Permanent Building Asso.
was a healthy concern when he left it, and that the cele-
brated Murphy loans didn’t amount to “ playing ducks
and drakes with the money.” The world is too tired to
dispute Jimmy’s figures. Enough for the average critic
that the Murphy tribe drew half a million sterling, or
thereabouts, from the society, for building houses which
nobody could have been reasonably expected to live in.
* * * *
A recent interesting Companies Act decision is
that of Mr. Justice Romer to the effect that the Statute
of Limitations begins to run in favor of a co. against its
shareholders inre a div. from the date fixed for payment.
In reply to the contention that the Statute did not run at
all as against shareholders by reason of a trust, the Judge
said that a co. was not a trustee of a dividend for its
shareholders. All which renders Unclaimed Dividends
legislation more imperative than ever.
It certainly does surprise the world to hear that
Mr. Howell expects the historically-boomed White Rock
mine, lately acquired for a song, to pay divs. on the whole
capital of the big ore-smelting co. The ore improves with
sinking, and the many small veins in the surface quarry
are expected to merge into a great and rich central lode.
Assays from the main shaft now being sunk give 160 z.,
450 z., and 51oz. of silver respectively per ton of ore, in
place of the average of lOoz. to 12oz. per ton shown in the
millions of tons exposed in the surface-workings. The
development of the mine is now proceeding rapidly, and
it is intended to erect a plant capable of treating 1000 tons
of ore per day.
* * * *
White Rock, it will be remembered, was once
floated with a capital of £1,000,000. Many persons thought
that the galena stringers distinguishing the show might
one day turn into a defined lode in the deeper ground.
£lO,OOO would have proved it one way or another. Instead
of that, £70,000 was spent on plant to treat ore too poor
to pay.
• * * *
In preparation for a silver co. flotation boom,
lonv-abandoned mineral leases are being applied for all
round Broken Hill. More applications have been lodged
during the last two months than in all the time since the
collapse of the boom 7 years
The British concentrating plant got to work
last week. The Burnham syndicate (Danvers Power,
F.G.S., of Melb., is the Australian representative) will
have the furnaces in blast at the end of August.
* • * *
The B.H. Proprietary’s “pay ” for the week
ended June 12 was the heaviest on record.
* * * *
Prospecting westward in the B.H. North con-
tinues with'extremely disappointing results in hard
mineralised country. But in the ore body—what share-
holders would like to be able- to call the Eastern vein—
exposures of milling ore have lately been satisfactory.
With every damp day at Broken Hill there
comes the odor of burning Oregon from Block 11 of the
Proprietary. But the affected ground is well walled up,
and as immense quantities of sand have been washed in,
so that anything more than smouldering is out of the
Question, no uneasiness whatever is felt By the way, in
their recent circular the directors own up at last to the
probable loss of a quantity of valuable oxidised ore
through the fire. % „ ■
Block 14 is adding to its concentrating-mill on
the mine a plant for treating the zinc concentrates,
hitherto put aside “for future operation ; ” Mr, -Greenway s
plans Underground, this mine, which employs 460 men,
looks better than at any time since attention was given to
the sulphides. It is on the western branch of the lode
that the prospects are so much improved, though the ore
continues of low grade—a line for those who expect that,
because the western branch in the Junction is rich, it
must also be rich in the North. Why not, instead—As
the western branch in Block 14 is very poor, it must also
be poor in the North ?
* * * *
Broken Hill answers Westralia’s boast that, in
the first five months of the year, the fields produced
£320,702 worth of gold, by the approximation that in
April and May alone it produced £334,000 worth of silver
and lead—l,soo,OOOoz. of the former and 14,000 tons of the
latter.
♦ » » *
Sample of the unreliableness of the N.S.W.
Mines Dept.’s bulky annual report. On page 78 it is
stated that at Broken Hill 4297 miners won £2,719,946
worth of silver, lead, and ores. Page SI says that 4297
miners were working in the district for silver. But p. 14
says that all the silver, silver-lead, and ores won in the
whole province during 1895 was valued at £1,642,671. As
a matter of fact, the statement on p. 78 is slightly over a
million out. The table showing the annual export of
silver and lead is, by the way, notoriously wrong and mis-
leading : in some past years credit has been taken for
metal exported at Cockburn (from Broken Hill), and
again when re-exported at Sydney, whither it had been
shipped from Port Pirie.
• * * *
Opal-mining at White Cliffs (N.S.W.) looks up.
For 18 months past the sensitive gem-market has been
overstocked, but buyers have come about again lately.
Good stone has, of course, always had demand. Now,
also, an English co. has been formed to take over the two
chief properties—Turner & Richardson’s and the Wil-
cannia Co.’s. A higher tribute percentage for deep-won
gem has recently brought many windlasses on to the
field ; before, it was all shallow pit-prospecting.

petitions for a Govt, battery, on the terms proposed by
the Mining Conference and approved by the Minister.
* * * *
Eleanora, Hillgrove, N.S.W. : 436 tons, 5060 z.,
the record yield. Said that with sufficient water to treat
tailings as well as ore this mine can produce 600 oz. a fort-
night. Also, that the best rock is coming within a short
distance of the Garibaldi boundary. In other respects
the writer hears that this mine is looking much better—so
Bull may pull out, after all.
• * * *
Cable: “The Gallymont G.M. Co. (N.S.W.)
has been formed with a capital of £300,000, and is issuing
shares to the value of half that amount.” This is evi-
dently intended for the “Galley Swamp”—several old
leases taken over some months ago by the Anglo-Austra-
lian Exploration Co. of London. If so, the shock of such
a capital is alone enough to kill them.
* • m *
Another back-somersault by the Mt. Drysdale.
Two or three weeks ago it was announced that what was
thought to be a new pipe of rich ore had been struck. A
big yield was predicted, and rumors of a great quantity of
amalgam accumulating on the plates spread themselves
about. As the writer remarked, the public stood off —one
mine one boom in N.S.W., once bitten 100 times shy. The
total yield is 170 oz little more than from the low-
grade rock, and the poor, innocent S.M. Herald allows
it to appear that the great quantity of amalgam for so
little gold has puzzled the shareholders, and “ it is thought
that too much amalgam was allowed to accumulate on
the plates.” As a smart Yank said recently, one of the
most deplorable things in N.S.W. is the child-like ignor-
ance of the press in relation to that industry.

Some Melb. men have pooled the bulk of the
W.A. Goldfields Water Supply scrip, and anticipate turn-
ing the parcel over to a British shindykit for £75,000.
And—er —a few of the aforesaid crowd want a big div.

* * * *
Colin Templeton, St. Albans Wilson’s smart
right-hand man, was an original fifteen-pounder in George
Brookman’s Adelaide Prospecting Party that found the
Great Boulder, Ivanhoe, &c. So discrediting were various
expert reports on the properties that Templeton sold, for
£2OOO odd, an interest that to-day would stand him in
about £200,000. It is interesting to recall that Capt.
Oates was the only expert out of the early samplers who
had a good word for the shows. His summing-up:
“ Phenomenal returns should be produced.”
* * * »
Adelaide Exchange has 130 odd W.A. co’s on
its list. Paper capital, £7,000,000. A heavy slump would
paralyse W.A.
* * * *
Although the Ballarat-owned Morning Star, at
Mt. Magnet (W.A.), started the half with divs. it closed
the term £1389 in debt. However, late yields, vide
446 tons for 5080 z., have put the co. quite on its feet.
Six months milling : 3984 tons for 26190 z. 14dwt. ; divs.,
£3937 10s. Big yields are over, for the lode is roguing on
the co. When the Britishers spotted the formation’s
patchiness they “cried-off” their buying offer. Co.’s
wages-bill tots over £lOOO a month.
*.* * *
Bank of England shares at £7 or so are an
instance of the inflated values of Kalgoorlie scrip. The
mine is strongly suspected to be very patchy ; and until it
is better proved that fact ought to bar its shares being
worth more than a fraction of their present price.
* * * *
Friendship G.M. Co., Coolgardie, has been
registered ; capital, £85,000, of which £55,000 is issued
for public subscription. About three years since work
was started on this mine—situated in a very un-
savory neighborhood three miles S.E. of Coolgardie.
* * * *
Every night in the week, bar Sunday (which is
given up to drunks), the main street of Hannan’s is
crowded with scrip-dealers of every size and condition—
from the modest buyer of 100 in shilling stock, to the
man who bosses half-a-dozen shindy kits. Lately the
police have been specially engaged in keeping back the
crowds that surround the open calls.

» * * *
Burbank’s Birthday Gift, Coolgardie, was at
latest having another 500 tons put through at the Lon-
donderry. Seven shafts on the property; fair gold has
been got in most of them. None, however, are any depth.
If the mine can survive its proximity to Coolgardie, it
ought to be a future div.-payer.

A wire from Mt. Magnet, W.A., describes two
miners dollying 7460 z. gold from four whisky-cases of
stone. “ Save the Londonderry and the Wealth of
Nations the specimens from this whisky-case show ex-
celled everything—one of the most marvellous sights ever
seen in W.A. They have six more whisky-cases full of
stone.” What has become of the whisky ? Is it in the
wire ?
* * » *
Kalgoorlie, Friday.—“ Hill End West, at Broad
Arrow, has struck good gold in the shaft. An assay of
21b. of stone resulted in 12dwt. of gold, equal to the extra-
ordinary yield of 500 oz. to the ton.” This is published
under the heading—“ A yield of 500 oz. to the ton.” Thank
Heaven, the public are not fooled like newspapers !
* * * *
Coolgardie.—“ A rich lode, with abundance of
water, has been struck in the Hodgkinson mine. The
formation is similar to the Great Bouldf r.” Strange that
no formations like Bayley’s Reward are being struck now !
“ News has been received from Mount Magnet
that Kelly and party have struck an ironstone dyke
averaging 250 z. to the ton.” Most dykes are large, so
this should make a nice low-grade “ proposition.” The
boom is on right enough when such things appear in
print. Alas ! where are the great finds of a few months
ago ? What becomes of them, and where are all the tons
of gold they produce ?
“A trial crushing of 34 tons by a dry-blowing
machine at Mt. Charlotte (W.A.) yielded 240 z. The
machine was very slow, but otherwise was successful.”
Which is equivalent to saying that a mine would be a
success if it had more gold. In all ore-reducing machinery
time is the essence of the contract.
* * * *
Ivanhoe, at Hannan’s, the manager reports the
most important discovery ever made in the mine in the
eastern lode. Driven 7ft. without a sign of the hanging
wall, all good stone, and iSiri. of it, he says, will yield
10 to 15 oz. to the ton. The Bulletin hears that, under
the advice of a German expert, quite a number of German
princes and princesses have got into this mine.
* * * *
The secretary of the Boulder North West G.M.
Co. has sent a letter to Adelaide Stock Exchange intimat-
ing that it had been found that serious misrepresentations
had been made to the directors re that mine, also that
the ore in the mine contained no visible gold. A board-
meeting has been called to deal with the situation. Truth
is often sure but slow. It is remarkably slow with regard
to scores of other W.A. mines against which the same
indictment might be made.
• * * *
New Normanby, Ballarat, barren of divs. last
half, but prospects are cheerful. Clean-up shows 1800 oz.
6dwt. from 6440 tons - a heavy drop from the previous
term’s 22120 z. 13dwt. from 6512 tons. Co. paying ex’s
and has .1200 with which to shaft-sink the other 100 ft.
to get below the improved body of stone making
strongly underfoot from the 1000 ft. level.
* » * *
Hope, .Wood’s Point (Vic.), month’s stamping,
550 tons for 3630 z. 14dwt.; 6th 6d. div. Good enough for
the old-timer. Co. increasing its battery to 20-head to
thump bigger divs. out of the several years’ stone in sight.
* * * *
Golden Gate,Ballarat, which has been “coming”
many times the last eight years, now seems to be really
on the verge of doing something for call-payers. Working
a flat make of stone proved payable 60ft. wide, and shortly
expects to cut the two shoots of good stone tackled at
shallower levels. Six months record, 537 tons for 151 oz.
lOdwt.; average, 6dwt. Would pay if the battery could
be kept going full-time.
% * * *
North Duke, Maryborough (Vic.) has flopped
badly the last half: 7874 fathoms wash-dirt yielded
41780 z. Bdwt —average, lOdwt. 14Jgr. per fathom, as
against 18dwt. 12gr. the previous term. And the div.
slump—£l3,7so to a paltry £1250 ! wherefore scrippers
who got in on the strength of the directors’ boast that
“ the mine for the next half-year will yield a handsome
return,” must feel nasty. The North, with £985 in hand
and the mine just paying its way, doesn’t look to have
too healthy a future, and the crowd, to avert a crisis, are
fixing a combine with the youthful Duke United, and
intend to gradually dump the pair on J. Bull. A very
celebrated boomer, whose wife is heavily interested in
the Duke Estate, the royalty grabber, is manipulating the
deal.
* * * *
Chalk’s Freehold, Carisbrook (Vic.), has divd.
£23,750 on £44,500 called up. Gold won, £186,270, the
biggest “ cuts ” £80,354 to contract labor and £57,400 to
wages, and royalty £B4OO. Past half has given £lB,OOO
from gold, panning ss. 9d. per share, and leaving £782 to
the good. Chalk’s is making divs. from a very low
quality of wash-dirt, but by the way the stock is droop-
ing the crowd inside doesn’t think too much of the chance
of keeping divs. up.

Bendigo millionaire Lansell’s famous 180 has

* * * *
According to engineer Thwaites, of Melb. Board
of Works, over £20,000 worth of produce was taken off
Werribee sewage-farm last year, although not a ton of
sewage has yet reached it. Next year he reckons it will
double that sum, equal to 7500 tons, and when the works
are complete it will easily raise 15,000 tons of produce an-
nually, lowest value £BO,OOO yearly. Reads like a mining-
report.

V * * »
The new issue of 60,000 contributing shares in
the North Mount Lyell Mining Co. (No Liability) was
applied for twice over, so the float may be set down
as a decided success. Shareholders received 100 paid-
up shares in the new co. for each share held in the
old one, and up to the 17th they had priority of applica-
tion for the contributing shares. Very few, if any, of the
new shares reached the hands of the general public.
* * * *
How to float an “ extended ” wild cat, as dis-
closed by a certain Tas. mining syndicate. The property
was formerly held and abandoned by another co. The
present promoters reprinted on their prospectus that co.’s
reports, said reports being, of course, favorable. The
responsibility is shifted further back by the disclosure that
the report in question was pirated from a third co., which
held the ground in the dark ages. It also transpires
that the present co. doesn’t hold a quarter of the second
co.’s territory.
* * » *
It will interest a lot of people to read that “ Mr.
T. Caradoc Kelly, the well known New Guinea explorer,”
is now a temporary “ lion ” in London, where he figures
as a coach “ to teach all the ins-and-outs of gold-mining,
&c.” “ Etcetera ”is good. He has already, it seems, been
appointed bear-leader to the sons of several notable per-
sons, and takes them with him to Auckland, M.L., via
Coolgardie. “T. Caradoc Kelly” The Bulletin pre-
sumes to be identical with the gentleman who, years ago,
had so heated ah argument with some stubborn Vic.
miners over oertain alleged samples of New Guinea gold.
* * * *
It is often easier to get into W.A. than out
of it. The Absconding Debtors’ Act is often arbitrarily
brought into play at Albany just as a man is stepping
aboard. De Rose, one of the Great Boulder crowd, was
one of the last victims. A big growl concerning the abuse
of this Act is imminent.

N.S.W. Vice-Royalty having had play-
actors to dinner, Gimletdom is mourning—“ What-
ever is Eigureheadedness a-coming to? We
thought Lord ’Ampton was a ‘ God-fearing ’ man,
like Mr. Want, and his other Ministers ; we have
seen him presiding over gatherings ‘ for the King’s
service; ’ but, oh ! the snares that the Enemy sets
for the Exalted Horn ! ” There has been talk of
a deputation of the Salt of the Earth to point out
to “Brother ’Ampden” the pernicious example he
is setting to the tender and innocent and imitative
Lambs of the Fold, and to then and there pray
with him, for him, and at him. “Let us pluck
this Brand from the burning,” says one inspired
wag.
* * * »
Gov. Brassey was made an Oddfellow the
other day. It was a great occasion with much
frill and he-millinery about it, and the people who
made Brassey an Oddfellow were :
G.M. Header, acting as N.G. of the lodge, supported by
P.G. Tucker, of the Earl of Hopetoun Lodge, and P.G.
A. M. Pool. The V.G. was Deputy P.G.M. J. Sternberg,
M.L.C., Bendigo ; and his supporters were P.G.M. 0. H.
Bayley, and P.G.M. E. J. Dixon. The Grand Master act-
ing on this occasion was P.G.M. G. H. Hobson, supported
by P.G.M. Geo. Turner, the Premier, and P.G.M. E. L.

* * * #
Gov. Brassey’s recent lecture on Imperial
Eunuchty was a miserable fiasco in point of
interest. Not ten people in the whole assemblage
were able to follow the statistics which “ Uncle
Tom ” ladled out so lavishly ; for the speech was
little else but a string of figures showing that
Britain exported so many tons of sausage-casings
in 1863, while at the present day an increase was
observable of, &c., &c. In fact, the whole lecture
was practically a resume of W hitaker’s Almanac
for 1895, filling 40 pp. type-written matter, which
His Ex. read out slowly and with difficulty. Yet
the “loyal” crowd, which filled the hall and
bulged out on the other side, yelled vociferously
every time His Ex. stopped at a hard word, and
the Argus was loud in laudation the following
day. In the Argus’s view, Gov. Brassey is a sort
of standing rebuke to atheists.
* * * *
Gov. Brassey is growing more and more
unpunctual. He is usually 20 minutes late at the
theatre on a V.R. night, and at the recent St.
Vincent Hospital Ball (advertised for 8.30) the
first programmed dance did not start until an
hour-and-a-half later owing to the vice-regal
party’s delay.
* * * *
An ingenious representative of vice-royalty
thought lately that it would be a graceful recogni-
tion of her obligations if she adopted a young
aboriginal girl, as she had been told Governoress
Duff once did. Accordingly half-a-dozen young
aboriginals were rounded up and presented for
inspection. “ I wanted an aboriginal—a black
girl,” exclaimed the lady, after a survey. “ Ye-e-s,
your ladyship,” the tame official replied, “ but the
aboriginals on our mission station are all like
these.” “But these girls are white.” “Nearly
white, your ladyship: but they’re the only
aboriginals we have. One that was all black we
were keeping for a curiosity, but she died.”

Jusfcice-MinisterGould’s statement, inreply
to a Parliamentary question, that no N.S.W.
divorce-cases are ever heard in camera except when
“ respect to public decency or morality ’’ made
such a course desirable, is mere impudent shuffle.
Why should it be always “in the interests of
public decency and morality ” to suppress all
mention of the divorce-cases of powerful poli-
ticians ?
* • * *
The harricterial mnlentinn fhnf Ar,l„
, J n f Ddmscerlal contention that only such
delicate cases as nullity-of-marnage suits are heard
in camera in N.S.W. divorce-court, is most mislead-
mg. Several “society” and political divorce-
cases have been heard in absolute privacy during
late years, even though not technically in
camera.” The court, in these cases, sat either
before or after ordinary hours, and a sharp look-

should be on the premises. Also, the names m
these special cases were kept right out ot the p
lished lists. Wasn’t that practically in camera .

Hell is the place we go to when we don t
die. Everyone goes to hell who lives long enough.
A quaint dog and law item comes
from Melbourne. There were two
cousins, and one of them had his dog
stolen. Shortly after he met the second
cousin with a dog which seemed to be
the same dog to a hair, and promptly
called him a thief, whereupon he re-
ceived a “ woodener ”in the eye. I hen
the man with the assaulted eye laid an
information, and his assailant was ar-
rested in the morgue, where he was in the
act of identifying his sister, whose dead
body had just been recovered from the
Yarra. The case came into court, where
prosecutor with-
drew his charge out
of sympathy for
defendant’s be-
reavement ; and
Panton, P.M., com-
plimented him on
his kindly con-
duct, and said nice
fatherly things
about harmony m
families ; and de-
fendant asked if he
couldn’t have his
sympathetic rela-
tive arrested there
and then for per-
jury, and refused to
be harmonious on
any terms. Just
here a police-ser-
geant persuaded
him to leave rapid-
ly. What became
of the dog doesn’t
appear, but The
Bulletin is pre-
pared to swear that
if the cousin with
the dead s ister
does prosecute the cousin with the lost dog for
perjury, and the case comes before Mr. Panton, he
will receive small consideration. When a magis-
trate has just made some really beautiful remarks
about peace and harmony, and thereupon one of
the allegedly harmonious parties demands the
arrest of the other one, it makes the beak look
so beastly incongruous that he doesn’t forget it
for a long time.

prisons is an office created at the instigation of
the late Parkes, and was, in one of his numerous
dark days, offered to him as a steady billet by the
rulers of the hour.

have their names and addresses printed on their
carts. South Melb. Bench deliberately repeals
the law in the case of “ well-known ” and respect-
able defendants. As usual.

One of the pleasantest, coolest, most
central and best-appointed restaurants in Sydney
is the Manhattan Dining Room, situated in the
basement of the great Equitable Building in
George-street, Sydney. It is in the middle of the
busiest portion of Sydney, and is in every respect
a high-class establishment with good cooking, good
attendance, a lavish bill of fare, and luxurious
fittings and furnishings. #

ranked as “ Mr.,” while another section remains
severely unornamented Thus we have “ Mr.’’
F. E. Rogers and “Mr.” C. G. Wade, whereas
shorthand writers, professional witnesses and
others appear baldly as “ F. W. Curnow,” “ T. D.
El well,” and so forth. The point is only of value
as a hint to the Public Service Board, showing
what a lot of time some official barnacles must
waste in inventing and maintaining such micro-
scopic distinctions.
» * * *
A noteworthy incident in the Public Ser-
vice Board alterations in the N.S.W. Justice
Department is the appointment of a layman,
Mr. G. Miller, late Comptroller-General of N.S.W.
Prisons, to succeed that astute lawyer, Mr. A. C.
Fraser, as Under-Secretary. Mr. G. Miller is the
luckiest official in N.S.W. Civil Service. Why a
man who has had no connection with law in all
his life should be appointed secretary for Justice,
is as big a mystery as why Mr. Miller, after spend-
ing all his official life as a clerk in the Education
Department, was suddenly appointed Comptroller-
General of Prisons. However, possibly the Board,
in kicking the Union Club man upstairs, had in
view the benefit which would accrue to the Prisons
Department in being placed under that intelligent,
energetic and humane disciplinarian, Capt. Neiten-
stein, who has made so great a mark for himself,
and has long been so poorly paid, as chief of the
training-ship Sobraon.
Since, however, the under-secretary is the per-
manent head of the Justice Department, The
Bulletin protests vigorously against Comptroller
Miller, who, as known in his official capacity, is
only less bloodless than Minister Gould, being
thrust into the billet. Miller is the departmental
apologist for the gag, the lash, and the solitary
cell, the man who ordered “ white spirit mixed
with a nauseous drug ” to be served to sick
prisoners instead of brandy, and censured a gaol-
doctor for allowing spirits to strengthen the
stomachs of two prisoners who attended on a
patient in the gaol-hospital suffering from a par-
ticularly loathsome disease.
There is a certain harmony in bringing such a
man into direct departmental contact with Minis-
ter Gould —the harmony of two frogs croaking
under your window; but the man not content
with one frog at a time is extravagant and a
glutton. Comptroller Miller should be removed
from the Justice Department altogether. His re-
ports show that he has no conception of the prin-
ciples of the modern science of crime, and he
appears from those reports to be a man of small
and obstinate intellect, incapable of being taught
his own ignorance.
* * * *
At Balmain (Sydney) Court, the other day,
two young gentlemen employed at the local abat-
toirs were fined £2 each “ for throwing bullocks -
livers at people passing—a practice so common as
to be an intolerable nuisance.” Liver-throwing
is the first stage in the public history of these
young men. Shortly, no doubt, we shall hear 01
them as footballers.
* * * *
All Melb. cable-tram cars and dummies
are now fitted with hinged seats for the conductor
and gripman, so that on stretches of several blocks
both have decent opportunities of resting then
limbs, and thus avoiding congestion of the blood-
vessels in the legs, varicose veins, rheumatics, and
so on. The Bulletin presumes that this heralds
another reduction in wages. The tram co. has
given “ nix for nil ” so far.
The Sydney Chinese paper is in future to
be printed from type, a “ fount ’’—probably the
first used in Australia-havmg bee n iniportecl for
the purpose. It contains between 8000 and JOUU
oharacters, and 30 pairs of ordinary printers cases
are used to hold it.
A SKETCH BY “HOP’S” UNDERSTUDY.
' l Locking, tpeDaßumc*
The well-known solidity of first attempts
at baking dampers is proverbial. Some kangaroo-
shooters lately engaged a cook of the “boiling
water without burning it ” type. His first and
second efforts were simply thrown out, except a
part which was used for bullets. On the second
day he tackled a third damper, and, although the
dough persisted in sticking to his fingers, he
managed at last to get it into the ashes, and,
greatly exhausted, went outside for a rest. After
a while he noticed some birds come down and
pick at the remains of the first damper, and,
though they struggled hard, they could not get
their beaks out again and were easily caught.
The party used the insides of the other dampers
for bird-traps.
* * * *
The Argus has had luck. In the same
issue as that in which it hisses at Sangster for
making those sensational charges re the alleged
unseaworthiness of certain ships, particularly the
Wendouree, it is compelled to report the accident
on the latter vessel—the bursting of a boiler-tube
by which two men were boiled to death. Sang-
ster was pretty severe on the boilers of the Wen-
douree. Is this a strange chance or a terrible vin-
dication ? The Argus hastens with the assurance
that the explosion is no proof of the inefficiency of
the boiler. . Probably not—most likely it burst
with sheer indignation.
¥ * * *
The inevitable double event: Two Melb.
suburban railway station-masters just killed, by
trains, within a week. None killed before.
* * * *
Probably “Australian Natives’ Day” in
Melbourne will not long survive Chief-Sec. Pea-
cock’s order to suppress the sale of art-union
tickets. The A.N.A. annual fete has been run
solely on the gambling racket. A soft-headed
public bought shilling tickets for the ffite mainly
because each bit of paper represented a fraction
of a pen’orth of chance to win £ 1000. The exact
size of the fraction aforesaid can’t be accurately
stated, seeing that the number of art-union tickets
issued is wrapped in mystery. Neither does any-
one appear to know whether the drawing of prizes
provided for the large number of chances that
must have been left on hand unsold.

A Victorian spinster postmistress is in
trouble over alleged embezzlements, amounting to
several hundred pounds. Cherchez le masheur.
* * * *
A charitable movement is afoot amongst
the Chinese merchants of Sydney to send about
100 aged and decrepit Chows to China.
* * * *
The apartments of an absconder now very
much wanted by the police were decorated with
scores of photographs. But when the detectives
came to ransack the photographic gallery and to
rummage into all the belongings, not one solitary
photo of the Levanter himself, at any age what-
ever, could they find. Yet Levanter was a vain
man—just one who might be expected to have
photos, of himself taken in all sorts of attitudes.
There is one photo, extant of the gentleman —but
that is another story.
* * * *
From the report of Glebe branch of Sydney
City Mission, recently wept over by Gov. Hamp-
den :—
326 persons had been relieved and 1003 prayed with ; 45
had received medical aid, and 896 had received tracts.
The Glebe missioners are thus more than three
times as liberal with “flopping” as with bread ;
and nearly 20 times as generous with tracts as
with physic.
* * * *
At Ballarat (Vic.), a week or two ago, one
Mary Downes was tried for attempted suicide and
acquitted on the ground that she was insane.
Next day she was tried for arson, and was con-
victed on the ground that she wasn’t insane, and
got four years’ “ hard.” And now the Justice De-
partment has taken to gibbering to itself as it
walks, in its efforts to decide what it should do
with Mary Downes.

A blush betokens innocence—at a former
period.
x * * * *
Dear Bulletin, — Why should a man in a
crowded railway-carriage always give up his
seat to oblige a lady, who, the chances are,
feels much less tired than he ? Because
he is the slave of a foolish social cus-
tom founded upon bogus reverence
for the weaker sex. The other day
I got into a compartment full
of vulgar holiday-makers, and
had to stand until a cheer-
ful larrikiness said, “ ’Ere,
you take niy seat, mister ;
I’ve been doin’ a lot of
sittin’ all day, I ’ave.” It
was a strain upon my moral
courage, but I took that
seat, I did. The girl seemed
happy to do a bit of stand-
ing, and her bloke, who
remained seated as before,
said, “My colonial! Liz.,
there ain’t any blanky
nonsense about ’im.” The
bloke’s remark was a well-
deserved compliment, I
guess.—Weary.

A Melbourne master -
baker admitted in Haw-
thorn Court, last week,
that he had paid a “second
hand ” 3s. for sixteen
hours’ work, and summarily
sacked him for refusing to
work nineteen hours at a
stretch. Ye gods ! no won-
der the consumers find
fault with their daily bread. A loaf manu-
factured in a baker’s nineteenth consecutive
hour of toil (at 2|d. per hour) is likely to be a
ghastly failure.

lately hired a certain powerful tug-boat to act
temporarily as a ferry steamer have now in hand
quite a number of lawyers’ letters claiming
damages for having shaken women’s teeth out of
their heads.

from hotels,” say the purists. How very true —
out-door work, especially.

Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently,
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted , and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.

Aloe : But then Queensland tick-fever is pretty certainly
not due to the tick itself, but to a disease-germ carried
by the tick, just as fluke is not due to water-snails, but to
embryo flukes carried by them C.B.A. : The reason
the Judge awarded “ twelve months and one week’s im-
prisonment ” is that prisoners serving sentences under, or
up to, 12 months are not given any “ mitigation ” of their
ordered periods for “good conduct.” Good conduct
counts for mitigation in the case of any prisoner getting
over 12 months. Thus, the 12-months-and-a-week man
will probably work off, by “ good conduct,” one month,
thus serving only 11 months 1 week. Had he got 12
months bare he would have to serve the full period—
unless he was “ well-connected.” (2) An 18-months’
prisoner gets 3 months ordinary “mitigation”; a 2-
years’ man, 4 months’; a 3-years’ man, 6 months’ ; and so
on, in regulated proportions, until a 20-years’ man gets
5 years’ “mitigation.” Anastasia: Let us give the

A man arrested for wife-desertion at Hay,
when lodged in gaol, said he would sooner be
hanged than return to Hay. “ Hay and Hell and
Booligal. ”
Time deals kindly with the woman who
uses “ Babeskin ” soap. It softly removes the tell-
tale lines of the years, and renews the delicate
bloom of youth. One shilling packages of two
cakes. All chemists.*
There are signs—especially in its ‘ ‘ corres-
pondence” columns (for every newspaper “re-
ceives ” just those sorts of letters which it wishes
to receive) —that Sydney D.T. is “aspecting” its
alleged views so that it may readily and excusably
turn and rend the Reid Govt, when opportunity
and “commercial principles ” dictate such a course.
The rats, in fact, are preparing to leave. Masterly
feat! to be at one and the same time an “ official ”
and an “ independent ” organ.
Messrs. J. Ludowici and Son, Ltd. wish to
notify the Australian public that the dimensions
of their stock and the forethought of their arrange-
ments will enable them to keep the prices in
leather down for at least three more months.
Buyers should take advantage and communicate
with the firm at 162 Clarence-st., Sydney. *
Sydney D.T’s. interesting London corres-
pondent hears from Johannesburg that the
Jameson raid came to grief mainly because the
conspiracy was a whisky and water affair—a John
Jameson affair, in fact. Want of food and too
much grog did the trick. The expedition some-
how lost the run of the food-depots it had elabor-
ately arranged; nevertheless it managed to pick
up some vile Boer grog on the very day the
burghers potted Jameson’s men from behind
rocks.
When General Hutton first came to N.S. W.
he straightway announced that he intended to get
rid of certain locally-appointed military officers
and import Imperial ones to fill their places—
which autocratic announcement was speedily re-
sented by his official superior, Premier Dibbs, who
refused to allow him to dismiss (among others)
Colonels Spalding and Airey. But General
Hutton now has his way; the two officers named
have got notice of retirement—at the instance, it
appears, of “ the Colonial Defence Committee” in
London, to whom Gen. Hutton represented things
on arriving in England, and whose recommenda-
tion, endorsed by the new Commandant, has been
acted on by Premier Reid. That the officering of
the N.S. W. forces should thus be manipulated by
the Imperial authorities is, from an Australian
Democratic point of view, most grotesque.
The N.S.W. Railway Commissioners have
stoutly declined to abolish the preferential rates
of freight allowed to Eskbank Iron Works, even
though a . large deputation of business men
representative of the Sydney Chamber of Com-
merce, pleaded that the importing “iron mer-
chants of Sydney were being commercially cruci-
fied by the concessions granted to Eskbank The
railway commissioners pointed out that an in-
dustry upon which a large number of persons
depended, had been a direct result of the pre-
ferential rates ; also that the big traffic caused
therefrom really paid the railway department.
Which is a significant contribution to the protec-
tionism-m-practice question, delivered by a board
of commercial principles,” men who happen to
be statutorily independent of the present foreign-
trade, cheap jack importing government.

Sydney, .Tune 23, 1896.
My Dear Moorabinda—
Not so much the music as the audience was
relied on to make a success of the first concert of
the fifth season of the S.A.O.S. at Sydney Town
Hall. _ The last people heard of the society was.
that it was falling through with a hollow sound
because of the shyness of the subscription-list and
the stern fact that Sydney hadn’t enough orches-
tra in its soul to run a hurdy-gurdy with.
Therefore, when the society announced a fresh
start people were more greatly concerned to see
how the house filled than whether Hazon’s giftec
band was going to improve on its old form or
not. Considering the size of the Town Hall, the
attendance was realty very heartening, anc
included a girl in pale-pink from head to toe, who
looked like a peach-blossom ; and a stout lady in
sort of steel cuirass, which looked as if she hac
trimmed herself with chain pot-cleaners. The
programme, under Hazon’s electric baton, went
off in great style, the clever orchestralists being
diversified by Miss Milly Wynne, who made her
first appearance with the society and wore white
satin, subsequently brightened with many handed-
up bouquets. Her numbers, by Verdi and Gomez,
proved her to be the possessor of a showy high
soprano voice united to a good platform style, anc
a personality which reminded you of poor Violet
V arley.
Apropos singers Miss Nagel, the unseen
warbler of “Ben Bolt” in “Trilby,” has a rich,
round, somewhat baritoney contralto—the sort of
voice that seems lonely without whiskers, I always
think—but she must get nerves to match it, or
Australia won’t have in her another Crossley to
spring on a waiting world—unless the fashion
comes in of singing behind fire-screens. Miss
Nagel’s nervousness is inexpressibly refreshing in
an age when girls are pushing in the front row
from infancy upwards, but it is likely to stand in
her way all the same. I hear that several attempts
were made during the Sydney season of “Trilby ”
to lure her on to the concert-platform, but with no
avail—the damsel was willing, but her knees were
weak. Nervousness carried to those lengths is
generally as great a drawback to public success as
no voice at all, but I hope Miss Nagel, with her
lion’s voice and mouse’s courage, will get over her
stage panic in time to make a great name for
herself.
I was really sorry to miss the Royal Society’s
reception, last Thursday, for these attractive
functions only occur, as it were, once in every blue
moon, and Thursday’s function was made memor-
able by Professor Threlfall’s Rontgen-ray demon-
stration, which seems to have absolutely fascinated
the numerous laymen—a great crowd of doctors
attended —by whom it was witnessed.
The New Religion in Sydney ominously begins
in smoke. The celibate “ priest ”of St. James’s,
Sydney, where part of Govt. House worship goes
up, gave a big smoke-concert in the church nail
the other evening to the men of his flock, who all
brought their pipes by special invitation. The
object is to bring the wandering males, as it were,
of the flock round the shepherd, who is apparently
getting tired of the ring-fence of petticoats by
which he has found himself enclosed up to date.
Hence the new movement in High Church circles
to smoke the women out of at least one phase of
Kerristian fellowship. The Incumbent of St.
James’s definitely admitted the plague of women
in the churches when, in a speech during the
smoke-concert aforesaid, he told how he “once
knew a vicar whom somebody asked if he knew
much of the fathers (meaning the early fathers).
‘No,’ he replied, ‘but I see a good deal of the
mothers and daughters.’” Which strikes me as
being a very ungrateful remark for any parson to
make—unless he was riled at the fit of his last pair
of woolwork-slippers.
I wonder how women like the struggles of their
pet parsons to be free from their galling yoke?
When a woman finds her tame curate, warranted
to feed from the hand, suddenly barring her out
and spending whole evenings with men and
tobacco, she must feel as if ner pet kitten had
suddenly found voice and sworn at her. But, for
my part, I think the move only fair to the men.
Hitherto churches have been made interesting
solely to women. The show of millinery, for ex-
ample, and the celibate curate who pays visits and
drinks tea, only appeal to the feminine community
—but the new regime of “ a pipe with the parson ”
on certain evenings, and a monthly smoke-concert,
will round up the sceptical male while the novelty
lasts, anyhow. And when that wears off lots of
new “wheezes” for keeping the men up to the
mark will present themselves to a mettlesome
high churchman who knows just how to dish the
devil with lots of sacerdotal sauce to deaden the
flavor. Choir-girls, par exemple, are a compara-
tively unworked field in Sydney, regarded as a
means of bringing young men to church on Sun-
days. The present system is crude and ill-digested
—precious material boxed up anyhow, wearing
unbecoming clothes when it ought to be carefully
stage-managed in angelic but tricky night-gowns-
cwm-surplices.
Everybody’s thoughts seem to be on tobacco
just now. There has lately been a newspaper-war
as to whether smoking should be allowed in Sydney
Botanic Gardens. Certainly, as they stand, our
Gardens, where nobody (but nurse-maids) ever goes,
are social wildernesses. You have to poke about
to discover the whereabouts of your five o’clock
cup of tea, and when you get it you drink it fac-
ing a blank wall, and wander out again feeling
like a wet Sunday afternoon, although the Gardens
themselves are beautiful always. Of course T
know all this has nothing to do with smoking
there—but then I don’t care a row of pins about
the smoke question—l only want to point out
that the Gardens entertain you so sadly that some
novelty ought to be tried there. The women’s
grievance about the Gardens is the absence of a
smartly-managed tea-room with a wide verandah
studded with little tables, whence one could com-
mand a spirited peep of the harbor.
Gov. and Lady Hampden and party visited
Sydney Zoo the other day and were speechless
with rapture at the tout ensemble. But, to my
mind, the Zoo must be a mixed pleasure to any-
body really fond of animals. The goaded elephant
loaded with brats ; the camel in a 6 x 4 enclosure ;
the cassowary stalking up and down a narrow bit
of sand; the gaunt, wild beasts in barred cages
roaring for meat; and, lastly, the strings of
broken-down horses who have slaved to the last
gasp being led in the back way to be slaughtered
to feed the howling mob of captives. I suppose,
though, we must have a Zoo so as to let the State
school-children have ocular demonstration that the
leopard doesn’t change his spots.
Government House appears to have broken out
in a fresh place over its “ at homes ” this season.
The new caper appears to be to hold what were
usually afternoon receptions by gaslight, and to
allow a large suburban majority in off the grass,
in a manner of speaking, for the first time. This
will make Guvmentouse wildly popular, and is
the sort of thing that leads to her Ex. becoming a
“sister” to a number of unknown people. The
garden-reception racket was played out during the
Duff period, when many truly loyal subjects never
saw the pattern of the V.R. carpet; and though it
(I mean the garden-reception) was cheap (the
crowd generally walked to and from it), it was
more or less nasty. Receptions by candlelight
changed all that. There is something far more
serious to the average would-be society female in
paying your respects to vice-royalty in evening
dress backed up by a stiffly-starched husband and
a hansom cab than doing it alone by daylight in
your Sunday frock and making the pilgrimage
“per boot”—though everybody, of course, isn’t
glad of the innovation. The old, now-disrupted
Govt. House clique hurries through the motley
assemblage with its nose in the air, so I hear.
The thing Gov. Hampden feels most profoundly
grateful for in Australia up to date is a shepherd’s
bluey. On a recent shooting-expedition after
bush turkeys, the roads through 20,000 or 30,000
acre paddocks (the size of these amazed H.E.)
got too heavy for expeditious travelling, and,
darkness setting in, the guns were glad to seek
shelter in a shepherd’s hut. “ I will never forget,”
says Gov. H., “the pleasure I experienced when
my kind host found me a blanket to cover myself
with. ” After all, how close we all are at times to
the naked aborigine who yells “Gib it blanket.”
Put a Governor in the middle of a 10,000-acre
paddock without his eider-down quilt, and the
same cry wells up naturally and immediately from
the bottom of his soul.
Dot Boucicault’s farewell to Australia and sever-
ance with the B. & B. co. was a crowded-out
festival at Sydney Criterion last Saturday, adorned
with speeches, flowers (there were lots of flowers
for Mrs. Brough, and one wreath of laurel fell for
D. 8.), a purse of money, and an illuminated ad-
dress. To my idea the audience should have come
in crape, with black-bordered handkerchiefs, and
a leading undertaker should have been hired to
come on and hand the testimonial in a coffin
while the orchestra played the Dead March. For
the departure of the gifted Dot hits Australia
right over the heart. Not only does his going rob
the local stage of the most versatile actor and most
accomplished dramatic tutor it has known, but it
also puts an end to that most useful abbreviation,
“The B. & B. Co.” Nobody would have the
hardihood to write about “ the B. comedy co.” in
the land of the great adjective.
By the way, the Brough dramatic co. will now
(for the first time in its history) visit North
Queensland —hitherto it has never travelled north
of Brisbane. North Queensland —the co. goes as
far as Townsville and Charters Towers—has now
ahead of it the biggest treat it has ever experi-
enced. I advise every woman of brains and
artistic aspirations to save up her money and re-
serve a front seat for the entire season.
That much-adored actor, the bright-eyed Wallace
Brownlow, has left the stage, but not Australia ;
the destination of the accomplished baritone,
whom wild horses couldn’t drag to a tea-party, is
variously stated. His opera clothes, at least, w 1
be hard to fill. That is the worst of engaging a
large, athletic youth for parts usually tilled
small and curly lady-killers. The next man in
always looks as if papa s pants would ne\ er
Johnny, in a manner of speaking.
Quite a flight of fixtures is billed for cold July,
though the Tempe ball occurs on 30th June (next
Tuesday) at Sydney Town Hall and end® t _is
month up with festive honors. On 3rd •July tne
Sheep Show is to be opened at Sydney Agricul-
tural Ground by Gov. Hampden, on which occa-
sion ladies will be admitted free to gaze on the
erratic back-bone of the country and contemplate
mutton-chops in their wild state. The feature ol
the Sheep Show, from a public point of view, is,
of course, the sheep-dog trials. On 15th July
Miss O’Shaughnessy’s complimentary benefit con-
cert takes place in the Town Hall with a good list
of patrons, and on the same evening the Steam-
ship Co.’s officials give their annual ball at Pad-
dington Town Hall. On July 7, Mrs. Harvey,
gives a charity-conversazione and concert at Needs
rooms, and the Freemasons’ ball —the only public
ball now arranged by men —comes off in Sydney
Town Hall on 29th July in aid of the poor of
Sydney.
More fixtures for July: On 3rd a dance will be
held in Stratton’s Hall, Waverley, in aid of the
local poor. On the 14th the Fete Nationale
Francaise comes off at Correy’s gardens, Cabarita,
and a seasoned committee have the affair well in
hand. For the 20th H. Ex. has accepted the invi-
tation of the Institute of Bankers of N.S.W. and
the Australian Economic Association to a conver-
sazione, which will probably be one of the most
brilliant entertainments of the season.
The Sydney Nice People’s Club had a business
meeting (kept very quiet, for reasons) in Tatter-
sail’s Chambers, Pitt-street, on Friday last. To
escape the remarks of a vulgar and critical press
the nicest of the nice had to lift their silken skirts
and march up four flights of stairs to the hall
which is used for religious purposes and has
“Stewards of Truth” nailed up on the door.
Great pains were taken to keep the press out, but
no reporters appeared. The genial Sydney Bur-
dekin, framer of the imperishable phrase defining
the objects of the club, was not present. The
matter of renting a club house in the city was gone
into, and the large and smileful Hon. Rupert
Carington filled most of the chair.
From the composer I have received “Dorcas, a
Stately Dance,” by Percy Kehoe, and dedicated
to Florence Bland Holt, a very faithful, un-
flattered likeness of whom literally adorns the
cover. The “Stately Dance”" is quaintly con-
ceived and aptly named after the Quakeress in
the “Prodigal Daughter,” one of Mrs. Holt’s
prettiest parts. “ The Carbine Polka ” is also to
hand, Eric Johnson perpetrator. Words fail in
the teeth of a composition like this. “ The ’Bus-
horse Gallop” would be a fitter name, judging by
the quality and bump of the thing.
Writes my Melb. correspondent:—
“The discreet Wallington (he still carries his
damaged arm in a sling) is to get =£3ooo from Lord
Brassey as compensation, with which, unless his
friend Lord Hopetoun gets him a billet in Eng-
land, he will probably go to Westralia—that is, if
he does not get back to Melb. Club. It was not so
much that the captain fell out with the aides, as
some people have said, but that he and Lord
Brassey soon came to a difference of opinion about
what a Governor ought to do—His Ex. inclining
to the free and easy, and the private-sec. to the
conventional. And, not unnaturally, the lord
who pays the piper preferred to get a new
musician.
“ The Brassey’s are causing much trouble to in-
tending entertainers by withholding the date of
their first ball. One Mayor is tearing his hair, for
all the invitations for the S. Melb. Mayoral ball
are printed, and now he is roundaboutly told that
the hop at the stucco mansion will be held on the
same night.
“ Austral Salonites fell down and worshipped,
the other day, when Mrs. Brown Potter and
escort Bellew descended upon the Sanctuary of|the
Block, and for a whole afternoon shed their radi-
ance over the tea-tups. Mrs. Potter wore a won-
derful lemon-colored garment under a brown-
plush cloak, which she hugged round her conspira-
tor-wise or flung back in a heap when she remem-
bered the claims of the aforesaid bilious robe to
admiration. Mr. Bellew, whose curls nowadays
are powdered with silver, protested in a gushing
speech that Salonites could not be half so glad to
welcome him as he was to see them. His hearers
hung round and gloated rapturously over their
Idol until nearly 6 p.xn. Then he escaped with
his party and boarded a tram in the gathering
dusk, still shadowed by a faithful few.
“For the sake of Shakespeare’s poor, vexed
ghost, believers in a spirit world rejoice that the
Potter-Bellew “As You Like It” has, for the
time being, ceased from troubling. Women, as a
rule, are pitiful, and perhaps it was partly’ com-
punction for the hovering Bard of Avon that made
her own sex so impatient with Rosalind Potter
So grotesque,’ they all said, ‘and as flat as if
you d rolled her out with a rolling-pin ! ’
“A pretty, wealthy matron, who, a while back
shook off a troublesome husband in the Divorce
Court, intends, ’tis said, to bestow herself her
thousands, and her two children upon a youthful
Melb. barrister The happy man is an ardent

From my Brisbane correspondent:—
“ The opening of the first session of our 12th
Parliament was a soul-stirring function lasting-
over two days The principal inwardness of the
ceremony was the interesting study of osculatory
method. As members took their seats in the Lower
House, they developed some 70 different styles of
kissing, varying from a perfunctory bob of the Book
against a member’s beard to the sonorous smack
administered by one burly legislator-which smack
augured well for his moral code. Experience in
contraband caresses would probably have taimbr
noise t 0 glVe S ° S ° Ulful a Sal Ute W ' th much £
“Lord Lamington’s gubernatorial uniform was
new and resplendent, but nevertheless improve
of the fact that a man must be brought up to a
uniform to feel on good terms with it. P
“ Lady Lamington for once looked well out of
doors. A mantle of handsome black moire reach-
ing to her feet was trimmed with wide bands of
cream lace, with which the big sleeves and cape
were also garnished. The hat was suggestive of a
red velvet cushion tied in the middle.
“Mr Hoolan’s congratulation to the Premier on
his birthday ‘honors’ fairly brought down the
House. After felicitating him upon being the
only solvent knight in Queensland, he extended to
him his commiserations for being in such uncon-
genial company, and consoled him by hinting that
possibly in the dim future he, Jack Hoolan, might
be found amongst the Order—when, presumably,
the only cloud would be removed from Sir Hugh s
mental horizon.
“ The Governor’s Private Secretary is a tenor
who, it is said, once adorned the operatie stage.
At a fashionable charity concert, the other night,
the very smart set fairly mobbed the hall to catch
the first notes of the much-talked-of vocalist, for
rumor had said that Tapley would have to look to
his laurels. The gifted one gave evidence of a
tuneful and well-managed warble—of its compass
people were given no chance of judging; so far,
however, Tapley may sleep in peace. It must be
a trial to sing in an uncompromising collar, ear-
high.”
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
