The point on which Messrs. Brandt Bros.,
of 236 Pitt-street, Sydney, specially desire to
challenge creation is that their kerosene-gas is 65
per cent, cheaper than the ordinary article sup-
plied by the local soulless monopoly. This firm
has on exhibition the “Primus” kerosene gas
stoves, ovens, and cooking apparatus, which con-
vert kerosene into gas, burn without fumes or
smell and with no wick, and cook admirably and
cheaply. Also they offer the Hecla Primus forgo
which supplies sufficient heat to melt lead or brase
and to bend iron bars; the Vesuvius painter’s
burning-off lamp; the JEtna brazing lamp ; the
Ultra Primus lamp for heating soldering-irons,
and the well-known Anti-Candle, an illuminant
which recent improvements have brought practi-
cally to perfection.*

Old Col. Wells, who commanded the
N. infantry in the Soudan, has one joke. To

S.M. Herald : The Contingent has arrived at
Tamai. Wells dry.”
Judge Casey (Vic.;, in nonsuiting with
costs a libel-action plaintiff, has ruled that to call
a man (in letter addressed to a third person)
“ cowardly ” and “ a cur not fit to associate with
other men,” was not “that excess of comment
which would constitute malice.”
At a recent Adelaide banquet, Premier
Kingston was making notes for a speech. There
were one or two particularly godly people there,
and a friend whispered in the Premier’s ear Ihe
notes went rapidly into his pocket. He had been
writing on the back of a totalisator ticket.
The frigid piety of the S.A. vice-regal
party is appalling to ordinary sinners. Noel
Buxton, the just-arrived, is a rabid cold-tea man,
despite the beer foundation of-the family fortunes,
and the new aide has the humorless demeanor of
a Scotch policeman. But Gov. Buxton, despite
all drawbacks, is personally popular.
Masonic Grand-Master Brassey has just
been initiated into the mysteries of Oddfellowship.
This is what the Age calls “carrying out his pro-
mise to identify himself as far as possible with
Victorian life.” Fiddle! The. Governor merely
identifies himself with secret societies—the darkest
and most unwholesome corners of V ictorian life.

fell in the Court, the Q.C. was ready with Fiat
justiiia ruat ccetum.”

Louis Beeke will shine in London as a
smoking-room raconteur, telling grimly-funny
stories with his inimitable stutter. To socially
succeed in London, if you are not a local duke or
something of the kind, you must be and look like
an exotic. Becke’s many Australasian friends are
wondering how the ex-pirate, who really looks
like a pirate, will look in a long hat.
A good point about Premier Turner is that,
though he works late himself, he won’t let his
subordinates work late. He stews over figures
night after night in his office at the Treasury, but
he sends his orderly home at 5 p.m. regularly.
“Mr. Turner,” says an old civil servant, “ is the
most considerate Premier to his subordinates that
we have ever had within my memory. ”
“ Godfearin’ ” Garrard, when returning
thanks for his return at last N.S.W. general elec-
tion said something to the effect that he had never
doubted the result, as he felt he was in the hands
of a Higher Power. Whereupon his opponent
declared that had he known that the Almighty
was supporting Garrard, he would never have
stood against their united omnipotence.
The Tasmanian “travelling correspondent”
of a mainland weekly used to excuse late despatch
of “copy” by throwing the blame on the “bad
roads.” Having missed two consecutive weeks,
and feeling unequal to the strain of the position,
he was about to resign the post when a curt edi-
torial note arrived : “No copy this week ; are the
roads bad again ? ” He accordingly wired his re-
signation : “No roads at all— am making tracks.”
Rudyard Kipling has had his brother-in-
law bound over, on account of some empty threats.
He stated, when in the box, that he had never
been in a row in his life. Kipling, the depictor of
a hundred battles-royal and street rows, never had
personal experience ! It serves to show how far
psychologically an author may be from his crea-
tions. What, for instance, would the glorious
Mulvaney have done had a marriage-relation
threatened to lick him ?
The fount and origin of Yarney Parkes’s
truceless and widely-advertised feud with N.S.W.
Public Works Committee are found in the pro-
posal made by him, when chairman, that ,£5O
should be devoted to the purchase of a number of
books which he considered necessary for the edu-
cation of the members of the Committee. Varney
considered his proposal an essential to intelligent
work, but the committee jumped on it as an ebony
insult.

Melb. demise : Allan M‘Vean, for 40 years
Brunswick Presbyterian pastor. Such men pre-
serve respect to the parsonhood by their humility,
abstinence from specs, in the earthly mansions,
and good work amid the poor. Mac. was of the
here stands-a-post, good old Dr. Cairns order, and
was almost the last tusker in the wagging jaw of
the era of Hethermgton and Co. All the new-
fangled higher criticism, introduced by Menzies
and wrought up by Rentoul, he dismissed with
one semaphore wave of his arm.

Love, who lately killed his drunken wife
at Sydney, won’t be hanged. The jury expressly
found that he was mentally distracted at the time
of the deed, that he had long borne his domestic
troubles patiently, and that his wife’s dissolute-
ness ultimately unhinged his mind. The recom-
mendation to mercy was absolute, and the Reid
Government has, so far, been sensible enough
never to disregard a recommendation to mercy.
Besides, the jury’s suggestion will, no doubt, be
strongly urged by Judge Rogers, as humane a man
as ever wore a wig.
Tom Walker, the versatile everythingarian,
left Sydney the other day to conduct a teetotal
crusade in Maoriland, just about the time that
the lighthouse-keeper of The Brothers lighthouse
in Cook Strait saw sea-serpents.
A Coon correspondent to The Bulletin :
Sam Little, a well-known prospector, left Rocky Dig-
gings, East Coast (N.Q.) in March intending to walk to
Coen (about 40 miles distant). Six weeks afterwards hi«
remains were found by a search party 60 miles N.E. 01
Coen. Little had kept a diary on the back of a pocket-
mirror. The diary showed that he was hopelessly bushed
five days after leaving Rocky. He then built a small
gunyah which he thatched with gra. s. According to the
diary he existed for nearly three w eeks after this living on
raw mussels, having no matches to light a fire. He then
wrote his will on the bottom of an empty butter-tin. It
concluded, “God is my witness, brother, S. Little.
Round the sides of the tin were written his instructions
to the finders of his remains re the disposal of his pro-
perty and personal effects. Both will and instructions
were written briefly and concisely, and there was not a
word in either relating to the misery poor Sam must have
endured.
Spagnoletti: Zox scores, by proposing
that a Melb. artist, presumably Longstaff, shall
paint Queen Victoria, with a sitting, at Victorian
State expense. The Jews furnish the tip-top
loyalty, as well as the most extreme anarchism.
Well do I remember the time when the late A. T.
Clark, M.L.A., was alleged to have libelled H.M.
in his Williamstovvn paper. Zox called attention
to it in Parliament. The Ministry tried to bilk
him of a speech, by urging that other important
business demanded attention. “What ! ” shouted
Thoxth, “ Doth the honorable gentleman mean to
tell me that any bithnith can be tho important ath
the question of an inthult to Her Grathiouth
Majesty! ” Kerferd and colleagues could only
inly groan, while Zox orated a column of the
newspapers, well knowing that every word was
first-class copy.
N.S.W. Sheriff Cowper (son of a late
N.S.W.“Premier), who has been retrenched, was
the only occupant of that office who “ dined ” the
Judges in his banqueting hall at Darlinghurst
Court-house —in feeble imitation of a similar func-
tion performed by the High Sheriffs in Great
Britain. The function provoked little enthusiasm,
the space between the offices of host and guests
being rather too well marked. The High Sheriff
in Great Britain holds an honorary office of high
degree, whereas Australian Sheriffs are little more
than apotheosised bailiffs and boss executioners.
Australian Sheriffs’ dinners roundaboutly recall
the story of Beau Brummel when he had been
invited, with a number of friends, to a grand
dinner given by a wealthy nobody-in-partieular.
“ Oh, yes,” said Brummel, “ everything passed off
well—only the damned fellow would insist on
sitting down with us ! ”
Vic. Minister for Education Peacock—-the
Man with the Iron Laugh—not along ago visited
a Melb. suburban school. After some of the
classes had been put through their facings—to the
accompaniment of laughter which made the timid
little ones shake in their shoes—the youngsters
were lined up and Peacock proceeded to address
them. “ Now, children, do you know who I am?”
he commenced. A pause. “Don't you know who
I am?” said Peacock, surprised. A little girl’s
hand went up : “ Please, sir, the new inspector ! ”
Peacock laughed ‘ ‘ Hrrecrrechaw-haw-haw ! ”
while the blackboards rattled and a map fell off
the wall. When he stopped another hand went
up : “ Please, sir, you’re the man with a vacant
mind ! ” The head-master hastened to explair
that the lesson for the day had been Goldsmith’s
“Deserted Village,” where the couplet;occurs :
The watch-dog’s voice that bay’d the whispering wind,
And the loud laugh that spoke the vacant mind.
Peacock accepted the explanation glumly, and
didn’t laugh once during the rest of his visit.
And now it is sai(f that the “ Deserted Village’ is
to be struck out of the Vic. school-books.

Two Itives Saved.
Mas. S. G. Cox, Pepper Grove, Bacchus Marsh,
Victoria, was told by her doctor that she could
not live. The same dismal verdict wab given to
Mr. B. L. Cooke, Lyttleton, N.Z., but they both
escaped ; were saved by the same means, and it
will save you—if you take it in time. Mrs. Cox
says : “ I was taken suddenly ill, and called in our
doctor, who attended me without doing me the
least good. He then informed me that I was suf-
fering from chronic disease of the kidneys, and
that, having vainly tried everything, he considered
that it would be useless to continue his attendance.
At that time I was completely wasted away, and
in such a helpless state that I could not leave my
bed. I then, through the advice of friends, com-
menced taking Warner’s Safe Cure. The effects
which followed were marvellous. I can now look
back and say that if it had not been for Warner’s
Safe Cure, I should have been in my grave. To
those suffering from kidney disease I earnestly
advise a trial of this medicine before anything
else.”
Mr. Cooke says: “For months my life was
made a constant misery with sufferings from
Bright’s Disease, causing pains in my back, pain
m my sides, and terrible headaches, while my
water was full of red sand. The disease finally
affected my eyesight, and for three long weeks I
was totally blind. Reduced in flesh, weak, and
altogether disheartened, after the doctors failed to
help me, I finally heard of Warner’s Safe Cure for
kidney disease, and tried it; indeed, after using
seven bottles, with the Safe Pills, as required, I
was quite restored. ”*

Actually, Kanakaland has stipulated in a
mail contract that the contractor, the British-India
Co., shall man its steamers with white crews !
The £5OO reduction made by N.S.W-
Assembly in the Parkes Family grant was a
miserable tinkering at the fringe of a miserable
make-believe.
The Chinese Government has but one
official representative in Australasia a store-
keeper at Cooktown (Q.), who proudly signs him-
self “ Chinese Consul.”
Sydney Truth describes the action of R.
Jones, M.P. (N.S.W.), re the Mudgee <.aol bricks,
as “ moving an adjournment to discuss the bad
quality of the bricks which shelter his constituents.”
A Victorian “ railway committee ” recom-
mends that a tax “per acre” shall be levied on
“ all land allowed to lie idle and which might
otherwise furnish freight ”to any railway-line. It
is coming !
Tasmanian Treasurer Dobson remarks that
“ Protection is like feeding a dog on his own tail.”
But any sane dog would rather have his tail
scientifically amputated and cooked for his own
use than hacked off for the benefit of the other
dog—which is Freetrade.
There has been yet another scene in Melb.
district court, Attorney Gaunson having again
objected to teetotal faddist Lormer, J.P., sitting
on a licensing-case. Mr. Lormer has no more
right to sit in judgment on publicans than the
historical wolf had to sit on the lamb.
One result of the appointment of- Haw-
thorne, M.P., as N.S.W. Government Whip,
meaning an extra <£3oo a year, is the conversion
of some Reidites to a belief that it pays to be sus-
pected of semi-independence and an inclination
occasionally to vote “ agen the Government.”
The N.S.W. Solidarity Labor-party seems
very likely indeed to stick to Reid, not because it
directly fears him, for he has had his dissolution ;
but because (for one reason) if it threw out the
present Ministry Lyne would come along with a
dissolution in his pocket and cause no end of
trouble.
Justice Windeyer’s pretensions to a seat
on the Privy Council are laughed to scorn by the
rest of the Australias. The odds are 10,000 to 1
against his getting an appointment from United
Australia, for, outside his own province, he is
chiefly, if not solely, known as the man who tried
the Mount Rennie and Dean cases.
Last week’s N.S.W. political crisis. “ Let
us play at troopers tracking bushrangers,” says
Opposition-leader Lyne. “Phoo! this is only
political petty larceny. You can’t prove that we
blacked our faces or wore crape. Besides, you
helped us to let the big-brewery alleged bush-
rangers go,” retorts Premier Reid.
Ex-Treasurer Ward (M.L.) was lately ban-
queted by a few friends at Stratford, Taranaki,
and being pressed for a song he gave that ancient
melody, “There’s Bound to be a Row.” That
was the first forecast of his approaching downfall.
A few weeks later Judge Williams started over-
hauling the affairs of Ward’s dreadful Farmers’
Association, and, when it turned out that the
Treasurer could only pay about Is. in the <£l, the
row arrived.
The new S.A. Assembly should be the
most garrulous on record. The silent men of the
last Assembly—Kelly, Packham, Johnson, H. E.
Downer, Randell, and others —were all rejected at
the election, and all the old wind-bags were re-
turned by large majorities ; and as the most re-
liable wind-bag of all, Copley, the converted
Democrat, is now leader of the Opposition, which
consists mostly of Tory wind-bags, S.A. Parlia-
ment has a lively time ahead.
The curse of the late Dibbs Govt.’s piebald
tariff has come home to roost. N.S.W, Labor
members are now throwing at Opposition leader
Lyne the well-merited accusation that the 'Dibbs
Ministry, of which Lyne was first lieutenant,
associated, itself with Freetrader Salomons on i
deliberate agreement that its fiscal program •
should only amount to a revenue tariff, and not
Protectionist one.
If Australia must needs have a Judge on
the British Privy Council, the man who has a
clear right to the billet is Way (and his dog) of
South Australia—not Windeyer, who is impu-
dently trying to jump the claim. Way is the
senior Chief Justice of Australia. He is one of
the ablest lawyers on the Australian bench, and
has never mixed himself up with politics, or given
testimonials to dubious spooks, or served as an
advt. for anybody’s ghost. He has no Mount
Rennie on his judicial record, and he hasn’t gone
’Ome in search of the job, which is another point
in his favor. If there must be an Australian
Judge on the blessed Privy Council, Way and his
dog have distinctly the best claim to the honor—
against Windeyer and his spook.
It is alleged that it was Premier Reid who
suggested to the British Government—without
consulting the Ministries of the other six pro-
vinces—the appointment of Mr. Windeyeras Aus-
tralian Judge on the Privy Council. This off-
hand assumption of superiority will probably rile
the other Premiers and make Mr. Windeyer’s pro-
spects rather worse even than they otherwise
would be. If the' news reached Kingston when
the Portuguese side of his character was upper-
most he ought to kick, at all events.
Judge Murray’s exercise of the discretion
allowed him by current law—which only for the
direct interference of the Governor would have
made a Star Chamber performance of the Royal
Commission on Public Works ; Judge Backhouse’s
exercise of discretion in the solicitor Rofe affair ;
and several other similarly-questionable judicial
“discretions” within the past year or so, should
make the N.S.W. public pause, and pause thought-
fully, when Salomons asks that justice shall be
expedited by extending the discretionary powers
of judges.
When Parkes allowed Garrett to bring in
the Payment of Members Bill he was reproached
by Dibbs as an unprincipled Premier for allowing
a Minister to father a Bill in the teeth of the
Premier’s convictions. Parkes retaliated in similar
savage phrases when Dibbs, the liquor Premier,
allowed Kidd, the water-Minister, to bring in an
anti-whisky Bill ; and now Reid, also a liquor-Pre'-
mier, who jeered both Parkes and Dibbs for thus
dishonoring their Premiership, allows Minister
Joe Cook to bring in a Bill to flood the Legislature
with water.
According to the Sydney morning papers
it is probable the Reid Govt, will, when the
financial statement is made, announce its intention
of making some compromise over the sugar-duty.
If it does it deserves to be bundled neck and crop
out of office. It was returned by a Freetrade
majority to establish the glorious, blessings of
Freetrade in N.S.W. It promised to do so,
and if it doesn’t keep its promise then it is
an Ananias. Already there has been a great deal
too much compromise in the matter, and the
blessings of Freetrade have been postponed too
long.
Patriot Farnell, M.L.A., retired the other-
day from the poorly-salaried position of N.S.W.
Govt, whip because he was incapacitated through
ill-health, and was promptly appointed to a highly-
paid job (it pans out about ,£7OO a year) as mem-
ber of the Public Works Committee. Presumably
Mr. Farnell will attend in an insensible condition
on an air mattress and his fees will be put in his
pocket for him by a kindly colleague. But,
solemnly speaking, nowadays almost everybody
appointed to the Committee smells strongly of
iodoform, and clumps with a crutch, Or respires
God’s glorious oxygen through carbolicised cotton
wool.
“THE SAME BUT DIFFERENT
HE: “ When we were married I thought we were to be ‘two souls with but a
single thought.’”
SHE: “ Well, aren't we? Don't we both wish we were single again!”
Judge Docker is of opinion that, under the
N.S.W. criminal law, a magistrate can’t legally
sentence a woman to hard labor. It can, he
thinks, only be done by a Judge. This is a matter
which wants to be cleared up ; a woman has just
as much right, in these days of equality, to hard
abor as a man.

only one Chamber to deal with, we could make

To again allude to a well-worn theme—the
iniquities of the Ass in Bananaland. Brisbane
was actually appointed a polling-place for Carpen-
taria-2000 miles away, while Croydon, just on
the borders of that electorate and with more votes
than Brisbane for Carpentaria, could on no account
be gazetted. How long, 0 Lord !
Premier Kingston’s proposal re the bien-
•,retirement of half the Assembly, is a curious
example “f the appearance of atavistic spots on
the democratic leopard. It is the very continuity of
existence sought for in S. A. that helps to make Vic.
Leg Council one of the strongest plutocratic cham-
ber" in the world, and practically irremovable save
bv quasi-revolution iry methods. Supposing a
S A Parliament hateful to the country it would
require four years of extreme political tension to
get rid of it,'to say nothing of the danger of de-
fections in the half-house returned in the first two
years.
Deakin’s—shall we say treasonous —private
suggestion to Sargood that if objectionable to the
Upper House, part of the factories Bill might be
limited to three years, has evidently been greedily
snapped up in other quarters. Replying to the
deputation re the long hours of shop-assistants,
Premier Reid hinted that perhaps the Government
would be able to introduce the desired legislation
in the forthcoming Factories and Workshops Bill,
if only as an experiment, giving the Act, say,
three years to run. From the general tone of the
Preimer’s remarks the bell-wether of Vic, Council
is evidently to lead N.S.W. factories legislation.
Recently the N.S.W. Treasury circularised
a lot of the big Sydney firms asking if their cus-
tomers had received the full benefit of the aboli-
tion of duties—in other words, if, when 10 per
cent, duty was taken off, goods were 10 per cent,
cheaper ; and if not, why not? The answer sent
by at least two big firms was that they had given
their customers the entire benefit of the tariff re-
ductions, but that retail prices in their lines were
no lower, because whenever the duties came off
the English manufacturers raised their prices
sufficiently to cover the difference. So the Trea-
sury loses" a large amount of revenue, and neither
the local consumer nor the local dealer gains by it.
The profit goes to the English producer.
A great effort is going to be made at a
public overhaul of some branches of the Vic. tele-
graphs, and if it starts m, real earnest it may
develope into an inquiry into the whole postal
and telegraphic department. Then there will
probably be trouble. B.M.G. Duffy is the happy
incapable of the Vic. Ministry, and his 'inertness
has prevented the P.O. shar.ug, to any great
extent, in the general reorganisation. He got
into this Ministry, and into three previous ones,
because it is a sort of tradition in Melbourne that
there must be one more or less affable Irishman in
every Cabinet, but it is commonly believed that
Turner wouldn’t be sorry if he found a chance to
drop his Celtic colleague overboard wiilio t any
unnecessary splash.
Premier Seddon has announced himself ai
M.L. Treasurer in < f | l ace of the fallen Ward.
The experiment is bound to be interesting.
No one has hitherto regarded Richard of Kumar.i
as a financial expert; he is generally understood
to be only a practical, energetic, hard-headed man
of moderate education and respectable honesty,
who gets through most difficulties by pluck,
tenacity, and plain horse-sense, and when he meets
a difficulty that is beyond his mental resources
hits it an awful wipe with his great fist and flattens
it into atoms. Ward has gained credit for sup-
plying most of the financial detail of the Cabinet,
while Seddon furnished the broad principles ; and
though Seddon has risen unexpectedly to the Oc-
casion over and over again, this isn’t the sort of
occasion that he looks like rising to. Moreover,
financial problems can't be solved by welting them
with Digger Dick’s big fist.
Herewith The Bulletin presents the por-
trait of Robert Stout, probably, of all Australasian
Democrats now living, the one who has fallen
furthest and come down heaviest. He was once,
in theory at least, an advanced Liberal—a man
for and of the people, always preaching the gospel
of the oppressed and advocating purity and stern
morality. But, when the time came to do as well
as speak, he proved wanting. He became Vogel’s
colleague, joint-author of the Midland Railway
scheme and of the measure for the seizure of the
sinking-funds, K.C.M.G., and finally a bitter Tory
with intervals of Liberalism, and a disciple of
the National Ass. Stout is a brilliant orator and
probably the cleverest advocate in Maoriland. He
is a disappointed man inasmuch as he intended to
be the successor of Ballance, and) failing that, he
now proposes, by the aid of the Ass and the Cold-
Tea party, to some day succeed Ballance’s suc-
cessor. But Digger Dick Seddon sits heavy and
sits tight, and Stout, with all his brilliance, seeing
hardly strong enough to shift him.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing oyer a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
quyer of all other Australian gems.*
“ SUCCESS IN LIFE.—A new invention is
brought before the public and commands success.
A score of abominable imitations are immediately
introduced by the unscrupulous, who, in copying
the original olosely enough to deceive the public,
and yet not so exactly as to infringe upon legal
rights, exercise an ingenuity that, employed in an
original channel, could not fail to secure reputa-
tion and profit.”— Adams. Caution.—Examine
each bottle and see the capsule is marked ENO s
Fruit Salt.” Without it you have been imposed
on by a worthless imitation. Prepared bv J. C.
Eno*s Patent, at Eno’s “Fruit Salt Works,
London, S.E. Sold by all chemists and stores.

The Celebrated Taint dale Orchard, Ourimbah, near Gosford, N.S. W. For Sale by Richardson & Wrench, Ltd.
The celebrated Palmdale Orchard, for sale by
auction on Friday, 10th July. To orchardists,
fruit-exporters, gentlemen, and investors. The
well-known Palmdale Orchard, Ourimbah (in the
famous Gosford district), celebrated for the beauties
of its natural vegetation, marvellous richness of
soil, and strikingly effective views. Palmdale
Estate was a careful choice by its owners out of a
large area of primeval forest, and by art, skill, and
the judicious expenditure of a large sum of money,
a perfect property has been evolved, combining
both the pleasing residential and reproductive
aspects. The total area of land is 140 acres, about
90 of which are cleared, and thereout the
plantation is about 56 acres, representing a vast
number of trees of orangery and orchard sorts, and
in their collection no expense or trouble was
spared to procure the very best kinds ; so that in
a very few years a large annual income is assured.
Great delight, too, ca.n be enjoyed at Palmdale
such as is offered by very few places. Splendid
scenery. Palmdale cottage is most comfortably
furnished and commodious, well built of brick,
with verandah front, side, and rear, and contains
hall, nine rooms, pantry, kitchen, bathroom, &c.
Three cottages for men are conveniently placed,
containing severally three rooms and two rooms,
&c. There are all necessary outbuildings for effi-
ciently conducting so superior a property. The
fruit chamber is novel, and thoroughly suit-
able, by the principle of drafts of cool air, to
store equal to 1000 cases of oranges or other
fruits. It is being sold only on account of the
quite recent decease of W. Yon der Heyde, Esq.,
who held it with his partner, and it is now deter-
mined regretfully by the executors to part with
Palmdale. Cards to view for Wednesday and
Saturday in each week obtainable from either firm
of auctioneers, who will also be pleased to exhibit
to inquirers a schedule of the trees. Photographs
on view at auctioneers’. Richardson and Wrench,
Ltd. (in conjunction with Hardy and Gorman),
have received instructions from the executors of
the estate of the late W. Yon der Heyde, Esq., to
sell by public auction, at the rooms, Pitt-street,
Sydney, on FRIDAY, 10th JULY, at 11
o’clock, the above. Deserving of inspection and
attention.*

A. Saunders, of 815 George-street, Sydney
has long enjoyed a reputation as running about
tlTe cheapest, best-stocked and most widely-known
jewellery emporium in bydney. His stock in
eludes every known variety of every known article
in the jewellery biz. at every possible price except
the exorbitant one. The " bad times of recent
years have struck the jewellery industry very hard
and a large number of firms have been driven out
of the business. All the dealers whose prices were
high and their goods dusty and antiquated have
gone down in the general collapse, and A. Saunders
has risen on their ruins, and annexed whatever
remained of their trade, and is consequently doing
better than ever. There is always room at the
top, and the man who is at the top is never
crowded, and A. Saunders is that man and intends
continuing to be him. #

SPORTING NOTIONS.
“Sheffield Park,” gossips an Australian abroad
to The Bulletin, “is 43 miles from London, 2i hours
journey by the special slovv-train. The cricket-ground—
an ideal one for picnic cricket—slopes to the ropes and
developes doubles into fourers. An attack of worms has
demoralised the pitch, which kicked and crumbled until
it lay in the dusts of its own disintegration.
“ Albertedvvard was inside a dark-brown suit
and under a black ‘boxer.’ He had a ‘far-off, North Pole’
expression in his eye, his beard electro plated with early
old-age, and his cheek browned with heavenly tanaline.
At his approach the crowd cheered and clapped in chips,
and old Nineteen-and-Six exposed his glistening hairless
nob what time the bridge of his aquiline nose rose like
unto the back of a fighting ‘ Tom.’ A more regal-looking
man than his pictures show, not bloated, but bored to a
death of dullness.
“ The Kruger-faced Earl of Sheffield says the
last match has been played in the Park. He abhors the
London pressmen, whom he terms a ‘ lot of ragamuffins,’
simply because they commented on his childishness in
boycotting the members of Stoddart’s team from selection
in the initial match.
“‘Oh, what a difference in the morning,’ said
‘ W.G.’ to Trott when on the second day 5 wickets fell for
16 runs. George Henry Stephens replied later per medium
of ‘Jonah,’ whose fast ’uns rattled knees, ribs and
knuckles, or touched the bat and passed on to rest in a
waiting pair of hands. Alick Hearne, the pro., said he
never played against faster bowling. But Ranjitsinghi,
when asked how the present Spofforth compared with the
‘ Demon,’ answered —“ After half-a-dozen matches he will
not be heard of ; 41‘Kibbin is a better bowler.”
“The Indian Prince is an undersized, gimcrack-
built stripling. A plate of curry with two bright, black
beads stuck in, and a dozen large, clean grains of rice on
the lower edge, fairly represents his dark, puffy face. A
most brilliant bat, and a far greater favorite than ‘ W.G.’
He repeatedly cut Jones for four between six fieldsmen,
or changing his feet in a flash whipped the fastest to the
leg boundary. His marvellous eye and double-jointed
wrists made up for want of physique, and his pluck was
never ‘ out ’ when called on. In the field as active on his
legs as a strychnine-dosed cat, he works with astonishing
enthusiasm, and only English prejudice can keep him out
of the All-England Team.
“ W.G. never plays in a match without putting
in a claim to the title of William Goat. There was an
appeal for * run out ’ against Darling. The umpire had
his eyes open, and couldn’t give it. ‘ What! !! ’ shouted
the professionally-paid amateur, and subsided into a
silence so heavy that it weighed down the umpire’s eyelids
during the Englishmen’s second innings.
“With the bat Trott, Darling, and Hill shap’d
best in the order named ; Iredale, Gregory and Eady
worst, considering expectations. The solitary Victorian
who played is none the worse for being a stone lighter,
and batted well all round the wicket. Hill showed but
little nervousness, and satisfied even the English one-eyed
critics. Darling and Donnan batted steadily and surely ;
the latterly as indifferently as the former did well. Giff.
bowled with hard luck and batted with none, being given
out in the first round for a catch behind on the wicket-
keeper’s appeal only. George might indeed fairly have
exclaimed, ‘ WHAT ! !! ’.
“ Kelly missed a soft stump, but otherwise per-
formed creditably. M‘Kibbin opened with ‘tripe,’ and
pitched them still shorter after being boundary-ed, but
did better on a second trial. ‘ Jonah’s ’ audacity in hit-
ting the mighty W.G. on the knee, th/gh,' and ribs with
three opening balls is not likely to gain him the boss
cricket-cocky’s goodwill. But for that the strong man
recketh little. He has well, and smiles inwardly
at the thought of the pace he will get-on on a hell-fiery
wicket. Eady did not fill Trumble’s shoes, and his in-
clusion at such a price was worse than an error.
“ Giffen promenaded the ground with half-a-
dozen females —midgets and matrons —in deep black,
drawing forth the remark, ‘ There goes Giff. with his
mourning-coach.’
“Iredale has been described by the English
papers as of ‘aristocratic appearance;’ whilst Jones has
discovered his relationship to a family of nobility.
Climate, bai jove!
“ Australia’s Albert Edward has been engaged
at Lords. He gets, besides a salary, £5 a match. Barring
one score of 58 for Thornton’s XI., he has not, up to date,
done too much for his fivers.
“ Blondin Lyons, inspirited, would say, ‘l’m as
full of runs as a dog is of fleas ; I’ll make 1000 some day.’
No doubt; but where to ?
“ G, S. Trott was introduced to the Prince, who
gave him a flower and a cigar. The phlegmatic captain
immediately bit one weed and puffed complacently, put-
ting the other in his pocket, for his better-half, doubtless.
“Jack Harry has been temporarily taken on at
Lords, and played his first match, a week after landing,
for the M.C.C. v. Leicestershire. He went in first and
totalled B—the third highest score for his side.”
• * « *
Veteran Peel is apparently on the down-grade
as a bowler. He started this season with 1 wicket for 27,
and 2 for 90 against such moderate teams as Warwickshire
and Somerset. Richardson seems to be going strong as
ever, judging from his work against Essex—ll wickets for
170, and 10 of them clean bowled.
* * * *
Comparing the average performances of English
county elevens when playing against one another with
the performances of the same teams against “our boys,”
it would seem that the Englishmen are the better
batsmen, whilst the Australians are much stronger
in bowling. For instance, Surrey played Essex in the
same week that the latter county met Australia. Surrey
scored an innings of 430 against the same bowling which
the Australians couldn’t punish severely. On the other
hand, Essex scored 174 and 236—a considerably bolder
show than the county made when the Australians were in
attack. Somerset siogged a first innings of 323 against
Yorkshire, and Yorkshire replied with 400 ; also Yorkshire
put up a record of 887 against Warwickshire—bigger
figures than the Australians would be likely to make, or
allow their opponents to make, in matches with either of
those teams. So it happens that the Australians may
beat county after county, and yet not have as good a
batting-average in county matches as some of the elevens
that they conquer, because the Englishmen get taller
scores from English bowling.
* * * *
Writing to Adelaide, Ernest Jones early in
May seemed to have scented the Sussex engagement. He
also said that A. J. Webbe has a big opinion of Albert
Trott as a batsman, but does not fancy his bowling.
* * * *
Apropos the Australians’ slow-scoring at Shef-
field Park, on a perfect wicket and with perfect weather,
a London weekly trotted forth the old story of the public
schoolmaster who played the “ blocking game.” The
awful man had been at the wickets half-an-hour for 6 runs
when one of the big-boys respectfully informed him that by
a new rule of cricket the umpire could call him out if he
didn’t raise his bat once during every over. The school-
master appealed to his umpire for confirmation of the
boy’s statement, whereupon the old Yorkshire professional
gave it to him straight : “ Aw don’t know owt abaht that.
But aw know if tha’ does lift thee bat tha’ll domned soon
be out.”
* • * *
The Derbyshire man, Hulme, was the first
bowler to take it out of the Australians on a good wicket
—6 for 54. Clearly, the new man bothered them. They
pottered about until 4.30 p.m. for a score of 138, after
which the blawsted Britishers made 168, at a cost ol three
wickets only, within the next two hours.
• * • *
Meanwhile, the very latest news about the
match with All-England is that Australia went in, and
was knocked out in 75 minutes tor 53 runs. Then the
Englishmen went in, and at time of writing had made 267
for eight wickets, or something equally overwhelming.
Under these ghastly circumstances, all the above gush
about the Australian heroes seems waste of valuable
space. What is the good of gushing about 11 cricketers
who can only make 53 runs among them in the first inn-
ings of a test-match ?
P.S. —“ The glorious uncertainty! ” When The
Bulletin went to press the English Ist innings had
closed for 292, and the Australians, or some of them, were
playing real gate-money cricket: Trott, not out, 141;
Gregory, 103 —4 wickets for 283.
* * * *
This will be the third year of Bischoff in a G.N.
Hurdle Race at Flemington, and possibly the rogue will
again start a good favorite on the strength of a flattering
trial. One or two trainers, besides Scobie, have already
given Bischoff best, and their latest successor doesn’t
seem to have put a new heart into the horse, so far. Last
week Bischoff performed execrably at a Melb. suburban
meeting. But he ran fourth for the big race last year,
and the handicapper has let him in 31b. lighter this time,
therefore, sanguine punters who back rogues for the sake
of what they might do, in the face of repeated disappoint-
ments, may as well pour some more gold currency down
the Bischoifian sink. What’s the odds, when one loser is
just as good as another ?
* * * *
The great horse sport is, or ought to be, killing
itself in Sydney. Present average, about 572 meets a
year ! When a couple of trotting clubs come along and
increase the 11 shows a week to 14, 75 per cent, of the
Sydney public will gradually get into gaol—or the poor-
house.
* * * *
The threatened 8. A. anti-tote campaign has not
eventuated, the Adelaide clergy being too busy over the
share-market.
* * * *
True Blue, winner of the double at Randwick
last Saturday week, paid a div. of £3 for 2s. 6d. in an
Adelaide street place-tote on the Randwick Stakes.
* * ♦ *
In the U.S. they are giving trotting horses
cocaine injections before races to liven them up, and
many Yankee cyclists are said to be taking to cocaine.
May the Lord have mercy on their souls 1 Hell —it is
cocaine.
* * » *
The withdrawal of the two Maorilanders from
their Vic. G.N. engagements was quite expected by
people who knew the horses. They were handicapped
out of reason. Owner Douglas paid £9OO for two of his
intended visiting string, secured specially for the trip. He
reckoned without the handicapper.
* * * *
The novelty of two Sydney racing fixtures in one
day was spoilt on Saturday, rain smothering the intended
Brighton pony-show. A double galloping dose, and foot-
ball barracking in every nook and corner of the metro-
polis, leads strangers to believe that Sydneyites live on
sandwiches of horseflesh and leather.
* # * *
Warwick Farm’s decently-endowed Cup, with
alluring sweep-money in prospective, brought out a good
galloping field, but backers fell by the wayside. The
Trenton filly Wahine demonstrated unwonted finishing
pace, and secured a 20 to 1 chance, an almost unheard-of
winning price at a weekly meet. The enrichment of the
ring was brought about by favorite Newman being knocked
back by a two-horse fall. His training companion won,
but the plunging stable didn’t calculate on the contingency,
and the public followed the betting-ring tip. Glendenon
was unlucky in losing after pacing the field throughout.
The late run of Loch Leigh promised a remunerative
victory in the near future.
» * * •
Hurdler Havanna, Dan O’Brien’s casfc-off, played
leap-frog with his opponents at Caulfield on Saturday on
three legs and a swinger. Cigarette’s grey son, who
lumbered top-weight, appears to be a pretty prime cigar.
* * * *
Totalisator “fractions” at Adelaide R.C.
meeting came to £l9l.
* * * «
People who plunge early have to plunge often,
if their early fancies are scratched. That Melb. backer
who took Waterloo in a 3000 to 30 double illustrates the
turfy saw. Waterloo was .withdrawn next day. Punters
meet their Ikey Mo sooner or later.
• • * •
A solitary Rowe-street (Sydney) bookie was
fined last week for laying a silver double. Scores of
others, who were laying even more openly, escaped obser-
vation. Also, papers published the latest betting that
morning, as usual, and continue to do so, but tho . e are
the quotations of protected top-perch bookies, and do l’t
count. There’s nothing like protection. Even the police
are averse to freetrade in betting.
* * » »
A Christchurch (M.L.) turfite has perfected the
tote, the stamping of the ticket called for ringing the in-
vestment on to the horses’ number and on to the grand total
simultaneously. At present the money-taker calls the
number, another stamps it, and a third rings it on the
machine.
• • * •
A Sydney business man, who never attends
races, noting that there was money in the game, set a man
up with a £3O start in the silver-laying business some time
ago, and has been drawing dividends ever since. The
private cash biz. pays better than his own credit establish-
ment.
* * * *
Gold Prix, the Sydney small-stick candidate, is
a direct descendant of The Wild Irish Girl, winner of Mel-
bourne’s big steeple 40 years ago, when £lOOO was re-
fused for the lepping beauty.
• * * *
A light-weight G.N. double Bischoff and
Will-o’-the-Wisp. The former ran fourth in the Hurdles
last year, and the latter, a fine jumper, hails from Dai-
mio’s stable.
* * » »
Sydney horses have been handicapped in a
patchy sort of fashion for Vic. G.N. Hurdles, but they are
not a brilliant lot. Gold Prix will likely prove the most
reliable of them on the day. By no means a phenomenon,
Payten reckons her a cheap £5O worth, and expects some-
thing in a minor key if he doesn’t annex the coveted big
money.
* » * »
In a Sydney football-inquiry last week a witness
stated that the swearing used “on the umpire” was
“ every-day swearing.” The cognoscenti will smile. Foot-
ball-swearing is a fine art, and for a partisan to so depre-
ciate its merits shows a big lack of artistic instinct. By
the way, the club whose team threw so much mud and
waved their huge feet so violently at the umpire that he
went and got his revolver, has been expelled the N.S.W.
Football Association.
• • * •
The Wentvvorth-’Varsity contest interested a
limited attendance, principally barrackers, who got their
money’s worth. Dashing rushes and heavy scrums were
the main features, the forwards having most of the fun.
It was a fine athletic display ; neither’s line was crossed,
but Went.’s goaled from a free given against an opponent
for hanging on to a particularly dirty-looking ball. The
excellent efforts of the U.’s in the second term made
things look blue, but something always happened to
check their ardor. The solitary mishap of the day hap-
pened, as usual, to a 'Varsity man. Jh e y
convenient to have a playing-doctor in their tea ..

day, but the game was rowed under adverse circs.
ground was one vast sheet of water, and the P'° S
were mostly mud, earth, and very little ball. A
scores were a try each in the first spell. t

* * * *
The noble art has declined sadly in M.L. since
Harry Laing fought Slavin on Wanganui racecourse. A
coupie fighting for the so-called championship a few
weeks ago, gave a miserable exhibition, devoid of science,
and not up to a brisk mediocre athletic-hall sparring-
match. The festive Maori doesn’t seem to hanker after
glove and wrestling glory, as of old. A marked prefer-
ence now seems to be given to Saturday Rugby, whic
provides healthier hospital knock-outs, and affords a mucn
better show of getting in on t’other fellow when he isn t
looking.
* * * *
The Vic. football boundary-umpire idea, which,
when tried, was an unqualified success, making the game
faster, opener, and fairer to both sides, in addition to re-
lieving an exhausted and overworked central umpire, is
not followed up because of the slight expense ; it meant
expenditure representing several drinks weekly out of each
senior club’s sundry and professional account.
» * * *
Marchner, the German trick cyclist, who showed
at Adelaide recently, does some wonderful feats. W altz-
ing on wheels, riding without fork or handle-bar, jumping
on one wheel from a chair on a table to the floor, riding
on spokes only, are amongst his performances.
* ♦ * *
Italian Porta is of all cyclists on the Australian
track the most fastidious about his personal appearance.
He never leaves the dressing-room without surveying
himself in a tiny pocket mirror, to see if his hair is
straight, and it cuts him to the heart to go out a second
time in the afternoon in a sweater spattered with
mud. During the time he was laid up at St. Kilda
(Vic.), he used to be besieged by local damsels who
sought to do homage to his brown legs by loading the
bedroom with flowers and pots of jelly, and it was Porta’s
horror that any of the girls should be admitted while he
had a day’s growth on his chin. Shaving himself being
awkward, arrangements were made for a barber to ca.ll,
and, on that individual turning up, Porta subjected him
to a searching examination regarding his competency—
“ You no cut me? You no mek me hoogly?” was his
final suspicion. “ No, I won’t make you ugly,” guaranteed
the man of razors, and, accepting the assurance, the
Italian lay back on the pillow and submitted.
* * * •
Iron-jawed cyclist Martin has explained how he
wins his races. Prior to the event he reckons out the
amount of the prize-money in dollars, and then works up
his imagination until he can see a mythical heap of “ ten-
dollar bills ” at the winning-post—much as the “ subject"
of the hypnotist can feel a pin sticking in his left elbow.
When the bell sounds for the final lap, Bill’s visionary pile
swells in increasing perspective in his mind’s eye, and he
lets out for that pile as his historic countryman reached
for “ that pie.”
* * * •
At Cobar, N.S.W., the other day, John Griffiths
is said to have made the world’s record for endurance by
swinging clubs for thirty hours. Miserable wretch 1
# * * *
Michael, the 19-year-old Bst. Welsh cycling
wonder, is now getting £lOO a week for performing at
Westminster Aquarium, which has encouraged him to get
married. Mick’s trainer was so disgusted at his weakness
that he tendered his resignation on the engagement being
announced. Just as well, if his earnings depend upon
the Cambrian’s future winnings.
*— * *
That Kensington case demonstrates once again
the liability of the law to get lost when misinterpreting
itself. Bettors outside the gates (said Justice-Minister
Gould) were liable to punishment because they offended
on Crown land. But bookies inside (also Crown land)
were ungetatable because the course could not be con-
strued as a “place” within the meaning of the Act. A nice
discrimination between the nondescript silver layer and
the toppered gent with a cigar. The Crown derives
revenue from Kensington racecourse ; therefore, bookies
who play in its yard can lay from dawn till midnight
without let or hindrance.
* * * *
New word—Puntakin: a juvenile punter. Syd-
ney is full of them.
* * * *
“Buying a share” is the latest method of styling
owner’s connection with sweep-tickets. It sounds better
than “ blackmail."
* * * *
Timber-crossing incidents : one jockey refused
lately to ride a horse because it hadn't been properly
trained over hurdles. Another wouldn’t steer a certain
notorious customer unless he was shouted half a pint of
whisky before starting.
* * * *
One substantial winner in a Chinese ticket-lot-
tery the other night was a member of Sydney foorce. But
did he win, or was it only ordinary policeman’s luck?


“ Johnny ” Hawthorne, the new -N.S.W.
Govt. whip, is a staunch teetotaller, and was at
one time Grand Treasurer of the I.O.G.T’s. ,
The Goldsmiths’ and Silversmiths’ Alliance
is a firm which must commend itself to everybody, I
and should be carefully noted by buyers of jewel-
lery, The establishment is in King-street next
door to that good-looking and popular resort, the
catering rooms of the N.S.W. Fresh Food and Ice
Co. The Alliance’s premises are unique from the
standpoint of decorative shop art; it is incorrect
to say that they are a study in white and gold,
because the expression presents only a forcible
feeble picture to the mind’s eye. But waiving
this consideration and coming down to the bed-rock j
of all business tests—the question of price and
quality—the Alliance has perhaps the newest and
handsomest stock in Sydney ; the firm’s principals ;
are all long experienced in the jewellery art and
have made most advantageous arrangements for I
importing direct from leading English and Ameri-
can firms. One striking feature is the collection i
of American opals—beautiful gems alight with a
living sparkle.*

we are privileged to give below:
“I take pleasure in testifying to
the great benefit I derived from
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla. I suffered
from eruptions on my face of a very
irritating and vexatious nature.
For a considerable time I experi-
mented with various so-called blood
medicines, but without any allevia-

famous Sarsaparilla being strongly
recommended to me, I began to use
it, and after taking two bottles it
was most gratifying to see and feel
the effect in allaying the irritation
and reducing the eruptions. When
I had used three bottles the erup-
tions disappeared altogether, with-
out leaving a mark on my face, and
I have never been troubled with
anything of the kind since.”

“Barber’s rash” is nasty; syphilis is
worse. In France, of late, barbers have taken to
keeping razors, brushes, combs, and the like in a
1 per 1000 solution of mercuric sublimate. Cheap,
clean, and sanitary. Australia, please copy.
A would-be train-wrecker is at large in
Victoria. This individual apparently works
alone, and goes to infinite trouble in piling timber
on the railway-track, so far without much success.
The Australasian devotes a long article to specu-
lations as to what kind of maniac the wrecker
may be, and concludes at last that he is a dis-
torted monster of iniquity, partly ghoul, partly
Deeming, and the rest a mixture of Jack the
Ripper and bottomless pit. And probably he is
only an ordinary miscreant with a little more than
the usual desire to break something. Everybody
wants to break something. The desire to break
things and listen to the crash has been fixed in the
human brain by an all-wise Providence ; therefore
some people throw stones at a bottle, and some
throw stones at windows, and some roll big
boulders downhill on a holiday, and some try to
wreck trains, and some throw eggs at Thomas
Bent. But everybody wants to break something.
The only difference is that some people want to
break a larger thing into smaller pieces than
others do.
Copy of letter addressed as follows : “To
Honor Doctor and Director of Government Camel,
Coolgardie ”: —
The Honor, —Please excuse me I write you few lines en
you respict honor : Please I am bien this colony about 2
years ago and I have do it my camels agencies bussiness
here this time this business is done about 3 months I
have not founded any business to do —because I will ask
with you—if you favor me with you great pleasure and
you will give me place to do the Government Service —
like storekeeper of camel feed ore account maker to the
load of camel ore go with camel on his trip to make
understand of Afghan-man and I will drive the camel—
about any etc. work I will do it. Please I waite for you
great pleasure reply. lam a poor man and I have British
Subject I have en do India whole my service for the
Goverment. Plerse if you will appointed me en you any
service—l hope you and I never forget you with you
great charaty and I will do every service like Book-keeper
ore account maker ore drive the camel and entrpeter with
Afghan man. I know and read and write English and
French and Indian and Afghan. Please never forget you
servant and you will get any place to keep me. Please
apply me with letter Coolgardie P. Office—l am sure
please you will hope me Yours remain Servant Mahomed
Ali.
The ordinary business man’s most critical

The Jay Pee once more. At Prahran,
Vic., the other day, a man was charged with
brutally assaulting his wife. Owing to previous
illtreatment, the wife had gone out into a situa-
tion, but was persuaded by her lord and master to
leave it and come back to his loving fists, which
fists next day doled out to her such a brutal
thrashing that she was unable to give evidence for
a fortnight. Fined £5, or three months. At the
same court, a man who stole a diamond ring got
six months, without the option, and an intimation
from the Bench that, had the case come before the
Supreme Court, he would have got five years.
Proputty, proputty, proputty !

Southern Queensland is largely Teutonic.
At a recent Brisbane police court case both
parties were Germans; counsel on both ®^ es
German ; examination and cross-examination at
one stage also in German, until a witness, object-
ing to a barrister’s interpretation, discovered he
could express himself in fluent English.

A Much-valued Testimonial from one of
Australia’s Most-Prominent Explorers.
Grand Hotel, Spring-st., Melbourne,
March 7th, 1895.
To Herr Rassmussen,
Proprietor of the Alfaline Remedies.
Dear Sir, —It gives me great pleasure to inform
you that I have derived great benefit from your
celebrated Alfaline Remedies during my explor-
ing expeditions throughout Australia, and I coa-
sider them invaluable to all travellers in the bush.
—Yours very truly, B. BUTTON,
(Formerly a Councillor, Brighton, England).
Witness : A. F. Beker, Manager, Grand Hotel,
Spring-street, Melbourne.
Mr. Button writes later: —“ The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill ; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. 1
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”

Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir,—ln the year of 1871 I was eight days
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness in the joints, and have tried
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney
I had a great difficulty in walking, and went to
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E._
W. O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as.
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On]
Thursday before Good Eriday I took the first of
your Pills; since then I have used 4 boxes Special!
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special!
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver-
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline]
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing;
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years agoi.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.
Witness: Edward Hardy, proprietor Town
Hall Coffee Palace, 521 George-street.

A Ballarat banker who embezzled £1077
was sent to gaol for six months by pious Judge
Hodges the other day. His Honor said that his
bowels of compassion had been moved by the fact
that.prisoner had stolen the money because of the
sickness of those most dear to him, and had not
spent it on horse-racing or other low pleasures.
How would the burglar who cracked cribs to buy
medical comforts for his sick mother or his little
brother Bill fare at the hands of Judge Hodges?
The Bulletin fears that the filial or fraternal
sentiments of a low thief would not count for
righteousness. Only respectable-looking prisoners
who have had a clean shave and who wear
‘ ‘ decent ” clothes are supposed by the law to be
possessed of the finer emotions. And then em-
bezzlement is such a genteel crime ! Judges and
the “ best people ” in Victoria grow so tolerant or
it that it is in danger of becoming a new virtue.
Henry Lawson's book of collected verses,
containing all his best Bulletin poems, may be
had post-free from this office for ss. sd. in stamps
or postal-notes of any province.

The Bulletin notices that all the baby-
farming murderesses bowled out during the last
few years have possessed a distinct literary twist.
They all wrote excellent letters—perhaps that may
T5&T«r-wecessity of their calling. Mrs. Dyer, hanged
in London last week, wrote most plausible, free,
and flowing epistles, and while awaiting trial
asked a friend to send her “ Pr-ndemiis,” “East
Lynne,” and “ Bamaby Rudge.”
Apropos the case of Surgeon Lea, of the
Ringarooma, thus a writer in the Practitioner :
Some years back a naval officer whom I knew was com-
ing round from Sydney to Melbourne on one of the large
mail liners, and was horrified to hear the captain order a
course which would have driven the vessel on to the rocks
of Point Lonsdale. On speaking to the captain about it,
he was angrily told to mind his own business, and the
course was repeated to the quartermaster with emphasis.
The gentleman then went to the first officer and told him
of the circumstance, and the latter saw at once the danger
the vessel was in. He also remonstrated with the captain
in vain, and then, taking the law into his own hands, had
the captain secured in his own cabin, and altered the
course just in time to save the ship. The captain had to
he removed to an asylum two days afterwards !
One Yallander, of Wangaratta, lately sued
Vie. Railway Depart, for damages sustained at a
gate. The gate in question was opened for Yal-
lander by a child, and, as plain tiff was driving
through, it swung back, striking and crippling his
horse. In the trouble that followed, Vallander
and his wife and child sustained cuts, bruises,
breaks, and abrasions valued at =61250. The trial
was conducted with due solemnity ; the accident
was admitted, also the child ; but defendants con-
tended that the child’s grandmother was the pro-
per person to have opened the gate, and, as she
had not done so, the department was not respon-
sible. Case dismissed with costs. Moral no one
should ever pass through a Vic. railway gate with-
out first assuring himself that it has been opened
by the duly-qualified grandmother, and if the
official grandmother is not there he should camp
on the wrong side of the gate till the old lady
turns up and opens the said gate within the mean-
ing of the act.
An Australian religious “ organ ” recently
advertised for a manager, “ able to keep books,
read proofs, ” &c., &c. From the deluge of applica-
tions received the most likely were sorted out, and
the writers requested to call on the “board of
management ” —consisting of half-a-dozen parsons.
Each quivering seeker for the good billet was
solemnly and closely interrogated with regard to
competency. He was then told that the salary
would be £1 per week ! If this information didn t
break-up the would-be manager, the boss parson
imperatively demanded “How do you intend to
live on this pound ? ” The suddenness of such a
question was too much for the majority of those
interviewed, and, murmuring something about
“ going to a quiet place to think it over, they
reached for their hats and staggered from the
awful presence. One quiet-looking young fellow,
however, whose clear, unclouded eye showed con-
sciousness of moral rectitude, met the question
unabashed. He pressed his hands together, cast
his orbs Heavenward, and said he d leave that
matter with the Lord.” The b.p. admitted he was
beaten, and the young man was duly installed.
And day by day he keeps those books,
And reads those holy proofs ;
His work is pure, he never looks
At marks of Satan’s hoofs. _
He hums a hymn in leisure time:
No worry clouds his brow ;
His life is sweet as a church-bell chime,
For he lives—well, God knows how !


Til.ore is s'ill living in Sydney, at a great
Age, a Q itkcr who, as a young man, was present
at tV? World's Anti-slavery Convention held in
London in June, 1840. Thomas Clarkson pre-
sided, and it was the scene which ensued, as a
tottering old man rose to open the proceedings,
that was taken by Haydon, the historical painter,
as a subject for his picture of the event. Hay-
don’s written account of the opening is a most
remarkable piece of word-painting. To f > con-
clusion of Clarkson’s speech, the vast meeting
responded with a tremendous “Amen.” Says
Haydon :
The death warrant of slavery all over the earth was
boded by that “ Amen.” I have seen the most affecting
tragedies, imitative and real, but never did I witness in
life or in the drama, so deep, so touching, so pathetic an
effect produced on any great assembly, as by the few un-
affected, unsophisticated words of this aged and agitated
man.
The old Quaker spoke that day to Sir George
Stephen (cousin to N.S.W. Chief Justice Alfred
Stephen) who had taken a leading part in the
Anti-Slavery Propaganda, and he remembers dis-
tinctly Stephen, in response to a remark that it
was appalling to think that English gentlemen
could be guilty of the atrocities attributed to them
in their treatment of slaves in the West Indies,
saying, “ The English gentleman only believes in
liberty and justice for his own class.” Stephen, a
few years afterwards, came to Australia, and,
after a career at the bar, occupied a quasi-judicial
position in Melbourne. It was his son, Wilber-
force Stephen, who gave the Victorians their
Education Act. Sir George, as a young barrister
in London, wrote a book on horse-dealing, and in
his later years in Melbourne wrote a “ Life of
Christ.”

“The Sport of Kings"—and
Other Thieves .
The usual healthy crop of race-accidents at
Country shows have been chronicled lately, but merely in
a perfunctory, insignificant fashion. At one undertakers’
gathering a couple fell in one race, two more in another
and four in a third. The fact that eight jocks, weren’t
killed instead of only two horses was a lucky circum-
stance. “ It’s not the dangerous courses, as alleged,” said
a steward lately ; “ it’s the foul riding that brings ’em
down.” Precisely. The stock - furrowed circus - tracks
have nothing to do with it. The Government ought to
seize the majority of these country run-rounds, and
gazette them as graveyards.
* * * *
One of the most interested spectators of a recent
neck-and-neck tussle was a leading rider who would have
been in an uncomfortably tight place financially had his
well-backed choice lost. Yet he has had sacksful of
money in his time. The old tale, and the old moral.
Jockeys who make and keep money form a very small
proportion of the professional crowd. The assets of the
average reinsman generally comprise a strong pair of
hands, a capacity for moistening the system, ahd a huge
stock of native impudence.
* * * *
One jockey, with stronger arms than will-power,
was lately heard cursing the day he was born. “ Passed
out to-day,” he said, “and it’s my birthday. And, blime,
I got a ‘ sixer ’ this very day last year.”
* * * *
The election of the first lady member of a racing-
club in M.L. opens a new source of club revenue. The
New Turfite of the future won’t badger her male friends
to take her to the races. She’ll join the club and go “ on
her own.” And Ikey Mo may yet tremble in his boots if the
fair sex—but The Bulletin forbears, for the present at
all events.
* * * »
A certain trainer drew another trainer’s rather
smart prad in a sweep and was asked for a big cut out of
the probable proceeds or else the horse wouldn’t weigh
out. The holder, not over friendly, and not very parti-
cular, told him to scratch and be hanged. He did. Just
what the ticket man wanted. He won the stake with a
well-backed ’un of his own, and scooped a large slice of
the sweep from the man who had drawn his horse. It’s
now nip and tuck between those two stables, sweep or no
sweep.

At Gawler Jockey Club meeting, the other day,
“ a man who believes in the office ” discovered on the way
up that the meeting was being held on the sixth day of
the week, the sixth month of the year, in the year 1896.
He put £1 on No. 6 in each race (in a 10s. tote), and won
£27 2s. on the day. It will be seen that the divs. are
above bookmakers’ prices.

* * * *
The new game of cricket-billiards, concocted by
R. 0. Norman, of Melbourne, may come into fashion at
small tea-parties. An oval-shaped board, suggesting an
American billiard-table with the corners rounded off, and
a netting gutter substituted for cushions, serves as the
cricket-field. It is a single-wicket game, the wicket being
a board (that turns on' a pivot when struck) situated at
the “spot” end of the table. The bowler delivers a
small-sized billiard-ball from “baulk’’-line with a cue,
whilst the batsman, standing behind the wicket, defends
it with a mace. The batsman is required to keep within
popping-crease limits, and if he pushes the head of his
mace beyond the crease and misses the ball he has to con-
sider himself stumped. Also, if he knocks a fieldsman
down with his mace he goes sorrowfully back to the
apocryphal pavilion. But these ways of getting out are
more feminine than masculine. Ladies play the game. In
a general way the batsman is either clean-bowled or caught;
occasionally the ball glances off his mace into the wicket.
A catch means the overturning of a fieldsman by a ball
which the batsman has struck. Eleven devil’s-pool pins
impersonate the field, and the captain of the “ out” side
places them and shifts them according to his judgment of
the batsman’s play. The placing of the field is subject to
distance conditions, which give the batsman a reasonable
chance to slip or drive the ball between the pins. As a
-nile, the trickiest striker, up to now, has been “ caught
out " withtirtwenty strokes. A long innings is a rarity,
so the game may be said to move fast. Cricket-billiards,
regarded as a harmless form of amusement, suitably for
the domestic dining-table, is worth further consideration.
* * * *
Young Flack, the Melb. harrier who lately won
the Olympic mile-footrace at Athens, has, it appears, had
a right “ royal ” time in the capital city of degenerate
Greece. The King sent his carriage to convey the vic-
torious Australian back to the city, and the latter was
publicly honored by the Athenians with a gorgeousness
reminiscent of their Pagan-predecessors’ god-worship.
He appeared at theatres, churches, &c., with King George
and his family, and on his departure from Athens was
cheered by countless thousands end greeted at every
stopping-place by crowds of gaping Greeks.
» * * *
The establishment of a racing co. to run a big
stableful of first-ekss horses and make unlimited money
is advocated by someone at ’Ome, who points to the big
stakes landed by leading stables as evidence that there is
cash in the venture. The details read well on paper, but
the scheme is based on a winning assumption. Though
laughed at in many quarters, a Melbourne paper takes up
the theme in all seriousness, and advocates racing or
breeding co’s in Australia. The latter would be right
enough but for the trivial fact that breeding doesn’t pay,
anyway. What are studs returning annually now ? And
why was the famous Sylvia Park in M.L. broken up?
Racing co’s might pay divs., but calls would have to be
made much oftener. Motto : “ Straight racing.” But
what about the baleful influence of Ikey Mo, and the jocks
he rules ? And what a nice billet the manager would
have answering letter queries by the hundred every week
as to when the co’s horses would be on the job, or what
their chances were in the Woolloomooloo Handicap or
Rooty Tooty Oup!
Maoriland is thinking of kicking hard
against the local Press Association. This Associa-
tion, which includes practically all the existing
papers, supplies cable intelligence to members
only. No new paper is admitted without a
payment of <£soo cash; failing that it must
do without cable news, and is cheerfully told
to go and run a service of its own. As the average
new paper can’t part <£soo, and as very few papers
can live without foreign cables, there are practi-
cally no journals starting in Maoriland nowadays.
Every now and then an old one dies or amalga-
mates, and as its place isn’t filled the Maoriland
press steadily diminishes.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A friend called on Parkes in ’93, just after
another chip had sprung from the old block.
“Mr. ,” squeaked the gay parent of 78, “you
see hus hin that ’appy frame of mind which natur-
ally follows hupon the solution hof a difficult pro-
blem.” “Indeed, Sir Henry.” “Yes, we have
decided to name my youngest son Cobden.”
“ Cobden, Sir Henry ! But consider: when he
goes to school the boys will call him Cobby !
Cobby Parkes isn’t nice. Why not name him
after some American patriot—Washington, Lin-
coln, Garfield?” “Mr. ——, we ’ave considered
the matter from he very point of view, and we ’ave
decided to keep the three names you suggest for
the next three boys.”
* * * »
At a dinner given by Major Rennie, of the
Mutual Insurance Co. of New York, at the Aus-
tralia Hotel, Parkes was placed opposite editor
Curnow, of S.M. Herald. Oysters were served.
“ Very good oysters, Sir Henry,” said Curnow ;
“fine food; very strengthening, very stimulating.”
“ Mis-ter Curnow,” said Parkes, “ Hi ham a hold
man hof fourscore, and Hi don’t need these hadven-
titious haids.” And he added such a gloss to this
text that the editor blushed into his plate for the
rest of the dinner.

One Saturday Parkes was going up to Faulcon-
bridge, and W. B. Piddington was a passenger by
the same train on his way to Mt. Piddington.
“Ha, Piddington, are you hoff up to the moun-
tains ? ” “ Yes, Sir Henry. I'm going to my little

you know; must fulfil the residence condi-

’ave hin that basket, Piddington?” “In this

tainly, Sir Henry ! ” and Piddington opened the

Piddington?” “’Tis a bottle of hock, Sir Henry.
“’Ock ! Dear me ! Now I wonder have you a
corkscrew, Piddington?” “Yes; would you like
a glass, Sir Henry?” “Piddington, since you
press me, Hi would.” And while Piddington was
opening the hock, Parkes devoured the sandwiches.
“Ha!” holding the glass up, “ this looks good’ock !
Drinking it and taking the bottle, “this is good
’ock, Piddington!” And Parkes the
hock —the sandwiches were already gone. “But
Hi perceive you ’ave another bottle, Piddington.
May I hask what is hin that bottle, Pidding-
ton? ” “Claret, Sir Henry.” “Well, Pidding-
ton, hif there his one wine Hi prefer to
hanother, that wine is claret.” And the claret
followed the hock. “ But what have you in that
parcel, Piddington?” “Oh, a little bit of cake,
Sir Henry.” “ Cake ? Dear me ! ” And the cake
followed the sandwiches. “ Piddington,” said the
old man, getting out of the train at Faulconbridge,
“Hi thank you for your ’ospitality. Hi could
wish that we travelled together hoftener, Pidding-
ton. Oood-bye, Piddington! ”

In the Day of Judgment?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He knew he was dying. He read it in his
daughter’b tear-blurred face, in the silent figure of
the doctor, standing motionless by the window,
waiting. He felt its numbness creeping upwards
from his feit. Oh, the peace df death’s stillness
after years df life’s agdiiy ! Oh, td sleep* and
and sleep, artd sleep, with nd remembrance, with
hd hauntirtg remorse for a deadly crime, with nd
Sickening terror of a day when it should be found
out. Was there a moment when he had not
thought of that day, of the cold grip of the hand-
cuffs, of his daughter’s moan over her blasted life ?
And—oh, Cod! —had he not felt the rope round
his throat a thousand times, and wakened with a
shriek of terror with the drops of agony upon his
forehead ? Death—oblivion— was all he had prayed
for, and now death was come. Death, yes; but
afterwards ?
» * » *
A sweet, tender face was before him, a sad,
patient face beneath a widow’s cap. He saw her
with her hands on the piano, with the firelight
fl ashing on her hair, on the polished wood, and in
the eyes of the small, round-faced boy at her side.
It must have been Sunday evening, for she sang
a hymn, the piping, childish treble mingling with
her low voice. It was long, long ago, but he re-
me inhered it all. ‘ ‘ Repent / confess ! thou shalt ”
an 1 suddenly, at the remembrance of some recent
boyish crime, the shrill voice had broken into a
Sob. “ What is it, little sott? What’s wrong with
mother’s boy ? ”
If she would beat him, he had reasoned to him-
self, he would not mind, but she would not do
that. She would be very sorry, perhaps she
would cry, and so he would never tell her. The
influences of early training and the traditions of
childhood gained their authority over the man
o.ice more. As sharply now, as in those days of
unreasoning faith, the anguish of an unrelieved
conscience ptrmeated him.
* * * *
How hot it was, how breathless and over-
powering ! —only a sheet of brown paper to-night
between us and ; he chuckled feebly at the
familiar phrase, then fell to shuddering. Only a
sheet of paper—good God ! there was nothing,
nothing between him and that awful yawning
chasm. The flames were flinging up their long
fiery arms to drag him down. He was lost, lost;
Oh, Cod, have mercy; he Would confess before it
Was too late. His dry lips moved, and his face
twitched with agony.
“ Something is troubling him,” whispered the
doctor ; “ try to soothe him ; ” and his daughter
bent over him. •
‘ ‘ What is it, dear father ? Do you want to
speak to me?”
“ If we confess our sin— if, if— no salvation else,
nothing but outer darkness and gnashing of
teeth” But no words would come, for all his
struggles. The doctor raised the sick man’s head
and put a cordial to his lips.
“ Dear, dear father,’ sobbed the girl, “ speak to
me. What is it, father ? ”
His brain was suddenly cleared. Yes, confession
would drive away the demon horror that possessed
him, and give him peace ; but what would it do
for his pure young daughter ? In the moment of
clear vision he saw her crushed under the burden
of another’s guilt, shunned by all, heart-broken.
His lips ceased to struggle for speech, He would
lose his soul, but he would not hurt hers. The
darkness was closing round him. He was losing
the light for ever. The surging of many waters
came over his head, and the light went out.
* * * *
And. who shall say that there was not joy
among the angels over the sinner who left his sin
Unconfessed ?

Dear Bulletin, —It has been pointed out
to me that, in a yarn of mine printed in a
recent Bulletin, I make a drunken police-
magistrate “ raise his head on one elbow ” ! . . .
“ Well ? ” “ Why, he couldn’t do it! ” “ Couldn’t
he ? That’s all you know. It was in the old days,
in the bad old North, when a police-magistrate,
especially a drunken police-magistrate, could do
anything ! Why, I’ve seen a drunken magistrate
in the old days picturesquely raise h : s tivo heads
on one elbow.”—Yours, The Dipsomaniac.
London Evening News, a paper run by
the Harmsworths—who also own Answers— is
about the most shameless stealer of Bulletin
matter on the face of this earth. It never ack-
nowledges anything, and it grabs on a large, com-
prehensive scale everything that suits its purpose.
In its issue Of April 25, for example, it reprinted
under a new title Henry Fletcher’s story “Hjs
Lucky Day,” which was printed in The Bulletin
of 7th March, and paid for by this journal, and it
rldt only left out the writer’s riame biit put the
steal under the heading “ Our Short Story.” Out
short story, be it observed : Harrrisworth’s short
story, in fact—not Harmsworth’s Comprehensive
Steal. Likewise, these “ enterprising ” half-
penny pirates stole S. A. Mills’s graphic Bulletin
story, “160 in the Shade,” and republished it
under another title as original.
Under a column headed “ Original (?)
Correspondence,” a London newspaper called
Colonial Enterprise, of March 26, has an in-
stalment of a story of Louis Becke’s, published in
Sydney Sunday Times, and entitled “The Great
Crushing at Mount Sugar Bag.” .“‘Colonial’
Enterprise ” is good. No acknowledgment of
either author or paper is given by. this friendly
thief.

It was doomsday for Marvel ! The lightning danced
and gleamed in the heavens like the quadrillion brandish-
ing halberdiers of a mailed ethereal army in the im-
memorial war between inanimate Nature and man, with
death amidst all its beauty ; now with the seinti lating
glare of colossal diamonds, now changing into Titanic
spears and broad-arrows of blue and obvoluting oriflammes
and long tridents of serpentine-green, and now flaring
with the shimmering sheen of scutiform rotary-polished
silver and the incandescent glow of laminiferous sheets
and braziers of diaphanous burnished majolica in the
treasure-house of Olympus. The heavens opened, ani
the four winds were let loose all together. Then came
terrene, seismic disturbances, and a sulphurous smell as a
zig-zagging biuelight blazed in the zenith, quickly flicker-
ing down the matchlock iron stanchion of the infundibuli-
form gargoyle above the balcony ; the window rattled,
and Marvel, standing in such imminent proximity to an
infuriated universe, was terrified !—first staggering, then
knocked down by the lightning, she lay, with a charred
arm, prone, torpescent, and unconscious on the floor.

Among the recently reduced-m-screw

One high official of “ John’s ” department was
as partial to the messenger as he was to an after-
noon nap. It was “ John’s” special duty to arouse
that official about 3.30, so that he might, by the
exercise of great diligence, get his coat on, his
hair brushed, and his hands washed by the time
the clock struck four. On one occasion “John,”
absent on a message for the Minister, could not
return to the office till after 4, and as it never en-
tered his head that a public servant of such stand-
ing as Mr. Jobkins should so violate official
tradition as to stay after 4, he accordingly did not
look into his superior’s room. Mr. Jobkins was
thus left in the sleep of the easy-worked and heavy-
lunching till the chill of dusk aroused him. Making
his way to the messenger’s quarters, he reproached
him for his remissness. “Sir,” said “John,” “I
did not think, sir, that one of your name and posi-
tion, sir, could object, sir, to spend an hour longer
than usual in the service of your country.” Where-
upon the high official retired rebuked.

He was a Northern locomotive-inspector,
and his name was a word of terror. At a certain
station on the Central Line he was examining an
engine in his customary microscopical style. At
last he discovered a small spot of rust on the
crank-shaft and proceeded to rate the hapless
engine-cleaner therefore. Finally the latter fired
up: “Well, you see—l only cleaned the blamed
thing with the naked eye—not with a forty-foot
telescope.”

and she said in such a funny voice—for she comes from
Ireland, you know', and Mrs. O’Shea too—“ Yes indeed,
Mrs. O’Shea, when my man was took had two years ago
come Candlemas,.ai d wasn’t able to keep anything on the
stomach of him but hot fermentations, the doctor elevated
his pains (for sure ’tw'as windy colic he had, had cess to it)
w’ith subterraneous interjections of cocoa;” and Mrs.
O’Shea said, “God be good to us, sure my husband’s wife-
died of that same, six years ago come Palm Sunday,
which is three months after he married me ; but sure the
doctor called it poisoning, for I saw him put it in h r
death sen tance, and he wrote it ‘ All collie poisoning
through gin,’ so he did,” and that’s all I can think of now,
so good-bye from Yours truly, Lotta Green-Pease (a little
over 7 years old).
